601 of 892 DOCUMENTS


J.B. Steven v. Bd. of Comm'Rs


No. 1724 C.D. 1993, No. 1725 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


164 Pa. Commw. 315; 643 A.2d 142; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 249


March 3, 1994, ARGUED  
May 25, 1994, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Application for Reargument Denied July 20, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 419.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. G.D. 93-3478. State Agency, Common Pleas Court of Allegheny County. Judge McLEAN.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant advertising company sought review, in consolidated appeals, of decisions of the Common Pleas Court of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which sustained the preliminary objections of appellees, municipalities, and dismissed appellant's complaints in mandamus and actions for declaratory relief from appellees' ordinances limiting appellant's outdoor advertising.


OVERVIEW: Appellant advertising company sought permission from appellees, municipalities, to put up billboards. Appellant filed substantive validity challenges to appellees' ordinances and requests for curative amendments. After hearings, the validity challenges were granted and curative amendments were adopted by both appellees. Appellees denied permits, and appellant brought actions at law and for equitable and declaratory relief. The trial court sustained appellees' preliminary objections and dismissed the equity actions, and appellant sought review. The court affirmed the trial court's decisions. The court stated that after a successful validity challenge, a governing body could not totally prohibit the successful challenger's proposed development nor subject it to unreasonable and burdensome restrictions, but it could subject the plans to reasonable restrictions as might be otherwise provided for in its ordinance. As a result, the court stated that appellant's right to site specific relief was not clear without a determination as to what restrictions applied, making mandamus inappropriate. The court also found that the applicable statute made declaratory relief unavailable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order sustaining preliminary objections of appellees, municipalities, to appellant advertising company's actions seeking mandamus and declaratory relief against appellees' denial of appellant's request to install billboards, because the restrictions on appellant's plans available to appellees were an issue for the trial court and declaratory relief was unavailable by the terms of the applicable statute.

 

602 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Golden Rule Ins. Co. v. Ins. Dep't


No. 800 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


163 Pa. Commw. 509; 641 A.2d 1255; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 193


October 7, 1993, Argued  
April 20, 1994, FILED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner insurance company challenged a final order of respondent insurance commissioner, which disapproved under the Insurance Company Law of 1921, 40 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 751, rate modifications on policy forms for guaranteed renewable policies.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner insurance company sought review of respondent insurance commissioner's final order disapproving rate modifications for four policy forms for guaranteed renewable policies. Respondent issued a written disapproval 85 days after petitioner's submission and petitioner requested a hearing. Respondent issued its final order based on its findings that Pennsylvania's lower than national average rates should have been a factor in setting petitioner's rates. The court held that renewal of an insurance policy at different premiums created new contracts; therefore, respondent had authority to approve or disapprove renewal premiums under the Insurance Company Law of 1921, 40 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 751. The court also held that under 40 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 477b, petitioner's rates were approved as a matter of law after 60 days had passed without a determination from respondent. However, the rates became void upon disapproval thereof. The court reversed with respect to respondent's holding that petitioner's rate filings were not approved by operation of law and otherwise affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed as to respondent insurance commissioner's finding that petitioner insurance company rates were not approved by operation of law, but affirmed in all other respects. Once respondent had made its final determination, petitioner's disapproved rates became void.

 

603 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Braksator v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


No. 1466 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


163 Pa. Commw. 332; 641 A.2d 44; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 174


January 31, 1994, ARGUED  
April 12, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 92-3718-17-5. Common Pleas Court of Bucks County. Judge CLARK


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sign owners sought review the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the order of appellee zoning hearing board that dismissed, as not ripe for review, appellants' challenge to the constitutional validity of the Sign Ordinance of Northhampton Township, Pa., § 140-90 (1981), as amended by appellee township.


OVERVIEW: Appellee township amended the Sign Ordinance of Northhampton Township, Pa., § 140-90 (1981), regulating the size, placement, and design of signs within its limits. Appellant sign owners challenged the validity of § 140-90 as an unconstitutional taking. Appellee zoning hearing board found that pre-existing, nonconforming signs would continue to exist until the amendment's effective date. Appellee board denied appellants' application as not ripe for review because there was no present restriction on the use of appellants' signs. The trial court affirmed. Appellants sought review. The court reversed and remanded for consideration of the substantive issues that were raised by appellants and adequately developed for review. The court held that § 140-90, as of its effective date, had been applied to appellants as owners of nonconforming structures. The court found that appellants would suffer hardship if judicial consideration was delayed because they were subject to civil penalties for failure to comply and the expenses of applying for a special exception or modifying, replacing, or removing the signs.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of appellee zoning hearing board and remanded for consideration of the substantive issues adequately developed for judicial review because appellee township's ordinance had been applied to appellant sign owners who would have suffered hardship if judicial consideration was delayed.

 

604 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Mindek v. Lilley


No. 1036 C.D. 1992


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


163 Pa. Commw. 214; 640 A.2d 508; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 157


September 3, 1993, Submitted on Briefs  
April 5, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 91-3018. Common Pleas Court of Washington County. Judge TERPUTAC.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a pro se litigant, challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Washington County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed appellant's complaint against appellees, three county district judges, with prejudice. Appellant argued that because appellees had not filed notices of acceptance under § 1 of the Act of April 13, 1859, P.L. 592, they were occupying their offices illegally.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, a pro se litigant, filed a complaint against appellees, three county district judges. The complaint alleged that appellees held office illegally because under § 1 of the Act of April 13, 1859, P.L. 592 (Act 577), they were required to file notices of acceptance, which they had not done. Appellant simultaneously filed a petition for leave to proceed in forma pauperis (IFP). The lower court dismissed the complaint as frivolous because Act 577 had been repealed by the Act of April 22, 1909, P.L. 104 (Act 63). Appellant argued that Act 63 did not repeal Act 577 and that failure to rule on the IFP petition within 20 days violated Pa. R. Civ. P. 240. The court first examined Pa. R. Civ. P. 240(j), which allowed a court to dismiss an action before acting on the IFP petition if the action was frivolous. Appellant's complaint was grounded in repealed law. The complaint was frivolous. Therefore, dismissal of the complaint and the IFP petition was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court dismissing the complaint. The requirement that appellees, three county district judges, file notices of acceptance was contained in a statute that was repealed in 1909. Because appellant pro se litigant's complaint was frivolous, dismissal of the in forma pauperis petition was also proper.

 

605 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Creighton


No. 551 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


163 Pa. Commw. 68; 639 A.2d 1296; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 139


November 18, 1993, Argued  
March 30, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. S.A. 3023 of 1992. Common Pleas Court of Allegheny County. Judge SCHEIB.



DISPOSITION: Vacated and Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant cat owner sought review of an order of the Common Pleas Court of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which issued a summary conviction for violation of an ordinance of the Borough of Carnegie, which prohibited possession of more than five cats or dogs within the borough.


OVERVIEW: Appellant cat owner kept 25 cats in her home. The trial court issued a summary conviction for violation of Borough of Carnegie Ordinance No. 1089, which limited the number of cats or dogs which a person could keep to five. On appeal, the court vacated the conviction. The court held that the borough had the authority to pass ordinances relating to ownership of pets under 53 P.S. § 46202(13) (repealed), and to nuisances in general under 53 P.S. § 46202(5), (6), and (74) (repealed). The court held that appellant could not challenge the ordinance's enactment, as the time for doing so under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5571(c)(5) had expired. The court held that the borough had not established that the ordinance was a reasonable means of achieving a legitimate governmental goal, as it did not indicate why more than five dogs or cats would constitute a nuisance or risk to public health and safety. The court noted that the trial court made no finding that appellant's cats were a nuisance in fact. The court remanded for the trial court to determine what goals the borough sought to achieve, whether they were legitimate governmental goals, and whether the means used were reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court's summary conviction of appellant cat owner for violation of the borough ordinance prohibiting possession of more than five cats or dogs, and remanded for the trial court to address the legitimacy of the borough's governmental goals and the reasonableness of the means used to achieve them, because the trial court had failed to do so and there was no finding as to whether appellant's cats were a nuisance in fact.

 

606 of 892 DOCUMENTS


T.L.C. Servs. v. Kamin


No. 1628 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


162 Pa. Commw. 547; 639 A.2d 926; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 120


December 15, 1993, Argued  
March 16, 1994, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied October 12, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Pa. LEXIS 523.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 28-89 (Misc.). Common Pleas Court of Clinton County. Judge BROWN, President Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners challenged the order of the Common Pleas Court of Clinton County (Pennsylvania), that affirmed the report of a Board of View laying out a private road over appellants' property, as sought by appellee neighbor under the Private Road Act, 36 P. S. § 2731 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellee neighbor filed a petition pursuant to the Private Road Act (Act), 36 P. S. § 2731 et seq., requesting that a Board of View lay out a private road over appellant landowners' property to provide appellee's property with public road access. The board determined that a private road was necessary and granted appellee's request. Appellants sought review of the order of the trial court that approved the Board of View's determination. Appellants claimed that the statute allowed a taking of private property without adequate compensation and sufficient due process, and did not require appellee to file a bond, in violation of U.S. Const. amend XIV, Pa. Const. arts. I, § 10, art. X, § 4. The court affirmed the judgment because the Act was not subject to the provisions of the Eminent Domain Code, 26 P. S. §§ 1-101 et seq., and had consistently been held to be constitutional.


OUTCOME: The order which validated the determination of a Board of View, that established a private road across appellant landowners' property to appellee neighbor's landlocked property, was affirmed because the Eminent Domain Code did not apply, and the Private Road Act was constitutional.

 

607 of 892 DOCUMENTS


George Clay Steam Fire Engine & Hose Co. v. Pennsylvania Human Relations Comm'n


No. 2741 C.D. 1992


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


162 Pa. Commw. 468; 639 A.2d 893; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 103


October 7, 1993, Submitted on Briefs  
March 10, 1994, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied February 23, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Pa. LEXIS 2243.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. E-50698-D, E-50699-D, E-50700-D and E-50701-D. State Agency, Pennsylvania Human Relations Commission.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner company sought review of an order from the Pennsylvania Human Relations Commission that found petitioner in violation of 43 P.S. §§ 951 - 962.2 (1955). Petitioner contended that the commission erred in finding that the complainants established a prima facie case of sex discrimination; that the hearing examiner improperly admitted and relied upon hearsay, opinion, and irrelevant evidence; and that petitioner was denied due process.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner company that was found to have discriminated on the basis of sex contended on appeal that the commission's enabling legislation unconstitutionally mandated commingling of prosecutorial and adjudicatory functions. The court held that petitioner failed to meet its burden of production. The court reasoned that petitioner needed to show that the votes cast against the complainants' applications were cast for legitimate, non-discriminatory reasons. The court held that the commission did not commit an error in concluding that petitioner discriminated even though the complainants never called witnesses to testify about the motivation for their votes. The court held that there was no commingling of functions within the commission and that petitioner received adequate due process. The court also reasoned that because petitioner did not supply any contrary evidence to suggest that the commission did not review the entire record, it was presumed that the commission acted properly. The order was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The order that found that petitioner company used sex-based discrimination was affirmed. The court held that the commission did not err in concluding that the complainants established a prima facie case of discrimination, and in concluding that petitioner did not meet its burden of production. The court also held that the commission did not commingle its prosecutory and adjudicatory functions.

Collins v. Upper Salford Township Bd. of Supervisors


No. 1193 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


162 Pa. Commw. 403; 639 A.2d 861; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 106


February 3, 1994, ARGUED  
March 9, 1994, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted February 24, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Pa. LEXIS 2232.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. 90-08660. Common Pleas Court of Montgomery County. Judge CORSO.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant developer sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a decision that denied appellant's curative amendment challenge to a zoning ordinance. Appellant asserted that the trial court erred in determining that mobile home parks were permitted in the light industrial and limited light industrial districts.


OVERVIEW: Appellant developer who challenged the validity of a zoning ordinance on the basis that it was unconstitutional sought review of an order that affirmed a decision that denied appellant's curative amendment that challenged the zoning ordinance. The issue presented on appeal was whether the board erred in determining that the zoning ordinance did not unconstitutionally exclude or fail to provide for a fair share of mobile home park development in the township. The court concluded that the township's failure to provide for mobile home park development effectively prohibited the use. The court reasoned that because the township provided no justification for completely prohibiting the use, the zoning ordinance unconstitutionally excluded mobile home parks. The court also held that the trial court was best situated to determine the extent to which appellant's proposal should have been approved. The order was reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The order which affirmed a decision that denied appellant's curative amendment challenge to a zoning ordinance was reverse and remand. The court held that the zoning ordinance unconstitutionally excluded mobile home parks. The court also held that the trial court was best situated to determine the extent to which appellant's proposal should have been approved.

 

609 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Merchant v. State Bd. of Medicine


No. 329 M.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


162 Pa. Commw. 332; 638 A.2d 484; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 96


December 17, 1993, Argued  
_March 2, 1994, FILED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Pennsylvania State Board of Medicine filed preliminary objections to the court's jurisdiction for petitioner doctor's failure to exhaust administrative remedies before filing his petition for review in the nature of a complaint in equity, resulting from the initiation of disciplinary proceedings against petitioner under § 40(a) of the Medical Practice Act of 1985, 63 P.S. § 422.40(a) for allegedly unprofessional conduct.


OVERVIEW: After respondent Pennsylvania State Board of Medicine (board) temporarily suspended petitioner doctor's license for professional misconduct under § 40(a) of the Medical Practice Act of 1985 (Act), 63 P.S. § 422.40(a), petitioner filed a petition for review in the nature of a complaint in equity, and the board filed preliminary objections to the court's jurisdiction. The court sustained the board's objections, holding that petitioner failed to exhaust his administrative remedies, which resulted in a lack of jurisdiction. The board's decision to immediately suspend petitioner's license, based on a determination of the sufficiency of the evidence, was prosecutorial in nature, rather than adjudicatory. Under § 41 of the Act, 63 P.S. § 422.41, those board members who participated in the decision to prosecute petitioner were precluded from adjudicating the charges against him. Therefore, the remaining untainted board member's decision was considered the final adjudication. Further, because petitioner had an adequate administrative remedy, he had the opportunity to challenge the authority of the prosecuting attorney to press additional charges before the board.


OUTCOME: The court sustained respondent Pennsylvania State Board of Medicine's (board) preliminary objection to the court's jurisdiction, holding that petitioner doctor failed to exhaust his administrative remedies, resulting in a lack of jurisdiction to consider the issues in the petition.

 

610 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Dep't of Transp. v. Wylie


No. 305 C.D. 1992


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


162 Pa. Commw. 219; 638 A.2d 433; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 84


January 12, 1994, Submitted on Briefs  
February 25, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. SA-91-2877. Common Pleas Court of Allegheny County. Judge SCHEIB.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant driver sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed her appeal of a 90-day suspension of her driver's license by respondent Department of Transportation pursuant to § 13(m) of the Controlled Substance, Drug, Device and Cosmetic Act, 35 P.S. § 780-113(m).


OVERVIEW: Appellant driver was charged, pled guilty to, and was sentenced on January 29, 1991 for fraudulently obtaining prescription narcotics. On August 13, 1991, the Clerk of Courts certified appellant's conviction to respondent Department of Transportation. On September 10, 1991, respondent sent appellant a notice of the suspension of her operating privileges. Appellant sought review of the suspension in the trial court. The trial court affirmed the suspension of appellant's license and the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that 35 P.S. § 780-113(m) met substantive due process constitutional requirements because the operation of a motor vehicle was not a property right but a privilege and determined that § 780-113(m) was rationally related to promote the legitimate state interest of deterring illegal drug use. The court also held that § 780-113(m) met equal protection safeguards because it imposed mandatory and uniform penalties on licensees convicted of certain drug offenses. The court found that appellant was not entitled to vacation of the suspension due to unreasonable delay because the delay was caused by the judicial system and not by respondent.


OUTCOME: The judgment affirming appellant's driver's suspension of her motor vehicle operator's license for committing a drug offense was affirmed, because the statute was rationally related to the state's legitimate objective of deterring illegal drug use, because it imposed mandatory and uniform penalties , and because any delay in effecting the suspension was not attributed to the state transportation office.

 

611 of 892 DOCUMENTS


In re City of Scranton


No. 2338 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


162 Pa. Commw. 109; 638 A.2d 379; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 64


November 19, 1993 ARGUED  
February 11, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 93-Civil-3242. Common Pleas Court of Lackawanna County. Judges MUNLEY, COTTONE and O'MALLEY



DISPOSITION: The decision of the common pleas court is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lackawanna County (Pennsylvania), which denied its request pursuant to § 123(c) of the Municipalities Financial Recovery Act, 53 P.S. § 11701.123(c), for approval of a one-year increase in the nonresident earned income tax from 1.0 percent to 1.6 percent.


OVERVIEW: The Department of Community Affairs declared appellant city to be a distressed municipality under the Municipalities Financial Recovery Act (Act), 53 P.S. §§ 11701.101-11701.501. Thereafter, appellant city filed a petition under § 123(c) of the Act seeking approval to raise its nonresident earned income tax from 1.0 percent to 1.6 percent. Appellant sought review of the trial court's order that denied its request, and the court reversed. The court held that the trial court disregarded the correct scope of review and overstepped its bounds when it determined that although a deficit existed, taxes should not be increased on nonresidents where there was an avenue for increasing taxes on residents. The court ruled that the imposition was not unconstitutional, as it could not agree that nonresidents would obtain no benefit from their increased taxes. Therefore, the court entered an order granting appellant's request for approval.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of appellant city's request because the trial court disregarded the correct scope of review and overstepped when it determined that, although a deficit existed, taxes should not be increased on nonresidents.

 

612 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Jubelirer v. Singel


No. 510 M.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


162 Pa. Commw. 55; 638 A.2d 352; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 68


December 15, 1993, Submitted on Briefs  
February 9, 1994, FILED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Republican senators challenged the actions of respondents, Democratic senators, the lieutenant governor, and the state Senate as a body, that resulted in the seating of a senator.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner Republican senators contended that a newly elected senator's seating was invalid as it violated Pa. Const. art. III, § 13, and that the procedure which was followed violated Pa. Const. art. I, § 26, and Pa. Const. art. II. Respondents, Democratic senators, the lieutenant governor, and the state Senate as a body, objected to the petition. The challenged senator was elected at a special election, and sworn into office in the Senate chamber in the presence of respondent lieutenant governor and the president pro tempore of the Senate, while respondent Senate was not in session. Respondent Senate, including the challenged senator, voted to permit the challenged senator to participate and to be seated. The court held that the petition failed to state a claim upon which relief could be granted, because members of the general assembly were immune from judicial review for legislative acts under the Speech and Debate Clause, Pa. Const. art. II, § 15. The court held that the new senator had not violated Pa. Const. art. III, § 13, when he voted on his own seating. The court held that the rights of another newly elected senator were not violated because he was not permitted to vote.


OUTCOME: The court held that petitioner Republican senators failed to state a claim upon which relief could be granted., because respondents, Democratic senators, the lieutenant governor, and the Senate as a body, were immune from suit under the Speech and Debate Clause of the Pennsylvania Constitution, as their seating of a senator was a legislative act.

Lee Hosp. v. Cambria County Bd. of Assessment Appeals


NO. 2513 C.D. 1992


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


162 Pa. Commw. 38; 638 A.2d 344; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 60


June 15, 1993, ARGUED  
February 8, 1994, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal and/or Cross-Petition Discontinued September 13, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 1992-2846, Term 1992. Common Pleas Court of Cambria County. Judge LEAHEY.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, we affirm the order of the trial court which dismissed Lee's complaint for injunctive and declaratory relief and granted appellees' preliminary objections.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hospital sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Cambria County (Pennsylvania), which granted the preliminary objections of appellee county commissioners and several intervenors and dismissed appellant's complaint for injunctive and declaratory relief from appellees' decision to impose property tax on appellant.


OVERVIEW: Appellee county commissioners notified appellant hospital and several other hospitals that they would be subject to property taxation. Appellee offered to accept a payment in lieu of taxation, but appellant refused and challenged taxation. A hearing was set to review the decision to tax appellant, but prior to the hearing appellant filed a complaint for injunctive and declaratory relief. Appellee and several intervenors filed preliminary objections. The trial court granted the objections and dismissed appellant's complaint. Appellant sought review. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the trial court's dismissal was proper because equity jurisdiction was not available where appellant had failed to exhaust its remedies under the Fourth to Eighth Class County Assessment Law, 72 P.S. § 5453.701. Further, the court held that the dual function of appellees under 72 P.S. §§ 5453.301, 302, 403, and 601 did not violate appellant's due process rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV. In addition, appellant was not denied its equal protection rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and the Uniformity Clause of Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant hospital's complaint for injunctive and declaratory relief against appellee county commissioners, because equity jurisdiction was not available where appellant failed to exhaust its remedies under the Fourth to Eighth Class County Assessment Law to challenge appellees' decision to tax appellant.

Grimaud v. Department of Envtl. Resources


No. 2618 C.D. 1992


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


161 Pa. Commw. 647; 638 A.2d 299; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 55


December 17, 1993 Argued  
February 4, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. EHB Docket No. 91-510-MR. State Agency, Environmental Hearing Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landowners appealed from on order of the Environmental Hearing Board (Pennsylvania), which granted intervenor sewage treatment facility's motion to quash petitioners' appeal of a sewage permit issued by respondent Department of Environmental Resources as untimely and denying petitioner's request to appeal nunc pro tunc.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner landowners filed an untimely notice of appeal to the Environmental Hearing Board (board), challenging respondent Department of Environmental Resources' issuance of a National Pollution Discharge Elimination System Permit. Intervenor sewage treatment facility filed a motion to quash petitioner's appeal. The board granted intervenor's motion and dismissed the appeal. In addition, the board denied petitioners' request for allowance of appeal nunc pro tunc. On appeal, the court affirmed the board's order, holding that the regulatory procedures adopted by respondent were reasonably adequate to afford petitioners due process notice of an impending action affecting their properties. Further, the court held that the prescribed time limits for appeals were jurisdictional and that, when petitioners failed to appeal the properly noticed administrative agency action within the prescribed time period, the board lost jurisdiction. The court held that there were no facts touching jurisdiction which were at issue; therefore no hearing was required. Finally, the court declined the opportunity to judicially enlarge non-negligent justifications for nunc pro tunc appeals.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Environmental Hearing Board's order which granted intervenor sewage treatment facility's motion to quash petitioner landowners' appeal of a sewage permit issued by respondent Department of Environmental Resources. Further, the court declined the prospect of judicially enlarging non-negligent justifications for nunc pro tunc appeals.

Pa. Human Relations Comm'n v. Sch. Dist.


No. 1056 C.D. 1973


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


161 Pa. Commw. 658; 638 A.2d 304; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 59



February 4, 1994, FILED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Pennsylvania Human Relations Commission and intervenors, parents and others, sought enforcement of petitioner's final order rejecting the voluntary desegregation plan of respondent School District of Philadelphia, and the court's desegregation order.


OVERVIEW: After petitioner Pennsylvania Human Relations Commission rejected the desegregation plan of respondent School District of Philadelphia, petitioner sought to have its final order enforced, in which intervenors, parents and others, joined. Petitioner and intervenors also sought enforcement of the court's order that denied in part respondent's motion for directed verdict. The court granted petitioner's motion and directed counsel to appear to review the process for court appointment of a master or team to develop a desegregation plan and timetable for implementation. Petitioner was authorized to order racially-based assignment of students to correct de facto segregation, and thus, so was respondent. State courts were not precluded from remedying de facto segregation under state law. Respondent had a legal responsibility and duty to take steps to correct the de facto segregation, and petitioner properly considered current conditions of segregation. Respondent failed to desegregate the public schools by all feasible means and continued to maintain a racially segregated school environment.


OUTCOME: The court granted the request of petitioner Pennsylvania Human Relations Commission and intervenors, parents and others, to enforce petitioner's final order rejecting the desegregation plan of defendant School District of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), and the court's desegregation order, and directed counsel of the parties to appear to review the process for court appointment of a master or team to develop a desegregation plan and timetable.

 

616 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Mandl v. Commonwealth


No. 311 M.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


161 Pa. Commw. 481; 637 A.2d 703; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 32


September 13, 1993, Submitted on Briefs  
January 26, 1994, FILED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, retail bakers, filed a motion for post-trial relief from the court's amended order which denied their request for declaratory judgment and dismissed their petition for review. Petitioners had unsuccessfully challenged their classification as eating establishments and had attempted to fit within a tax exemption for wholesale bakers.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, retail bakers, challenged their classification as an eating establishment and the six percent sales tax which accompanied it. Petitioners sought to classify themselves with wholesale bakers to take advantage of tax exemptions available to wholesale bakers. The court had dismissed their request for declaratory judgment and dismissed their petition for review. Petitioner filed for post trial relief, and the court denied the petition. The court found that wholesale bakers generally employed far more people than retail bakers and were subject to inducements such as tax-exempt status to relocate within Pennsylvania. The classification in this case did not violate the uniformity clause of the Pa. Const. or the equal protection clause of the U.S. Const. amend XIV because there was a legitimate distinction between wholesale and retail bakers. The distinction between wholesale and retail provided a nonarbitrary, reasonable, and just basis to grant a processing exclusion for sales and use tax to wholesale and not retail bakers because it sought to encourage wholesale bakers to relocate within Pennsylvania, thus providing jobs and tax revenues.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner retail bakers' motion for post trial relief and affirmed its decision to deny petitioners' request for declaratory judgment and to deny their petition for review. The court found that the taxing classification challenged by petitioners was based upon a legitimate distinction which showed a nonarbitrary, reasonable, and just basis for the grant of a processing exclusion to wholesale and not retail bakers.

Public Advocate v. Philadelphia Gas Comm'n


No. 2545 C.D. 1992


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


161 Pa. Commw. 428; 637 A.2d 676; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 24


October 6, 1993, ARGUED  
January 14, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 2961 January Term, 1992. Common Pleas Court of Philadelphia County. Judge LORD.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff consumer groups appealed the judgment of the Common Pleas Court of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) which affirmed in part and reversed in part defendant gas commission's decision granting a rate increase to defendant gas works.


OVERVIEW: Defendant gas commission granted a rate increase to defendant gas works and plaintiff consumer groups appealed to the trial court. Defendants, city and gas works management corporation, intervened. The trial court affirmed in part and reversed in part, holding that defendant commission did not violate the Sunshine Act, that a city ordinance which required that the base rate be calculated to include a fixed annual payment to defendant city was not unconstitutional, and that defendant commission erred in reducing defendant gas works' rate relief for implementation of a cost-saving measure. On appeal, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court remanded for recalculation of the rate increase without the fixed payment to defendant city because it found that the fixed amount was arbitrary in that it did not represent a fair return on defendant city's investment in defendant gas works and because there was no relationship between the amount and benefits conferred on rate payers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded for recalculation of defendant gas works' rate increase because an annual payment to defendant city previously included in the base rate calculation was arbitrary in that it did not represent a fair return on defendant city's investment.

 

618 of 892 DOCUMENTS


In re Condemnation of Right of Way for Legislative Route 1876 (In re Marivitz)


No. 1612 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


161 Pa. Commw. 247; 636 A.2d 1241; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 3


November 19, 1993, ARGUED  
January 6, 1994, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal and/or Cross-Petition Denied December 13, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Common Pleas Court of Allegheny County. Judge WEKSELMAN.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Department of Transportation challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee landowners' preliminary objections to a declaration of taking filed by appellant to acquire the fee title underlying the easement for highway purposes previously acquired by appellant in the subject property.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Department of Transportation had acquired a highway easement on appellee landowners' land. Appellant did not improve the land. Appellant sought a declaration of taking seeking to acquire the underlying fee with respect to the property, and appellees resisted. The lower court sustained appellees' preliminary objections to the taking, holding that, because appellant did not seek the taking for highway use, the taking was improper, and appellant sought review. The court reversed the lower court's decision. The court held that 71 P.S. § 513(e) allowed appellant more rights than merely an easement in the land and that a taking need not have been solely for highway purposes. Because appellees did not allege that appellant formally vacated the easement in their land, the lower court was not permitted to ignore the controlling case law to find that appellant abandoned its rights in the property. The decision not to construct the originally envisioned structures did not constitute the formal vacation of the easement in the manner required by law. The court held that an abandonment of the easement did not occur because appellant never formally vacated the easement.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that appellant Department of Transportation's declaration of the taking of the underlying fee was valid and reversed the order sustaining appellee landowners' preliminary objections and remanded the case for further proceedings. The taking was valid because appellant was not required to use easement for highway purposes and had never formally abandoned its easement.

Farley v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


No. 297 C.D. 1993, No. 303 C.D. 1993, No. 330 C.D. 1993, No. 333 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


161 Pa. Commw. 229; 636 A.2d 1232; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 11


October 6, 1993, ARGUED  
January 6, 1994, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied October 21, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 90-00713, 90-21533, 90-20676 and 90-03108. Common Pleas Court of Montgomery County. Judge LAWRENCE.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed appellee zoning hearing board's decision to deny appellants' challenges to the validity of appellee township's zoning ordinance that restricted the number of student homes and the number of individuals allowed to reside in such homes.


OVERVIEW: Appellant property owners challenged appellee township's zoning ordinance which restricted the number of student homes and the number of individuals allowed to reside in such homes. Appellee zoning hearing board denied appellants' challenges to the validity of the zoning ordinance, and the trial court affirmed. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that collateral estoppel only barred it from considering the federal constitutional challenges of those appellants who participated in a prior federal court action, and found that appellants had standing to sue under the Pennsylvania Municipalities Planning Code, 53 P.S. §§ 10909.1(a)(1) and 10913.3. Further, the court held that the ordinance did not violate appellants' equal protection rights because it was rationally related to a legitimate government purpose, and that the ordinance was not unconstitutionally vague because it provided fair notice to appellants. Lastly, the court held that the ordinance was not an unconstitutional partial exclusion of a particular type of use given that appellants failed to meet their burden of proving that there was a lack of "fair share" of a particular type of zoning.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that affirmed the denial of appellant property owners' challenges to appellee township's zoning ordinance, because collateral estoppel barred the federal constitutional claims of several appellants, and the ordinance did not violate appellants' equal protection rights, was not unconstitutionally vague, and was not an unconstitutional partial exclusion of a particular type of use.

Stone & Edwards Ins. Agency v. Dep't of Ins.


No. 216 M.D. 1992, No. 317 M.D. 1992


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


161 Pa. Commw. 177; 636 A.2d 293; 1994 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 2


September 15, 1993, ARGUED  
January 3, 1994, FILED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, insurance agency and agents, and respondents, insurance department and officers, filed motions for summary judgment in an action seeking to have § 506 of the Administrative Code, 71 P.S. § 186, § 102(a) of the Administrative Agency Law, 2 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 102(a), and §§ 7, 8, and 9 of the Unfair Insurance Practices Act, 40 P.S. §§ 1171.7-1171.11, ruled unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, insurance agency and agents, filed an action against respondents, insurance department and officers, seeking to have portions of the Administrative Code, 71 P.S. § 186, of the Administrative Agency Law, 2 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 102(a), and the Unfair Insurance Practices Act (UIPA), 40 P.S. §§ 1171.7-1171.11, declared unconstitutional. Both parties filed motions for summary judgment. The court granted respondents' motion for summary judgment, holding that the uncontroverted evidence established that the UIPA, which, on its face vested both prosecutorial and adjudicative functions in respondent department, was constitutional because there was no impermissible commingling where, in practice, a wall of division between the insurance commissioner, the adjudicator, and the prosecutor existed. The court granted respondents' motion for summary judgment, and denied petitioners' request for mandamus, holding that respondents' holding of petitioners' license applications pending the outcome of the enforcement action did not violate petitioners' due process rights because petitioners did not have a protected property interest in a license which did not yet exist.


OUTCOME: The court granted partial summary judgment to respondents, insurance department and officers, in the action filed by petitioners, insurance company and agents, because the statute under which enforcement proceedings were brought was constitutional insofar as there was not an impermissible commingling of prosecutorial and adjudicative functions. Moreover, respondents' "holding" of petitioners' license applications did not violate due process.

Commonwealth ex rel. Gallas v. Pa. Labor Relations Bd.


No. 314 M.D. 1992


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


161 Pa. Commw. 97; 636 A.2d 253; 1993 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 787


October 6, 1993, Submitted  
December 30, 1993, FILED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner judges and respondent unions each filed motions for summary judgment in petitioners' action for extraordinary relief, which challenged respondents' attempt to organize court personnel under the Public Employee Relations Act, 43 P.S. §§ 1101.101 - 1101.2301.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner judges filed an action for extraordinary relief after a hearing examiner concluded that only judicial secretaries to common pleas judges were confidential employees excluded from organization under the Public Employee Relations Act (Act), 43 P.S. §§ 1101.101 - 1101.2301. Petitioners and respondent unions each filed motions for summary judgment. The court granted each party's motion in part and denied each motion in part. The court held that the judicial secretaries and judicial tipstaves were confidential employees pursuant to 43 P.S. 1101.301(2) and thus could not be included in respondents' proposed collective bargaining unit. However, the court held that other court personnel were includable, and held that such inclusion did not violate § 2301(a)(2) of the Judicial Code, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2301(a)(2), did not violate Canon 3B(4) of the Code of Judicial Conduct, and did not violate the separation of powers doctrine of Pa. Const. art. V, § 1.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner judges' and respondent unions' motions for summary judgment in part, because judicial secretaries and judicial tipstaves were confidential employees and thus could not be included in respondents' proposed collective bargaining unit. However, other court personnel were includable, and that their inclusion did not violate the Code of Judicial Conduct or the separation of powers doctrine.

San Van v. Sch. Dist.


No. 258 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


160 Pa. Commw. 483; 635 A.2d 254; 1993 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 813


September 15, 1993, Argued  
December 15, 1993, Decided  
December 15, 1993, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied April 25, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. 84-S-1991. State Agency, Common Pleas Court of Dauphin County. Judge TURGEON


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas, Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which granted declaratory relief to appellee, holding that appellants' imposition of a 10 percent amusement tax violated 53 P.S. § 6908(10) and the Pennsylvania Constitution.


OVERVIEW: Appellee operated a miniature golf course. Appellants sought to impose an amusement tax on appellee's facility under a township ordinance imposing an amusements tax. Appellee asserted that the tax violated the Local Tax Enabling Act (Act), 53 P.S. § 6908, which provided for limitations on the amount of tax imposed by political subdivisions on golf courses. Appellee sought declaratory relief, seeking to invalidate imposition of the amusement tax under the ordinance. The trial court held that the amusement tax, as applied to appellee, violated Section 8(10) of the Act, 53 P.S. 6908(10), as well as Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1. On appeal, the court reversed. It found that appellants had the authority to impose such an amusement tax on miniature golf courses, as they were distinct from and not included within the meaning of golf courses under § 6908(10). Appellee failed to meet its burden of proving there was no distinction between golf courses and miniature golf courses. Thus, the trial court erred in ruling that the imposition of an amusement tax on appellee's miniature golf course violated the statute and the uniformity clause of the Pennsylvania Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's ruling that appellants' ordinance imposing an amusement tax on appellee's miniature golf course was unconstitutional. The ordinance was valid as applied to appellee; there was no showing that a miniature golf course was included within provisions limiting taxes to be levied by political subdivisions on golf courses.

Cherry v. City of Phila.


NO. 579 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


160 Pa. Commw. 179; 634 A.2d 754; 1993 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 730


October 5, 1993, Argued  
November 24, 1993, Decided  
November 24, 1993, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted November 18, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 467 November Term, 1992. Common Pleas Court of Philadelphia County. Judge AVELLINO.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a declaratory judgment action filed by appellant attorney against appellee city, appellant challenged the decision from the Common Pleas Court of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) that sustained appellee's preliminary objections and dismissed the complaint without prejudice for lack of jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: Appellee city notified appellant attorney that, by doing business within the city, he was in violation of the taxing and licensing provisions of the city code. Appellant filed a declaratory judgment action against appellee. The trial court sustained appellee's preliminary objections that appellant had failed to exhaust his administrative remedies and failed to sate a cause of action upon which relief could be granted, determining that it lacked jurisdiction and dismissed the complaint without prejudice. Seeking review, appellant claimed he was not required to pursue his administrative remedies because he presented a substantial question of constitutionality so that the trial court should have exercised its equitable jurisdiction under the Declaratory Judgments Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 7531-7541. The court noted that appellant only claimed that "as applied," the tax was unconstitutional, which was insufficient to invoke equity jurisdiction. Because appellant's complaint did not set forth a substantial constitutional question, the trial court did not err in refusing to exercise its equity jurisdiction to decide the case under the act. The trial court's decision was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision to sustain appellee's preliminary objections and dismiss the complaint without prejudice for lack of equity jurisdiction under the Declaratory Judgments Act because appellant's complaint did not set forth a substantial constitutional question and he had not pursued administrative remedies.

Rebottini v. State Ethics Comm'n


No. 2165 C.D. 1992, No. 2166 C.D. 1992, No. 2167 C.D. 1992, No. 2168 C.D. 1992


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


160 Pa. Commw. 157; 634 A.2d 743; 1993 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 716


June 18, 1993, Argued  
November 23, 1993, Decided  
November 23, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied December 15, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Pa. LEXIS 764.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From Orders Nos. 861, 862, 863 and 864. State Agency, State Ethics Commission.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner municipal authority board members sought review of orders from respondent State Ethics Commission (Pennsylvania), which determined that each petitioners had violated Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 43, § 403(a) by using their public office to obtain financial gain other than compensation provided for by law, and ordered the payment of restitution.


OVERVIEW: On review, the court reversed the factual findings with regard to petitioner municipal authority board members' conduct concerning the North Huntingdon Township Municipal Authority. The court found that the compensation was provided for by law, noting that Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 309C specifically authorized petitioners' actions with respect to petitioners' voting on their own appointment to officer positions and their own salaries. Nevertheless, the court then reversed with respect to the conclusion that petitioners' actions as Western Westmoreland Municipal Authority members had violated Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 65, § 403(a). The court noted that petitioners had done more than just vote on their officer election and salaries; petitioners had created officer positions specifically to circumvent the Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 309B requirement that board members salaries be set by the authority's governing bodies. The court concluded by vacating the restitution order, noting that the statute in effect at the time of petitioners' actions did not provide for such a remedy.


OUTCOME: Although the court reversed the determination that petitioner municipal authority board members' actions in voting on their own appointment to officers and their own salaries violated the State Ethics Act, and vacated the restitution order, the court affirmed the conclusion that a violation occurred in petitioners' attempt to create new officer positions in order to avoid the requisite vote on proposed salaries.

Pennsylvania State Police v. Pennsylvania State Troopers' Ass'n


NO. 980 C.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


159 Pa. Commw. 489; 633 A.2d 1278; 1993 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 831; 144 L.R.R.M. 2700


April 8, 1992, Submitted  
November 8, 1993, Decided  
November 8, 1993, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. Order dated April 1, 1991. State Agency Pennsylvania State Troopers' Association.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and Reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner State Police sought review of an arbitration award by State Agency Pennsylvania State Troopers' Association that sustained respondent trooper's grievance and held that respondent did not engage in unbecoming conduct when he exposed himself in his place of employment.


OVERVIEW: Respondent trooper exposed himself in his place of employment, was charged with unbecoming conduct, and disciplinary action was imposed, but was reversed by an arbitrator. Petitioner state police contended that this was in error. The court determined that the collective bargaining agreement between petitioner state police, respondent trooper, and the union provided for grievance arbitration, and that the agreement fell within the scope of the Uniform Arbitration Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 42 §§ 7301-7320, specifically §7302(d), which specified the essence test as the proper standard for review. The court determined that once it was found that the conduct took place, respondent's conduct could not have been deemed not unbecoming because the finding did not meet the requisite test because the conduct was an affront to the public and could have been subject to criminal charges. The court reversed the arbitrator's decision and reinstated respondent's suspension.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the arbitrator's award and reinstated the suspension of respondent trooper as previously imposed, having determined that respondent's conduct was not only an affront to the public, but could have been subject to criminal charges, and constituted unbecoming conduct.

Times Publishing Co. v. Michel


NO. 1390 C.D. 1992, NO. 1488 C.D. 1992, NO. 1542 C.D. 1992


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


159 Pa. Commw. 398; 633 A.2d 1233; 1993 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 679; 22 Media L. Rep. 1138


March 3, 1993, Argued  
November 1, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal and/or Cross-Petition Denied June 17, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. 4505-1992. Common Pleas Court of Erie County. Judge LEVIN


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, reporter and publisher, and appellee sheriff both sought review of an order of the Common Pleas Court of Erie County (Pennsylvania), which granted appellants limited access to appellee's approved firearm applications under the Right-To-Know Act, 65 P.S. §§ 66.1-66.4.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, reporter and publisher, were refused the right to view the applications for firearm licenses that appellee sheriff had issued. Under the Right-To-Know Act (Act), 65 P.S. §§ 66.1-66.4, the trial court held that only the address and the telephone and social security numbers of the licensees were not subject to disclosure. The trial court granted the disclosure only of licensees' names from the applications that predated the order. Both parties appealed and the court affirmed because public disclosure of the licensees' address and telephone and social security numbers constituted an unwarranted invasion of personal privacy that outweighed any public benefit. The remaining information in the applications did not implicate the same concerns and was not protected from disclosure. The court reversed the order that restricted retroactive disclosure to names only because even though existing license holders had no notice that their information would be subject to disclosure, they were not deprived of any right due to the fact that address and telephone and social security numbers were exempt from disclosure under the Act's personal security exception.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that granted appellants, reporter and publisher, limited access to approved firearm applications because the licensees' address and telephone and social security numbers were exempt from disclosure under the personal security exception of the Right-to-Know Act. The court reversed the prohibition against retroactive disclosure because existing license holders were not deprived of any right.

McGrath v. State Bd. of Dentistry


No. 25 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


159 Pa. Commw. 159; 632 A.2d 1027; 1993 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 644


June 18, 1993, Argued  
October 19, 1993, Decided  
October 19, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From File No. 90-46-02210. State Agency, State Board of Dentistry.



DISPOSITION: Because McGrath did not raise his procedural due process argument before the SBOD, nor was he prevented from raising it there, he has waived the issue on appeal. Accordingly, we affirm the order of the SBOD.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner dentist sought review of a decision of respondent State Board of Dentistry (Pennsylvania), which suspended his license to practice dentistry and imposed a civil penalty on him.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed respondent board of dentistry's order, which suspended petitioner dentist's license to practice and imposed a civil penalty because he failed to raise his arguments before respondent. While the court was permitted, under Pa. R. App. P. 1551(a)(1), to consider a challenge to the facial validity of a statute even when that question was not previously raised before the governmental unit, petitioner had failed to include the issue in his petition for review. The court further held that petitioner failed to properly preserve his second argument for appeal. The court held that Pa. R. App. P. 1551(a)(3) was not applicable because the "commingling-of-functions" issue could have been raised before respondent prior to its adjudication and, therefore, petitioner failed to exercise due diligence under the rule. Moreover, the case relied upon by petitioner merely reaffirmed the constitutional requirement that, where both prosecutorial and adjudicatory functions reposed in a single administrative agency, walls of division had to be constructed to eliminate the threat or appearance of bias, and did not establish any new legal principle nor overrule past precedent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed respondent board's order, which suspended petitioner dentist's license to practice and imposed a civil penalty, because petitioner failed to raise his procedural due process arguments before respondent and failed to present the issue in his petition for review.

In re Substitute Nomination Certificate of Evans


:No. 340 M.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


158 Pa. Commw. 297; 631 A.2d 797; 1993 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 587


September 1, 1993, Heard  
September 2, 1993, Decided  
September 2, 1993, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] Publication Ordered September 20, 1993.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Memorandum Opinion Redesignated Opinion and Ordered Pubished September 20, 1993.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner objectors filed a petition objecting to and seeking to set aside a substitute nomination certificate under section 979 of the Election Code, 25 P.S. § 2939, and respondent candidate filed a motion to dismiss the objection petition as untimely under section 982 of the Election Code, 25 P.S. § 2942.


OVERVIEW: Respondent candidate sought and won the Republican Party nomination for common pleas court judge and the Democratic Party filed a substitute nomination certificate, substituting respondent as the Democratic candidate for that position. Petitioner objectors filed a petition objecting to and seeking to set aside the substitute nomination certificate. The court dismissed the petition because under section 981.1 of the Election Code, 25 P.S. § 2949.1, a candidate for common pleas court judge was exempt from the prohibition in section 979 of the Election Code, 25 P.S. § 2939, against the substitute nomination by a political party of a candidate who was already nominated by any other political party for the same office. The court also granted respondent's motion to dismiss the objection petition as untimely because petitioners filed the objection petition within three days after the substitute nomination certificate was filed, but did not serve the Bureau of Elections within three days. The court found that the three-day limit of 25 P.S. § 2942 applied to both filing and service and that the seven-day period found in 25 P.S. § 2937 did not apply to service, as petitioners argued.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition because respondent candidate, a nominee for a position on the court of common pleas, was not subject to the statute prohibiting a person already nominated by one political party from being nominated as a substitute candidate by another political party for the same office and granted respondent's motion to dismiss because petitioner objectors did not timely perfect their objection.

Pa. Institutional Health Servs. v. Dep't of Corr.


No. 22 M.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


158 Pa. Commw. 221; 631 A.2d 767; 1993 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 547

August 31, 1993, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners health care company and individual doctors and psychiatrists filed suit challenging debarment proceedings to be conducted by respondent department of corrections. Respondent filed preliminary objections, asserting that petitioners failed to state a claim upon which relief could be granted and failed to exhaust their administrative remedies before seeking judicial review.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, health care services company and individual doctors and psychiatrists, were awarded a contract by respondent department of corrections to provide health care services to inmates. Following an inmate death, respondent notified petitioners that they were being suspended and that respondent was contemplating their debarment, which would preclude them from further contracting with state agencies. Petitioners filed suit challenging the implementation of contractor suspension and debarment. Respondent filed preliminary objections, asserting that petitioners failed to state a cognizable cause of action and failed to exhaust available administrative remedies. In overruling respondent's preliminary objections, the court concluded that petitioners presented a valid constitutional claim. Where the official who would sit in judgment on the debarment proceedings was the same official who issued the notice of suspension, the potential for bias and the appearance of non-objectivity created a fatal constitutional defect. Where the remedy itself was constitutionally flawed, it would be futile to require petitioners to exhaust that remedy before seeking judicial review.


OUTCOME: The preliminary objections of respondent department of corrections were overruled, as petitioners, health care services company and individual doctors and psychiatrists, asserted a valid claim in challenging the constitutional validity of the administrative remedial procedures. Where the constitutionality of the remedy was at issue, the requirement that such remedy be exhausted would not be enforced.

 

633 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Dep't of Transp. v. Brown


No. 419 C.D. 1987, No. 969 C.D. 1987, No. 2862 C.D. 1987


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


157 Pa. Commw. 610; 630 A.2d 927; 1993 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 518


June 18, 1993, Submitted on Briefs  
August 17, 1993, Decided  
August 17, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal and/or Cross-Petition Granted May 13, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 5945 of 1986, 6881 of 1986 and 87-3644. Common Pleas Court of Washington County. Judge BELL.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the decisions of Judge John F. Bell are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant department of transportation sought review of a judgment from the Common Pleas Court of Washington County (Pennsylvania), which sustained appeals brought by appellee driver licensees challenging the suspension of their licenses pursuant to 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1519(c).


OVERVIEW: After suffering seizures, appellee driver licensees had their operating privileges suspended by appellant department of transportation under the provisions of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1519(c). The trial court sustained appeals brought by appellees. The trial court determined that the regulation upon which appellant based the suspensions, 62 Pa. Code § 83.4, was substantively unreasonable and offensive to epileptic licensees' procedural due process rights. The court affirmed, finding that operating privileges constituted a constitutionally protected property interest which appellant could not terminate without providing due process protection. The court found that the suspension of issued licenses involved state action that adjudicated important interests of appellees and, thus, licenses could not be taken away without the procedural due process required by U.S. Const. amend IV. Additionally, the court found that the regulations, by creating a presumption of incompetence, denied appellees an opportunity to present evidence rebutting the presumption of incompetence. The court found no paramount state justification for curtailing appellees' procedural rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment that sustained appeals brought by appellee driver licensees which challenged the suspension of their drivers' licenses by appellant department of transportation. The court held that operating privileges constituted a constitutionally protected property interest which appellant could not terminate without providing due process protection.

Giosa v. Sch. Dist.


No. 796 C.D. 1992


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district challenged a decision of the Common Pleas Court of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which entered judgment in favor of appellee injured union worker, finding appellant 100 percent negligent for appellee's injuries when he fell on a district sidewalk while striking.


OVERVIEW: Appellee injured union worker filed an action against appellant school district for injuries sustained when he slipped and fell on appellant's sidewalk while striking with his union. The court overruled appellant's remaining points of error.


OUTCOME: In appellee injured union worker's action to recover for injuries sustained in a slip and fall, the court affirmed the finding of appellant school district's negligence, but reversed and remanded for a recalculation of damages after deducting money appellee received under an insurance policy.

Giant Eagle v. Milk Mktg. Bd.


No. 1998 C.D. 1992


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


157 Pa. Commw. 419; 630 A.2d 478; 1993 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 474


March 30, 1993, Argued  
July 30, 1993, Decided  
July 30, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Application for Reargument, Reconsideration and Modification is Denied September 10, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 568. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied November 3, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Pa. LEXIS 589.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From Legal Docket No. CC-92-193. State Agency, Pennsylvania Milk Marketing Board.



DISPOSITION: As noted previously, the sole basis for denial of Giant Eagle's application was the Board's erroneous interpretation of section 404. Therefore, we reverse the order of the Board and remand this matter to the Board with instructions to grant Giant Eagle's application.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner corporation appealed an order of respondent Milk Marketing Board (Pennsylvania), which denied petitioner's application for a milk dealer's license under 31 P.S. § 700j-404, because petitioner was not licensed as a milk dealer on the effective date of the statute. Petitioner contended that the statute merely prohibits respondent from denying a store's application for a license if that store was licensed on the effective date.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner corporation owned a chain of retail grocery stores. Petitioner filed an application with respondent Milk Marketing Board to obtain a milk dealer's license. Respondent denied petitioner's application on grounds that 31 P.S. § 700j-404, only allowed the issuance of a license to those applicants who were licensed as milk dealers as of the effective date of the amendment to the statute. Petitioner appealed and contended that respondent erred in its interpretation and argued that the statute merely prohibited respondent from denying an application for a milk dealer's license if that applicant was licensed as a milk dealer as of the effective date. The court agreed and reversed the order. The court held that respondent's interpretation of the statute would lead to an absurd and unreasonable result, since the passage of time would extinguish the pool of applicant's and leave the state without milk dealers. The court remanded the matter to respondent with instructions to grant petitioner's application.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of respondent Milk Marketing Board which denied petitioner corporation's application for a milk dealer's license on grounds that petitioner was not licensed as a milk dealer on the effective date of the statute, because respondent's interpretation of the statute led to an absurd and unreasonable result and was therefore clearly erroneous.

637 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Keener v. Carnegie Natural Gas Co. (in Re Carnegie Natural Gas Co.)


No. 200 C.D. 1993


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


157 Pa. Commw. 217; 629 A.2d 256; 1993 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 444


June 17, 1993, Argued  
July 19, 1993, Decided  
July 19, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. Misc. 87 of 1992 and No. 21 in Equity, 1992. Common Pleas Court of Allegheny County



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, we affirm.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, property owners, sought review of the decision of the Common Pleas Court, Greene County (Pennsylvania), which approved the bond of appellee natural gas company and granted a writ of possession to appellee for an easement along the edge of appellants' property for installation of a gas distribution line.


OVERVIEW: Appellee natural gas company commenced eminent domain proceedings pursuant to 15 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. 1511(g), after unsuccessfully negotiating with appellants, property owners, to secure an easement for the gas distribution line along the boundary of appellants' property. The trial court granted a writ of possession to appellee for the easement and appellants sought review. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court and held that 15 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 1511(g)(2) was not unconstitutional because appellants were afforded due process. Appellants failed to prove that the condemnation was arbitrary and capricious; they only proved that other alternatives existed. The court concluded that no matter who currently owned the property or if a private company also benefitted from the construction, a gas line servicing a public prison facility served a public purpose. Therefore, the taking by eminent domain did not lose its public character merely because there may have existed therein some feature of private gain. If the public good was enhanced, it was immaterial that a private interest was also benefitted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court in favor of appellee natural gas company because appellants, property owners, failed to prove that the condemnation of their property was arbitrary and capricious. The taking did not lose its public character simply because there could have been some feature of private gain.

City of Harrisburg v. McNulty


No. 236 M.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


156 Pa. Commw. 593; 628 A.2d 914; 1993 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 407


March 31, 1993, Argued  
July 6, 1993, Decided  
July 6, 1993, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city brought a class action in the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania against respondent Pennsylvania Department of Revenue that sought a declaratory judgment and mandamus relief. Petitioner filed a motion for partial judgment on its pleadings to declare invalid an administrative regulation promulgated by respondent under the Public Utility Realty Tax Act, 72 P.S. §§ 8101-A -- 8108-A.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner city commenced a class action in the court against respondent Pennsylvania Department of Revenue, which requested a declaratory judgment to invalidate an administrative regulation promulgated by respondent, along with mandamus relief. The court denied petitioner's motion for partial judgment on the pleadings because respondent's regulation of 61 Pa. Code § 159.1(b) was neither invalid or unconstitutional. The court held that petitioner could proceed with its mandamus action to compel respondent to conduct its statutory obligations under the Public Utility Realty Tax Act, 72 P.S. §§ 8101-A -- 8108-A. The court determined that the regulation did not prevent petitioner or the class members from bringing an action before the court to compel respondent to perform its statutory obligations. Thus, the court upheld the regulation because it reflected the legislative decision to not establish an administrative or statutory remedy to local taxing authorities that sought timely and proper distribution of collected real estate taxes. The court considered this legislative decision to be appropriate because respondent's only responsibility for distribution was simply administrative.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner city's motion for partial judgment on the pleadings and held that the regulation promulgated by the respondent Pennsylvania Department of Revenue was not invalid or unconstitutional, and that petitioner could pursue its mandamus action in the court's original jurisdiction seeking to compel respondent to perform its statutory obligations under the Public Utility Realty Tax Act.

Commonwealth ex rel. Nicholas v. Pennsylvania Labor Rels. Bd.


No. 362 M. D. 1992


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


156 Pa. Commw. 498; 628 A.2d 485; 1993 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 395


February 3, 1993, Argued  
June 29, 1993, Decided  
June 29, 1993, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the preliminary objections of the Board and of the Teamsters are sustained, and the petition for review is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner judge, individually and on behalf of the judges of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, and petitioner Montgomery County, filed a petition for review in the nature of a complaint in equity, quo warranto, prohibition, and for declaratory relief under the Public Employee Relations Act, 43 P.S. §§ 1101.101-1101.2301. Respondent Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board and intervenor teamsters filed preliminary objections.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner judge, individually and on behalf of the judges of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, and petitioner Montgomery County filed a complaint in the nature of equity, quo warranto, prohibition, and for declaratory relief under the Public Employee Relations Act (Act), 43 P.S. §§ 1101.101-1101.2301. Respondent Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board and intervenor teamsters filed preliminary objections. The court dismissed the request for review. The court sustained the demurrers to petitioners' first count because no violation of the separation of powers doctrine existed under relevant precedent. The Act's application to the courts was constitutional to the extent that judges retained supervisory control over their personnel. The courts remained able to take proper steps to maintain independence. The court sustained the demurrers to petitioners' second count because the relevant authorities invalidated the Sunset Act, which allowed respondent to exist and hence its actions were not null and void. The court sustained the demurrers to petitioners' final count because they failed to exhaust their administrative remedies under 34 Pa. Code § 95.96(b).


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the request of petitioner judge, individually and on behalf of the judges of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, and petitioner Montgomery County, for review of their complaint in the nature of equity, quo warranto, prohibition, and for declaratory relief under statute. The court sustained all of the preliminary objections of respondent Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board and intervenor teamsters.

DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the May 24, 1991 order of the trial court is reversed to the extent that it found a violation of the Sunshine Act, and is affirmed in all other respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff amusement entity and defendant district challenged the judgment of the Common Pleas Court of Chester County (Pennsylvania) which held that while the Tax Resolution of 1969 had not violated plaintiff's rights under the Due Process Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, its enforcement by defendant violated plaintiff's rights under the Equal Protection Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff amusement entity and defendant district appealed the trial court's judgment that found while defendant's Tax Resolution of 1969 (Resolution) had not violated the Due Process Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, its selective enforcement by defendant violated plaintiff's rights under the Equal Protection Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and the Sunshine Act (Act), 65 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 262. The court affirmed the portion of the trial court's judgment that found plaintiff's claims were not barred by the doctrines of res judicata or laches and that the Resolution was merely a tax on the patron and was not violative of plaintiff's rights under the Due Process Clause.
