Warner Co. v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


No. 1875 C.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


148 Pa. Commw. 609; 612 A.2d 578; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 450


March 4, 1992, Argued  
June 24, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied March 17, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. 90-08517. Common Pleas Court of Chester County. Judge THOMAS G. GAVIN


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant quarry owner sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed appellee zoning board's decision that Tredyffrin Township, Pa., Ordinance HR-135, § 1702A(6) was valid as adopted by appellee township.


OVERVIEW: Appellant quarry owner operated its quarry as a lawful preexisting nonconforming use. Appellee township adopted Tredyffrin Township, Pa., Ordinance HR-135, § 1702A(6), which established quarries as a use permitted by special exception in specific quarrying districts. Both appellee zoning board and the trial court upheld the ordinance's validity. On appeal, the court reversed as to §§ 1702A(6)(c), (d), (e), and (f) because they attempted to regulate surface mining and were preempted by the Noncoal Surface Mining Conservation and Reclamation Act (Act), 52 P.S. § 3316. The court affirmed as to §§ 1702A(6)(a), (b), and (g) because they dealt with traditional land use regulations that were a legitimate exercise of appellee township's powers and were not preempted by the Act. The ordinance was valid because it did not limit or attempt to prohibit appellant's continuation of use. Appellant failed to establish that the ordinance's imposition of setbacks and differential treatment were not related to the health, safety, morals, and general welfare of the community.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order upholding the validity of a rezoning ordinance as to the sections that regulated surface mining activities because they were preempted by the Noncoal Surface Mining Conservation and Reclamation Act. The court affirmed the validity of the remaining sections because they concerned traditional land use regulations and were valid exercises of appellee township's authority.

 

680 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Kratzer v. Board of Supervisors


No. 1616 C.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


148 Pa. Commw. 454; 611 A.2d 809; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 431


April 9, 1992, Argued  
June 11, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 44 of 1991. Common Pleas Court of Juniata County. Judge KEITH B. QUIGLEY, President Judge



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the order of the trial court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners sought review of a decision of the Common Pleas Court of Juniata County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an order of the township board of supervisors denying appellants' request for a curative amendment to the township zoning ordinance pursuant to 53 P.S. § 10609.1.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the trial court's affirmance of an order of respondent township board of supervisors, which denied appellant property owners' request for a curative amendment to a zoning ordinance. The court held that, while the ordinance did not specifically provide for two-family dwellings, it did allow the construction of "multi-family buildings" as a special exception in a certain residential district. The court held that two-family dwellings were included and subsumed within the term "multi-family buildings" because the ordinance defined two-family dwellings and treated other uses which would clearly be considered multi-family uses. The court held that it was inconceivable that the township would provide detailed definitions of these uses and yet fail to provide at least one district in which they could legally exist. Accordingly, the ordinance was not facially exclusionary of two-family residential uses. In addition, a use allowed by special exception was a permitted use, and an ordinance which allowed a use conditionally, as by special exception, was not de jure exclusionary. Thus, appellants failed to meet their burden of showing a partial or de facto exclusion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision affirming the order of the township board of supervisors, which denied appellant property owners' request for a curative amendment to the township zoning ordinance because appellants failed to meet their burden of proving a partial or de facto exclusion for two-family residential uses under the ordinance.

Demmy v. Pennsylvania State Police


No. 2035 C.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


148 Pa. Commw. 401; 611 A.2d 782; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 419


April 10, 1992, Argued  
June 5, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From Order dated August 23, 1991. State Agency, Pennsylvania State Police Commissioner



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, we affirm.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner security guard sought review of a decision of respondent state police commissioner, which revoked petitioner's lethal weapons certification because her visual acuity did not meet the standards of 37 Pa. Code § 21.11(3)(iv).


OVERVIEW: Respondent state police commissioner revoked the lethal weapons certification of petitioner, a security guard, because her visual acuity fell short of the standards of 37 Pa. Code § 21.11(3)(iv). Petitioner argued that her vision conformed to federal standards of the Atomic Energy Act (AEA), 42 U.S.C.S. §§2011-2296, and that these laws preempted the state regulations. While recognizing that Congress may preempt state law, the court noted that such legislation must contain explicit terms of preemption. The court did not find such language in the AEA. Petitioner also argued that the requirements of 37 Pa. Code §§21.5 and 21.21, regarding change in physical condition of an applicant for certification, were misapplied in her case because she had never been required to take a vision examination before. The court rejected her argument, holding that petitioner did not meet the vision standard when examined and therefore her license must be revoked.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of respondent state police commissioner, revoking petitioner security guard's lethal weapons certification because her visual acuity did not meet required standards. The court rejected petitioner's argument that federal regulations in this area preempted state law. The court also rejected petitioner's argument that respondent failed to show that her vision had changed since her last certification.

Curson v. West Conshohocken Municipal Authority


No. 2043 C.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


148 Pa. Commw. 386; 611 A.2d 775; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 423


March 5, 1992, Argued  
June 5, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 90-15914. Common Pleas Court of Montgomery County. Judge SAMUEL W. SALUS II.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants apartment owners sought review of a decision from the Common Pleas Court of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania) that ordered them to pay sewer connection fees to appellee municipal authority.


OVERVIEW: Appellants apartment owners were ordered by the lower court to pay sewer connection fees to appellee municipal authority. The court affirmed the order because it found no merit in appellants' claim that the connection fee per apartment unit was imposed without regard to the actual cost of the connection. They argued that it was therefore arbitrary, unreasonable, and unconstitutional. The court found that the fee was based on the actual cost of the connection and that appellee was authorized to collect such a fee by statute. The court also found appellants failed to offer any evidence that the fee was unreasonable or not uniform. The court affirmed also because the fee was applied uniformly to all apartment buildings.


OUTCOME: A lower court order that required appellants apartment owners to pay sewer connection fees to appellee municipal authority was affirmed. The court found the fee was based on the cost of the sewer construction and that it was uniformly imposed on all apartment buildings.

Cedarbrook Realty, Inc. v. Cheltenham Township


No. 227 C.D. 1991, No. 228 C.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


148 Pa. Commw. 310; 611 A.2d 335; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 406


November 22, 1991, Argued  
June 2, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied July 20, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. 83-18099. Common Pleas Court of Montgomery County. Judge RICHARD S. LOWE


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed appellant's exceptions and set final real estate tax assessments based on data furnished by appellee municipal bodies.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayer challenged the tax assessment of its property by appellee municipal bodies. The common pleas court dismissed appellant's exceptions and set a final assessment based on appellees' data. On appeal, the court affirmed the exclusion of the operating costs of appellant's country and golf club from the calculation of income because few residents utilized the clubs and appellant had never before challenged appellees' treatment of the clubs as separate parcels. The court remanded for re-evaluation of assessment methodologies in determining true market value and for allocating greater weight to the capitalization of income approach. The direct sales comparison approach relied on by the common pleas court ignored the current economic realities of the real estate market as well as the finding that appellant's property was unique. Because incorrect assessment ratios were applied to the property, the case was remanded for recalculation in accordance with 72 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5020-518.2(b) and the most recent State Tax Equalization Board ratios.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the exclusion of golf and country club operating expenses from appellant taxpayer's calculation of income because they were separate tax parcels. The court remanded for recalculation in accordance with the most recent State Tax Equalization Board ratios and for reconsideration of market value assessment methodologies because the direct sales comparison approach was inappropriately applied.

 

684 of 892 DOCUMENTS


City of Philadelphia v. Patton


NO. 2355 C.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


148 Pa. Commw. 141; 609 A.2d 903; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 379


March 5, 1992, Argued  
May 19, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted March 7, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 5150 February Term, 1990. Common Pleas Court of Philadelphia County. Judge EUGENE MAIER


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), that denied appellant's motion for a judgment on the pleadings, in a negligence action brought by appellee estate administrator. Appellant contended that the trial court erred in finding that a retroactive repeal of a waiver of governmental immunity was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city challenged the trial court's order that denied its motion for judgment on the pleadings in a negligence action brought be appellee estate administrator, which alleged that his wife died due to the negligent failure of appellant's police officers to provide immediate medical assistance. Appellant asserted that the trial court erred when it found that a retroactive repeal of a waiver of governmental immunity was unconstitutional. The court affirmed and noted that the legislature could not extinguish a right of action that had already accrued in appellee. The court further noted that a repeal of a law which created a right of action did not disturb an action accrued thereunder. The court found that substantive rights were governed by the law in effect at the time a cause of action accrued. The court held that appellee's cause of action accrued on the date his wife died, which was prior to the date an ordinance that prohibited appellant from pleading governmental immunity in negligence cases involving the police was repealed. The court would not permit appellant to constitutionally divest appellee of his vested right by retroactive application of the repealer ordinance.


OUTCOME: An order that denied appellant city's motion for a judgment on the pleadings was affirmed because the legislature could not extinguish an accrued right of action, and appellee estate administrator's substantive rights were governed by the law in effect at the time the action accrued. The court would not permit a retroactive application of a repealer ordinance that would have unconstitutionally divested appellee of his action.

Pennsylvania Ass'n of Life Underwriters v. Foster


No. 170 M.D. 1989 (AC)


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


147 Pa. Commw. 591; 608 A.2d 1099; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 347


December 16, 1991, Argued  
May 4, 1992, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner insurance agents and respondent statutory liquidator filed summary judgment motions with the court. Petitioners sought a declaration after respondent sent them a letter threatening to revoke their licenses unless they returned certain insurance commissions and agreed to cover one-half of unpaid claims originating after a certain date. Petitioners claimed due process violations.


OVERVIEW: Respondent statutory liquidator was appointed to an insolvent insurance company. Respondent sent a letter to petitioner insurance agents threatening to take away their licenses unless they repaid commissions and agreed to pay one-half of claims arising after a certain date. Both parties moved for summary judgment. The court denied respondent's motion after finding that petitioners were properly seeking a declaratory judgment under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 7541(a) to resolve uncertainty as to their statutory rights. The court also denied petitioners' motion for summary judgment. The court found that respondent's letter was an offer to settle a possible enforcement that did not usurp petitioners' due process rights. The court held that respondent had the statutory authority to issue the letter under the Insurance Department Act, 40 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 221.23(6). The court found that petitioners were unable to meet their burden of proof for summary judgment because 40 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 221.35(a), delineating respondent's right to recover insurance commissions, was reasonably amenable to two interpretations.


OUTCOME: The court denied the summary judgment motions of both petitioner insurance agents and respondent statutory liquidator. The court found that petitioners were properly seeking a declaratory judgment. The court found that petitioners could not meet their burden of proof on summary judgment because the statute was amendable to two interpretations.

Hudachek v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


No. 1378 C.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


147 Pa. Commw. 566; 608 A.2d 652; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 340


March 6, 1992, Argued  
April 29, 1992, Decided  
April 29, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 91-0030-14-5. Common Pleas Court of Bucks County. Judge EDWARD G. BIESTER, JR.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, we affirm the decision of the Bucks County Court of Common Pleas.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant homeowners sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas, Bucks County (Pennsylvania), that denied their challenge and upheld appellee Zoning Hearing Board's decision, which sustained a cease and desist order issued to appellants, dismissed appellant's constitutional challenge to the zoning ordinance, and denied their request for a variance.


OVERVIEW: Appellant homeowner's residence was located in an area zoned VR-2 as set forth in the joint municipal zoning ordinance (JMZO) adopted pursuant to 53 P.S. §§ 11101-A to 11108-A. When appellants applied for a building permit the plans submitted depicted a basement area which was to remain unfinished. Thereafter appellants decided to convert the basement into a law office. Such a home occupation was not permitted in a district zoned VR-2. Appellee borough's zoning officer issued a cease and desist order, which was sustained by appellee Zoning Hearing Board and affirmed by the trial court. On review, the court affirmed; appellant's challenge to validity of the constitutionality of the zoning ordinance was found to be meritless because 53 P.S. § 10811-A instructed courts to examine JMZO's as they applied to their entire jurisdictions and the JMZO under review permitted home occupation in other areas encompassed by it. Appellant's assertion of entitlement to a variance by estoppel was also without merit because appellants were duty bound to check the zoning status of their property, and there was no evidence that appellees showed some form of active acquiescence of the illegal use.


OUTCOME: The trial court order that upheld appellee Zoning Hearing Board's decision to cease and desist a home occupation was affirmed. The court found the doctrine of vested right to use property contrary to provisions of a zoning code was not applicable because a permit had never issued for a home occupation. Appellants were not entitled to a variance by estoppel because appellee borough and zoning hearing board timely enforced the zoning ordinance.

Pennsylvania Medical Soc. v. Foster


No. 137 M.D. 1990


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

September 11, 1991, Argued  
April 29, 1992, Decided  
April 29, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied May 29, 1992.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner medical society filed a motion for summary judgment with the court (Pennsylvania), based on its interpretations of the newly enacted physician billing and reimbursement provisions of the Motor Vehicle Financial Responsibility Law, 75 Pa. Const. Stat. §§ 1701-1799.7.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner medical society argued that the newly enacted physician billing and reimbursement provisions of the Motor Vehicle Financial Responsibility Law, 75 Pa. Const. Stat. §§ 1701-1799.7 violated physicians' substantive due process rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Pa. Const. art. I, § I, by unreasonably interfering with physicians' liberty and property interests in the practice of their profession. The court held that sections 1797(a) and 1797(b)(3), which prohibited direct patient billing for treatment of automobile accident victims by physicians, were not unconstitutionally overly broad because the sections pertained only to situations where a patient could reasonably be identified as an insured within the meaning of the statute. Furthermore, the court found the balance billing prohibition under section 1797 was rationally related to the valid state objective of regulating automobile insurance in a manner that made affordable insurance available. As such, the court denied petitioner's motion for summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner medical society's motion for summary judgment, holding that Motor Vehicle Financial Responsibility Law's prohibition against direct patient billing for treatment of automobile accident victims by physicians was not unconstitutionally overly broad because the prohibition only pertained to situations where a patient could reasonably be identified as an insured within the meaning of the statute.

Pennsylvania Ass'n of Rehabilitation Facilities v. Foster


NO. 368 M.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


147 Pa. Commw. 487; 608 A.2d 613; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 336


March 2, 1992, Argued  
April 28, 1992, Decided  
April 28, 1992, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, we overrule all of the Department's Preliminary Objections.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, an association of rehabilitation facilities, challenged the validity of 31 Pa. Code § 69.43(b), to which respondent insurance commissioner filed preliminary objections in the commonwealth court (Pennsylvania), contending that petitioner failed to exhaust its administrative remedies.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, an association of rehabilitation facilities, filed a petition for review, which challenged the validity of 31 Pa. Code § 69.43(b), a regulation that authorized relief from an existing Medicare reimbursement allowance. Respondent insurance commissioner filed preliminary objections to the petition, seeking injunctive relief. The court overruled respondent's preliminary objections and directed respondent to file an answer to the petition. The court exercised its pre-enforcement review of the regulation; the administrative remedy was inadequate and the effect of the regulation was direct and immediate. The court also noted that the rights of other facilities providing out-patient or home health care services was not impaired by the litigation, and the court held that petitioner's contention that § 69.43(b) was unconstitutionally confiscatory had the required specificity.


OUTCOME: The court overruled respondent insurance commissioner's preliminary objections to a request for review by petitioner, an association of rehabilitation facilities, holding that pre-enforcement review of a regulation was proper when the administrative remedies were either unavailable or inadequate.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowner challenged an order of the Common Pleas Court of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed his challenge to the validity of appellee board of commissioners' zoning ordinance; appellant argued that rezoning his property was confiscatory and illegal spot zoning.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landowner challenged the validity of appellee board of commissioners' zoning ordinance, which rezoned certain property in such a way so that property owners could no longer subdivide. Pennsylvania Bar Ass'n v. Commonwealth


NO. 120 M.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


147 Pa. Commw. 351; 607 A.2d 850; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 314


September 11, 1991, Argued  
April 20, 1992, Decided  
April 20, 1992, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Pennsylvania Bar Association sought summary relief asserting that the reporting requirements contained in 75 Pa. Const. Stat. §§ 1801-28 should be declared unconstitutional because it violated attorneys' procedural and substantive due process rights under the state and federal constitutions, deprived them of equal protection of the law, denied them a remedy for injury to reputation, and infringed on their protected speech.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner bar association filed a motion for summary relief asserting that the amendments contained in 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 1801-28 should be declared unconstitutional because it violated its members' procedural and substantive due process rights under the state and federal constitutions, deprived its members of equal protection of the law, denied its members a remedy for injury to reputation, and was impermissibly vague. Respondent insurance department contended that petitioner failed to show any harm resulting to its members from the operation. The court granted petitioner's motion on the basis that the reporting provisions of §§ 1801-28 were unconstitutional because they deprived petitioner's members of the fundamental right to protection of their reputations without regard for due process and were unconstitutionally vague. The court noted that petitioner was unable to provide specific instances of harm because, although it compiled attorney names who represented insurance claimants suspected of fraud, it did not disseminate that information. The court stated that maintenance of such a list constituted a serious threat to petitioner's members through negative stigmatization.


OUTCOME: The court granted summary relief to petitioner bar association on the basis that reporting provisions of the statute were unconstitutional because they deprived petitioner's members of the fundamental right to protection of their reputations without regard for due process and were unconstitutionally vague. The court stated that, although elimination of insurance fraud was significant, citizens could not be deprived of their rights arbitrarily.

Hitchings v. Pa. State Ethics Com


No. 273 C.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


147 Pa. Commw. 384; 607 A.2d 866; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 316


October 9, 1991, Submitted  
April 20, 1992, Decided  
April 20, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 799. State Agency, State Ethics Commission



DISPOSITION: The order of the Commission is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner fire captain sought review of a judgment from respondent, the State Ethics Commission (Pennsylvania). Respondent found that petitioner violated the State Ethics Act, 65 P.S. §§ 403(a) and 404(a), when he agreed to testify, at an hourly rate, for a theater owner that sought to prove it had not committed arson.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner fire captain, assigned to an arson team, investigated a fire that occurred at a theater. After a local television station reported that the fire was caused by arson, the theater owner filed a defamation suit against the station and hired petitioner to testify. Respondent, the State Ethics Commission, concluded that petitioner's work for the theater owner violated the provisions of the state ethics act, 65 P.S. §§ 403(a) and 404(a), which applied to public officials and public employees. The court reversed respondent's order. Petitioner was not a public employee and thus not subject to the provisions of the state ethics act. 51 Pa. Code § 1.1, which contained the definition of a public employee, stated that detectives were not public employees. Although neither the ethics act nor respondent's regulations defined the term "detective," the common and approved usage of the term indicate that a detective was one who performed investigations and who detected lawbreakers. Petitioner's duties, which centered on investigation and detection, rendered petitioner a detective. Petitioner was a detective, not a public employee, and only public employees were subject to the ethics act.


OUTCOME: The court reversed an order from respondent, the State Ethics Commission, which found that petitioner fire captain violated the State Ethics Act by accepting compensation for his testimony in a defamation suit. Petitioner was not subject to the provisions of the state ethics act because he was detective, not a public employee, under respondent's governing regulations.

 

692 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. 502-504 Gordon St.


NO. 1027 C.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


147 Pa. Commw. 330; 607 A.2d 839; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 308


December 16, 1991, Argued  
April 15, 1992, Decided  
April 15, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied June 24, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. 109 M of 1989. Common Pleas Court of Lehigh County. Judge MAXWELL E. DAVISON


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bar owners sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County (Pennsylvania), which forfeited to the state property owned by them together with all furnishings, equipment, and items of personalty found within on the basis that the bar operated on the premises was used or intended to be used to facilitate violations of the Controlled Substance, Drug, Device, and Cosmetic Act, 35 P.S. §§ 780-101 to 780-144.


OVERVIEW: Numerous raids by police at appellant owners' bar uncovered drugs concealed on patrons following pat-down searches. As a result, the lower court entered an order that the entire property and all contents, including the liquor license, be forfeited to the state on the basis that the bar was used or intended to be used to facilitate violations of the Controlled Substance, Drug, Device, and Cosmetic Act (Act), 35 P.S. §§ 780-101 to 780-144. The court found that testimony concerning the arrests made at appellants' bar, specific instances of unlawful drug activities, and physical evidence of drug-related materials on the premises supported the finding that the property was used to facilitate violations of the Act. Regardless, the court held that under the innocent owner defense, the lower court erred in entering an order forfeiting appellants' property because appellants did all that could reasonably be expected to prevent illegal use of their property where they reported illegal activities on their property to the police and warned bar patrons not to use drugs on the property. The court reversed the order forfeiting appellants' property.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order forfeiting appellant owners' property to the state, holding that under the innocent owner defense, it was error to enter an order forfeiting appellants' property where appellants did all that could reasonably be expected to prevent the illegal use of their property by reporting illegal activities on their property to the police and warning patrons not to use drugs on their property.

Starr v. Department of Environmental Resources


NO. 950 C.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


147 Pa. Commw. 196; 607 A.2d 321; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 300


December 16, 1991, Argued  
April 10, 1992, Decided  
April 10, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Commonwealth v. Blue Chip Transp. Co., 2012 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 953 (2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 87-203-W. State Agency, Environmental Hearing Board



DISPOSITION: The Board's order is therefore affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landowner sought review of a decision by the Environmental Hearing Board (Pennsylvania), which sustained the order of respondent department of environmental resources ordering petitioner, among other things, to get a permit to store tires on his property.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner landowner collected millions of discarded tires and never obtained a permit or approval from respondent department of environmental resources for the storage or disposal of tires on his property. Respondent issued a closure order, which petitioner appealed to the environmental hearing board (board). The board dismissed the appeal, and petitioner sought review of that decision. The court affirmed the board's decision, holding that the tires posed a fire danger and harbored mosquitoes and other insects, thus constituting a public health hazard. The court held that the tires were municipal waste, subject to regulation. The court found that the dictionary definition of "contain" clearly contemplated the keeping of the tires in discrete piles directly on the ground within the confines of petitioner's property. The court held that the majority of the tires remained at the site for over one year, triggering the presumption that the accumulation of tires fell within the statutory presumption of disposal under the Solid Waste Management Act, 35 P.S. § 6018.103. According to the court, petitioner failed to rebut the presumption by clear and convincing evidence to the contrary.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision that sustained an order directing petitioner landowner to stop accepting tires on his property and either to apply for a permit or submit a closure plan. The court held that the tires on petitioner's property were municipal waste and were subject to regulation.

Executive Life Ins. Co. v. Commonwealth


NO. 1466 C.D. 1985


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


147 Pa. Commw. 105; 606 A.2d 1282; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 280


February 5, 1992, Argued  
April 6, 1992, Decided  
April 6, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. Board Docket No. R-8794. State Agency, Board of Finance and Revenue


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurance company sought review of a decision of the Board of Finance and Revenue (Pennsylvania), in favor of respondent commonwealth, which sustained the settlements by the Department of Revenue of a retaliatory charge.


OVERVIEW: Appellant insurance company paid the retaliatory charge stated in a settlement but sought to recover it through administrative and judicial actions. Appellant claimed retaliatory charge computation should not have included annuity considerations, should have included all insurance companies doing business in Pennsylvania, and was violative of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and U.S. Const. art. 1, § 8. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision of the Board of Finance and Revenue, finding that the statute applied to insurance companies, not insurance products, so annuity considerations were properly included in the computation. The court also found that that statute was to be applied on an individual basis so it was possible that some companies would pay a charge and some would not. Finally, the court found that the statute was not violative of U.S. Const. amend XIV or U.S. Const. art. 1, § 8, where the statute's classifications were rationally related to the legitimate state purpose of equalizing and deterring burdens on interstate insurance companies and were freed from commerce clause restrictions by the McCarran-Ferguson Act, 15 U.S.C.S. § 1011.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the Board of Finance and Revenue, which sustained the settlement of a retaliatory charge, because the court found that the statute in question was correctly and constitutionally applied to appellant insurance company.

Baumgardner Oil Co. v. Commonwealth


No. 2038 C.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


146 Pa. Commw. 530; 606 A.2d 617; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 241


December 16, 1991, Argued  
March 24, 1992, Decided  
March 24, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied August 27, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From Nos. 663A of 1988 and 663B of 1988. Common Pleas Court of Franklin County. Judge JOHN W. KELLER, President Judge



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, we affirm the order of the trial court and remand this case for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner oil recyclers challenged an order from the Common Pleas Court, Franklin County (Pennsylvania), in a criminal action, where the trial court had denied an omnibus pretrial motion to quash or dismiss informations charging petitioners with violating the Solid Waste Management Act, 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6018.101 et seq., and the crimes code, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 101-9183.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner oil recyclers made an interlocutory appeal to orders of the trial court denying an omnibus pretrial motion seeking to dismiss or quash informations charging them with violating the Solid Waste Management Act (SWMA) 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6018.101 et seq., and the crimes code, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 101-9183. Petitioners had, allegedly, improperly blended solvent waste into reprocessed oil, which was determined to constitute illegal treatment and disposal of a hazardous waste. Petitioners contended that the informations failed to state offenses, SWMA was unconstitutionally vague, oil recycling had been pre-empted by federal law, selective prosecution, SWMA imposed unconstitutionally severe penalties for strict liability crimes, and SWMA violated the crimes code, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 305. The court affirmed. Used oil was a waste for purposes of the SWMA. The definitions of hazardous waste contained in the SWMA gave standards sufficient to allow an ordinary person to understand what conduct was proscribed. The existence of a federal regulatory or enforcement system did not imply the preemption of state law. No new crimes were created. The prosecution was not vindictive.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order from the trial court denying petitioner oil recyclers' pretrial motion to quash or dismiss informations charging petitioners with violating the Solid Waste Management Act (SWMA) and the crimes code, because used oil was a waste for purposes of the SWMA. The informations and the definitions of hazardous waste contained in the SWMA gave standards sufficient to allow understanding of what conduct was proscribed.

Pa. State Police v. Beer & Pop Warehouse


No. 700 C.D. 1991, No. 855 C.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


145 Pa. Commw. 355; 603 A.2d 284; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 111


November 15, 1991, Submitted on Briefs  
January 31, 1992, Decided  
January 31, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. S.A. 737-90. Common Pleas Court of Allegheny County. Judge ALAN S. PENKOWER



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bureau of liquor control challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) which reversed a decision of the Pennsylvania Liquor Control Board which had affirmed a determination of an administrative law judge imposing a $ 50 fine on appellee licensee for violation of 47 P.S. § 4-498(a). Appellee filed a precautionary cross-appeal.


OVERVIEW: The case began when, after an investigation, appellant bureau of liquor control issued a citation alleging that appellee licensee had violated 47 P.S. § 4-493(18). The administrative law judge concluded that because appellee had not displayed any advertisement for liquor on the outside of the licensed premises, there was no violation of § 4-493(18), but sua sponte found a violation of 47 P.S. § 4-498(a).On administrative appeal, the board rejected the constitutional arguments of appellee. Appellant filed a judicial appeal and asserted that it met its burden to demonstrate a violation of § 4-493(18) and that no due process violation occurred when the administrative law judge raised the § 4-498(a) violation sua sponte. The court held that the evidence did not as a matter of law demonstrate that appellee caused or permitted the violation of § 4-498(a). In order for appellee to have acquiesced a violation of the Liquor Code it would have needed at least to have been aware that the trailer used was in the lot. Therefore, the order of the trial court which reversed the board's imposition of the penalty was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the ruling of the trial court which reversed the board's imposition of a $ 50 fine. The court held that it was not shown that appellee licensee at any time displayed advertisements for liquor on the outside of its building. Appellee's precautionary cross-appeal was dismissed as moot.

Pa. State Troopers Ass'n v. Commonwealth


NO. 269 M.D. 1991, NO. 308 M.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


145 Pa. Commw. 291; 603 A.2d 253; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 105


December 18, 1991, Argued  
January 30, 1992, Decided  
January 30, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From ORIGINAL JURISDICTION


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth presented preliminary objections to two declaratory judgment actions brought by petitioners in the court's original jurisdiction to challenge the constitutionality of the State Employees' Retirement Code, 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5995.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners contended that the State Employees' Retirement Code, 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5995, violated Pa Const. art. III, § 31 by impermissibly repealing part of Act 111 of 1968, 43 P.S. §§ 217.1- 217.10 (Act 111). Petitioners argued that because the enactment of Act 111 was constitutionally mandated, its repeal or amendment would be unconstitutional. The court dismissed the petitions for review because the court found that petitioners failed to state a cause of action upon which relief could have been granted. Act 111 specifically included pensions within its list of terms and conditions of employment subject to collective bargaining. The court found that § 5995 was an amendment to Act 111, and Act 111 merely permitted, but did not require the Pennsylvania General Assembly to enact Act 111. As such, the General Assembly was constitutionally authorized to amend Act 111 by later limiting petitioners' statutory collective bargaining rights. The court further found that § 5955 was enacted later than Act 111, and it necessarily prevailed over any inconsistent provisions of Act 111.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the commonwealth's preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer for failure of petitioners to state a cause of action upon which relief could be granted, and dismissed the petitions for review, because the general assembly was not constitutionally prohibited from amending the statute, and petitioners' argument failed.

Kraushaar v. Wayne County Bd. of Assessment & Revision of Taxes


NO. 2410 C.D. 1990


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


145 Pa. Commw. 314; 603 A.2d 264; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 104


June 13, 1991, Argued  
January 30, 1992, Decided  
January 30, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied August 24, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. 111-1991-Civil. Common Pleas Court of Wayne County. President Judge Robert C. Conway


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In 26 consolidated cases, appellant developers sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Wayne County (Pennsylvania) that affirmed the decision of appellee, County Board of Assessment and Revision of Taxes, that imposed tax assessments upon 26 lots in a plan of subdivision owned by appellants.


OVERVIEW: Appellant developers subdivided a large acreage of real property into 27 separate lots for a proposed residential development. Appellants sold one of the lots and paved a cul-de-sac for access to six of the lots. Appellee, County Board of Assessment and Revision of Taxes, raised the assessment on the property based upon the subdivision, sale, and improvements. Appellee, upon a requested review by appellants, reduced the new valuations but not to the level assessed before the subdivision. The court of common pleas dismissed appellants' petition for appeal and affirmed appellee's assessment. The court affirmed the decision of the court of common pleas. The court held that appellee had authority to reassess all of the subdivided lots under Pa. Stat. Ann. § 5453.602a and the reassessment did not apply only when a lot was sold. The court held that once there had been a change in the condition of the property, such as a sale or improvement on a portion of it, appellee should allow reassessment of each new lot. The court held that this interpretation of the assessment statutes comported with Pa. Const. art. IX, §1, which required uniformity of taxes for the same class of subjects.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court of common pleas that had affirmed the property reassessment imposed by appellee, County Board of Assessment and Revision of Taxes. Appellee had authority to reassess property taxes on all of appellant developers' subdivided lots when one lot had been sold and improvements had been made on some of the other lots.

Glendon v. Dep't of Envtl. Res.


No. 18 C.D. 1991, No. 472 C.D. 1991, No. 517 C.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


145 Pa. Commw. 238; 603 A.2d 226; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 97


September 10, 1991, Argued  
January 28, 1992, Decided  
January 28, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied June 16, 1992. Reconsideration Denied January 14, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From Nos. 90-100-F and 90-104-F. State Agency Environmental Hearing Board


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, borough and energy company, sought review of orders issued by the Environmental Hearing Board (Pennsylvania), which denied petitioner borough's motion to intervene, dismissed petitioner energy company's appeal, and granted petitioner energy company's motion to dismiss one of petitioner borough's objections. The action involved a permit issued by respondent, Pennsylvania Department of Energy Resources, pursuant to 53 P.S. § 4001.511.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner energy company was given a permit by respondent, Department of Energy Resources, to build a solid waste facility in petitioner borough's community on the condition that a neighboring city agree to a waiver of the 53 P.S. § 4000.511 prohibition against such a facility being constructed within 300 yards of a park. Petitioner borough contended that the permit should not have been issued prior to the grant of the waiver. The agency's grant of petitioner energy company's motion to dismiss this objection was reversed. The court held that petitioner borough's interest in protecting its environment justified its intervention and challenge. Thus, petitioner energy company's motion to quash the petition for review was denied. Petitioner energy company was required to comply with 53 P.S. § 4000.511(a) permit requirements because it was a "new" facility. Although petitioner energy company experienced hardship as a result, the application of § 4000.511(a) was not unconstitutional. Petitioner energy company lacked the standing to challenge the waiver in 53 P.S. § 4000.511(d) because it could not be adversely affected by the provision. Finally, the court invalidated the permit.


OUTCOME: The order which denied petitioner borough's motion to intervene was reversed because it had a substantial interest in protecting its environment. The order which dismissed petitioner energy company's appeal was sustained because the application of the permit requirements was constitutional. The order which granted petitioner energy company's motion to dismiss one of petitioner borough's objections was reversed and the permit was declared invalid.

In re King Properties


No. 304 C.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


145 Pa. Commw. 139; 602 A.2d 486; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 88


September 13, 1991, Argued  
January 22, 1992, Decided  
January 22, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted January 13, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. CC-105-89. Common Pleas Court of Adams County. Judge OSCAR F. SPICER, President Judge


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth appealed an interlocutory order of the Common Pleas Court of Adams County (Pennsylvania), permitting defendant to redeem his interest in his house that had been forfeited, under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6801(a)(6)(i)(C), pursuant to defendant's conviction for possession of cocaine with intent to deliver.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to defendant's conviction for possession of cocaine with intent to deliver, the trial court ordered forfeiture of defendant's house pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6801(a)(6)(i)(C), concluding that it had been used to violate the law. The commonwealth challenged the trial court order granting defendant the right to redeem his property. The court found that defendant's motion to quash was without merit, for the redemption order was interlocutory and the commonwealth was not required to appeal until the court had resolved all issues. The court affirmed the forfeiture, but reversed the order granting defendant a right of redemption because an exercise of equitable powers was improper where the legislature provided a mandatory and exclusive statutory remedy and defendant failed to pursue it. The court held that the forfeiture statute did not exempt personal residences or provide an opportunity to redeem, and that forfeiture without redemption did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment, because although the nature of defendant's violation was de minimis, the statutory requirements were met.


OUTCOME: Order of the trial court forfeiting defendant's house pursuant to his conviction for possession of cocaine with intent to deliver was affirmed, but the part of the order granting defendant a right to redeem his property was reversed, because the forfeiture statute provided an exclusive remedy of which defendant did not avail himself, and it did not provide for redemption as an equitable remedy for a de minimis violation.

Garvey v. Rosanelli


No. 1263 C.D. 1990


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


144 Pa. Commw. 588; 601 A.2d 1334; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 46


December 4, 1990, Argued  
January 8, 1992, Decided  
January 8, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied September 24, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. 84-09044. Common Pleas Court of Bucks County. Judge MICHAEL J. KANE


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff homeowners appealed from an order of the Common Pleas Court of Bucks County (Pennsylvania) that granted the motion of defendant township for judgment on the pleadings in a civil action for damages involving alleged defects in the construction of a house. The trial court entered judgment on the pleadings on the basis of governmental immunity and the statute of limitations.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff homeowners filed an action against defendant township for damages relating to the construction of their home. The trial court granted defendant's motion for judgment on the pleadings on the basis that the action against defendant was barred by governmental immunity under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8541, and that the cause of action was time barred by the two-year statute of limitations under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5524. Plaintiffs appealed and the court reversed. The court held that the alleged negligent acts occurred during a gap between the judicial abrogation of governmental immunity and before its legislative reinstatement; therefore, there was no governmental immunity in existence at the time that the alleged negligent acts occurred. The court held that the discovery rule, which provided that the statute of limitations did not begin to run until plaintiffs discovered or, in the exercise of due diligence, should have discovered their injuries, applied to latent defects in residential construction cases. The court held that the ultimate resolution of whether plaintiffs' delay in discovering the defect was reasonable was a question for the jury.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that granted the motion of defendant township for judgment on the pleadings. The court held that the governmental immunity claimed by defendant had not been in effect at the time the negligent acts of which it was accused had occurred. The court held that there was a question of fact as to the issue of the statute of limitations, which was appropriate for a jury.

Lawrence G. Spielvogel, Inc. v. Cheltenham


No. 1572 C.D. 1990


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


144 Pa. Commw. 510; 601 A.2d 1310; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 38


March 5, 1991, Argued  
January 7, 1992, Decided  
January 7, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 87-03963. Common Pleas Court of Montgomery County. Judge WILLIAM H. YOHN, JR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review of a judgment of the Common Pleas Court of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania) which awarded appellee township the amount of business privilege taxes not paid by appellant pursuant to Cheltenham, Pa., Ordinance No. 1400 (1976).


OVERVIEW: Appellant corporation failed to pay business privilege taxes assessed by appellee township by Cheltenham, Pa., Ordinance No. 1400 (1976). The trial court awarded to appellee the delinquent amount, and the court affirmed this award. Appellant contended that the business privilege tax violated U.S. Const. art. I, § 8 by taxing gross receipts derived from interstate services. The court disagreed, holding that the tax was not a direct tax on interstate commerce, but a tax upon the privilege of maintaining a business office within appellee township. The trial court correctly placed the burden of proof on appellant to show that the interstate portion of its revenues was exempt from the business privilege tax. The court also held that appellee's estimation of appellant's taxes was proper under the Local Tax Enabling Act, 53 P.S. § 6902, because appellant refused to submit to an audit to determine the amount due. The court prevented appellant from raising the contention that appellee miscalculated the amount of the tax owed by appellant because appellant made no objection to the calculation at trial.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment awarding appellee township the amount of business privilege taxes not paid by appellant corporation was affirmed. The tax was properly estimated when appellant refused to submit to an audit. The business privilege tax did not violate the Commerce Clause of the U.S. Constitution because it taxed the privilege of maintaining a business within a township, rather than the revenues from any interstate commerce.

Gray v. Barnhart


No. 773 C.D. 1991


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


144 Pa. Commw. 474; 601 A.2d 924; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 17


October 8, 1991, Argued  
January 6, 1992, Decided  
January 6, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal and/or Cross Petition Denied November 17, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 2846 of 1990. Common Pleas Court of Westmoreland County. Judge CHARLES H. LOUGHRAN



DISPOSITION: Vacated and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of an order from the Common Pleas Court of Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania), which granted summary judgment in favor of appellees, the owners of a shooting range, in appellants' action for nuisance. The trial court found that, because there were no noise control ordinances in existence at the time the range was constructed, 35 Pa. Stat. §§ 4501-02 required that appellees' motion for summary judgment be granted.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, owners of land adjacent to a shooting range owned by appellees, brought an action to enjoin the use of the property and alleged that it constituted a nuisance in fact because of the noise generated. Appellees filed a motion for summary judgment and argued that 35 Pa. Stat. §§ 4501-02 provided them with statutory immunity from nuisance actions. The trial court granted the motion for summary judgment, finding that there were no noise ordinance regulations in place when the shooting range was constructed. On appeal, the court vacated the order and remanded the case for trial, holding that the statutory immunity only applied when there was a noise regulation in effect when the range was constructed and the range was at that time in compliance with the regulation. The court held that because there was no such regulation in place when the range was constructed, a triable issue of fact existed as to whether there was a nuisance.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the summary judgment, holding that the immunity afforded by the legislation applied only where a noise control ordinance was in effect at the time the shooting range was constructed and the range was in compliance with the provisions of that ordinance. The court held that because no noise control ordinance in effect when appellees' range was constructed, the question of whether such use was a nuisance had to be litigated.

M.P. v. Commonwealth, State Ethics Com.


NO. 46 M.D. 1989


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


144 Pa. Commw. 429; 601 A.2d 902; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 9


October 11, 1991, Argued  
January 3, 1992, Decided  
January 3, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From ORIGINAL JURISDICTION


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner elected official moved for summary judgment on his petition for declaratory judgment against respondent State Ethics Commission in respondent's investigation against petitioner.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner elected official moved for summary judgment on his petition for declaratory judgment against respondent State Ethics Commission in respondent's investigation against petitioner. Respondent issued petitioner a preliminary order outlining its case three days before termination of respondent under the Sunset Act, 71 P.S. §§ 1795.1-1795.14. Petitioner argued that respondent unlawfully continued its investigation of him during the Sunset period and that summary judgment was proper. Respondent argued that it had completed its investigation before Sunset and that nothing substantial occurred during the Sunset period. Respondent argued that the due process hearing after the termination date was merely a formality and not a significant act. The court granted petitioner's motion for summary judgment and held that the language of § 1795.6(f) of the Sunset Act was unequivocal as to termination. The court held that no functions or proceedings could be revived after the definitive declarations under § 1795.6(f) of respondent's termination and nonexistence. The court held that § 1795.6(f) permitted no interpretation other than to declare respondent terminated at Sunset time.


OUTCOME: Petitioner elected official's motion for summary judgment was granted because respondent State Ethics Commission was terminated on the date specified in the Sunset Act, and could not continue its investigation against petitioner after that date.

Philadelphia Suburban Corp. v. Commonwealth


No. 1423 C.D. 1988


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


144 Pa. Commw. 410; 601 A.2d 893; 1992 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 10


June 13, 1991, Argued  
January 3, 1992, Decided  
January 3, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. R-11,531. State Agency Board of Finance and Revenue



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, we reverse the decision of the Board of Finance and Revenue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner corporation appealed a decision by the Pennsylvania Board of Finance and Revenue which refused its petition for review of its petition for resettlement of its capital stock tax return which respondent Department of Revenue re-computed to show that petitioner owed a higher tax than petitioner had calculated.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner corporation filed its commonwealth capital stock tax return and reported a capital stock value. Respondent state department of revenue re-computed taxpayer's capital stock tax to be higher. After the petition for resettlement was refused by the Board of Appeals and the Board of Finance and Revenue, petitioner appealed. The court reversed, holding it could not ignore the explicit language of the code which mandated inclusion only of the "income per books" actually set forth on the investor's federal income tax return without inclusion of dividend income not actually reported as "income per books." That statutory definition of "average net income" fully comported with sound and generally accepted accounting principles. 61 Pa. Code § 155.26(b) of respondent's regulations substantially altered, and was inconsistent with, its statutory counterpart, 72 P.S. § 7601, was in derogation of the code and at odds with the legislative intent. The regulation was declared invalid to the extent it adjusted the "income per books" of a taxpayer using the equity method to account for dividends received from investee corporations.


OUTCOME: Decision of Board of Finance and Revenue increasing the amount of income tax owed by petitioner corporation reversed on the grounds that respondent state Department of Revenue's regulations substantially altered and was inconsistent with the unambiguous statutory definition of "average net income."

Holden v. Kay


No. 638 C.D. 1991


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


144 Pa. Commw. 254; 601 A.2d 453; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 687


October 8, 1991, Argued  
December 20, 1991, Decided  
December 20, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. 102 - 1988. Common Pleas Court of Lawrence County. Judge J. QUINT SALMON, Senior Judge


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged an order from the Common Pleas Court of Lawrence County (Pennsylvania), which made final a decree nisi granting appellees' petition for specific performance of a contract for the sale of land.


OVERVIEW: Appellees sold a parcel of land to appellants and leased back a smaller portion of the land sold. The lease contained an option for appellees' granddaughter to buy the leased portion of the parcel. She exercised the option and assigned it to appellees. Appellants refused to convey the leased portion, arguing that under the Municipal Planning Code and a Township Ordinance the subdivision had to be approved by authorities. Appellees filed a petition for specific performance. The trial court granted the petition, stating that the statute and the ordinance could only restrict the use of the land, not its alienablity. Appellants challenged, arguing that the order compelled them to perform an illegal transaction. Because evidence was excluded on the issue of the viability of subdividing the property, the final decree was vacated and remanded for a determination of whether it was possible to subdivide the parcel as contemplated in the lease and purchase option. If subdivision was not possible, the trial court erred when it ordered specific performance of the sales contract. If subdivision was possible, then appellants could not avoid their contractual obligations by refusing to subdivide.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court's order of specific performance and remanded for a determination of whether it was possible to subdivide the parcel as contemplated in the lease. If subdivision was not possible, then the trial court erred when it ordered specific performance of the sales contract. However, if subdivision was possible, then appellants would not be permitted to avoid their contractual obligations by refusing to subdivide.

 

708 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Board of Supervisors v. Laurelwood Constr. Co.


No. 290 C.D. 1991


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


144 Pa. Commw. 63; 600 A.2d 690; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 652


September 13, 1991, Argued  
December 5, 1991, Decided  
December 5, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied April 30, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. 88-01278. Common Pleas Court of Chester County. Judge C. CURTIS JOYNER


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant township board of supervisors sought review of the decision of the Common Pleas Court of Chester County (Pennsylvania), that granted summary judgment for appellee developer and directed the township to reimburse a fee assessed under North Coventry Pa., Township Ordinance § 515(D)(8). Appellant contended that appellee's action for declaratory judgment was not a proper vehicle by which to challenge the ordinance's validity.


OVERVIEW: Appellant township board of supervisors sought review of the decision that granted summary judgment for appellee developer and directed the township to reimburse appellee a fee assessed under North Coventry Pa., Township Ordinance § 515(D)(8) in lieu of a dedication of green space to the township. The court held that the trial court erred when it allowed appellee to proceed with its declaratory judgment action under 53 P.S. § 11001. The court held that appellee failed to pursue the remedial route mandated by 53 P.S. § 11004, which was by way of a curative amendment proceeding before appellant. Hence, appellee's challenge before the trial court was improper because the it was based solely on the validity of the ordinance. Appellee's failure to follow the remedial procedure outlined in § 11004 rendered the declaratory judgment action an unsuitable vehicle by which to challenge the validity of the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The decision that granted summary judgment in favor of appellee developer was reversed and the case was dismissed. Appellant township supervisors demonstrated that declaratory judgment was not a proper vehicle by which to challenge the ordinance's validity because a specific remedy was provided by statute.

Human Dev. of Erie, Inc. v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


No. 1735 C.D. 1989, No. 1800 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


143 Pa. Commw. 675; 600 A.2d 658; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 654


May 1, 1991, Argued  
December 3, 1991, Decided  
December 3, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Application for Reargument Denied January 27, 1992, Reported at 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 109.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. 3313 - A - 1988. Common Pleas Court of Erie County. Judge George Levin


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellees, disability service providers and disabled residents, challenged the order from the Common Pleas Court of Erie County (Pennsylvania), which denied their request for attorney's fees and costs in their zoning appeal against appellant town zoning board. Appellant initially challenged the trial court's order, which reversed appellant and found the town's group home ordinance unconstitutional. Appellant then abandoned its appeal.


OVERVIEW: Millcreek Township adopted an ordinance excluding certain types of group disability homes. Appellees, disability service providers and disabled residents, sought review with appellee town zoning board, challenging the constitutionality of the ordinance. Appellant rejected appellees' claims. The trial court reversed, finding a violation of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and the Fair Housing Act, 42 U.S.C.S. § 3604(f). Appellant sought review of the merits but abandoned its appeal. Appellees filed a cross-action for the trial court's denial of attorney's fees and costs pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988 and 42 U.S.C.S. § 3613(c) as prevailing parties. The appellate court affirmed. The court held that under the latter two statutes, attorney fees were not available to an administrative appeal from a zoning board decision. Such a challenge was not a "lawsuit." The Rules of Civil Procedure did not apply. Section 1988 fees could only be awarded in a lawsuit initiated by a complaint where a jury could be impaneled, and damages awarded.


OUTCOME: Order from the trial court denying attorney's fees to appellees, disability service providers and disabled residents, in their successful challenge to appellant town zoning board was affirmed. A zoning appeal was not a lawsuit but rather an administrative appeal, and appellees thus were not prevailing civil rights litigants in a civil action who would have been eligible for attorney's fees.

Commonwealth, DOT, Bureau of Driver Licensing v. Thomas


No. 84 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


143 Pa. Commw. 531; 600 A.2d 237; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 630; Bankr. L. Rep. (CCH) P74,348


June 28, 1990, Submitted on Briefs  
November 22, 1991, Decided  
November 22, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied April 14, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. 89-4524; Common Pleas Court of Washington County; John F. Bell, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, Pennsylvania Department of Transportation, Bureau of Driver Licensing, sought review of the order from the Court of Common Pleas, Washington County (Pennsylvania), which held that 11 U.S.C.S. § 525 prevented the suspension of appellee driver's operating privilege.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, Pennsylvania Department of Transportation, Bureau of Driver Licensing, suspended appellee driver's operating license for violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1785. Appellee challenged the suspension asserting that it would have frustrated his Chapter 13 bankruptcy plan as he depended on his driver's license for employment, and the trial court held that 11 U.S.C.S. § 525 prevented the suspension of appellee's license. On appeal, the court held that motor vehicle financial responsibility statutes were not discriminatory per se because state law might not make a discriminatory distinction based exclusively on the existence of a debt in bankruptcy, and they were valid as long as the law applied to all motorists regardless of their financial status. The court held that suspension was mandatory for a violation of § 1785 even where unfairness to appellee or inequity might have resulted. The court reversed the order of the trial court, finding that § 1785 did not violate the anti-discrimination provisions of the Bankruptcy Code, 11 U.S.C.S. §§ 1301-1330.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order from the trial court that held that the Bankruptcy Code prevented the suspension of appellee driver's operating privilege. The court held that suspension of appellee's license was mandatory under the motor vehicle financial responsibility statute, and that the statute was not discriminatorily based on the existence of appellee's debt in bankruptcy.

 

711 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Beistle Co. v. Commonwealth


NO. 3371 C.D. 1985, NO. 3372 C.D. 1985, NO. 3373 C.D. 1985


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


163 Pa. Commw. 1; 640 A.2d 483; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 728


December 6, 1990, ARGUED  
October 24, 1991, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Memorandum Opinion Redesignated Opinion and Ordered Published March 30, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALED From No. C-15103, C-15104 and C-15105


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner corporation appealed orders of the Pennsylvania Board of Finance and Revenue, which refused petitions for the refund of corporate net income tax for certain tax years based on the untimely filing of the claims.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner corporation had its headquarters and factory in Pennsylvania, but maintained an office and showroom in New York. Its New York employees solicited and accepted orders. Petitioner sought a refund of the tax paid to the commonwealth for certain years on the basis that much of its income had been made out of state. Petitioner failed previously to inform the Pennsylvania Department of Revenue that it conducted business in New York because it did not believe that it was subject to taxation there and because it anticipated that the "throw out" rule would be used in apportioning its income. Petitioner thereafter sought application of the five-year limitation period in 72 P.S. § 503(a)(4) for filing a claim refund. The Pennsylvania Board of Finance and Review refused the petitions for refund. The court affirmed. First, the cases relied upon by petitioner could not trigger the five-year limitation period because they did not involve an interpretation of the statute under which petitioner paid its corporate income tax to Pennsylvania. Second, petitioner should have known that it was subject to taxation in New York. Reliance on the "throw-out" rule could not save petitioner.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed orders of the Pennsylvania Board of Finance and Revenue, which refused petitions for the refund of corporate net income tax for certain tax years based on the untimely filing of the claims. Petitioner corporation failed in its burden of proving that it was entitled to rely on a five-year limitation period for filing a claim refund on the basis that much of its income had been made out of state.

Reeves v. Pennsylvania Game Com.


No. 280 C.D. 1991


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


143 Pa. Commw. 76; 598 A.2d 605; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 560

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appealed From Docket No. P. H. 89-101; Pennsylvania Game Commission.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner vehicle owner challenged the order by the Pennsylvania Game Commission, which denied his application for award of reasonable attorneys fees and expenses incurred in the retrieval of his vehicle after a determination by the court that the forfeiture of his vehicle was unconstitutional for assisting in the shooting of a deer under the Game and Wildlife Code, 34 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2310(a)(2).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner vehicle owner was arrested for assisting in the unlawful shooting of a deer and after he admitted guilt, his vehicle was seized under 34 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 2310(d), 23102. After a hearing on petitioner's request and appeal to the court, it was determined the confiscation was unconstitutional as it violated due process and petitioner's vehicle was ordered returned. OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of petitioner vehicle owner's application for attorneys fees and expenses incurred in the retrieval of his car confiscated by respondent Pennsylvania Game Commission. The court found that there was not an adjudication; therefore, petitioner was not entitled to attorneys fees and expenses.

Hill v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


No. 1509 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


142 Pa. Commw. 539; 597 A.2d 1245; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 531


April 2, 1991, Argued  
September 27, 1991, Decided  
September 27, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appealed from State Agency, Common Pleas Court of Berks County; No. 294 December Term, 1987; Judge Thomas J. Eshelman and Frederick Edenharter, Senior Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Berks County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed their appeal from the determination of appellee Zoning Hearing Board of Maxatawny Township (the board). The board found that their trash transfer station constituted an expansion of a pre-existing, non-conforming use and allowed such operation with certain conditions.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landowners operated a sanitation collection service on property, which was a non-conforming use. At the start, they hauled trash directly to the landfills and used the property for equipment storage. Thereafter, they found it necessary to store trash in their building until it could be transferred to landfills. Appellants received a permit from the Department of Environmental Resources (DER) to operate a trash transfer station. Appellee Zoning Hearing Board (the board) found that the trash transfer station was a permissible reasonable expansion of the pre-existing, non-conforming use as a sanitation collection service and imposed conditions to the continued operation of it. The trial court affirmed. The court affirmed and held that the board's findings were sufficient to support its conclusion that the trash transfer station was an expansion of the prior non-conforming use, in accordance with the Municipalities Planning Code, 53 P.S. § 10908(9). The court also affirmed certain conditions imposed to the transfer station such as hours of operation. However, it reversed other conditions and held that the board wrongfully restricted the type of waste to be processed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant landowners' appeal because the approval of the trash transfer station constituted an expansion of a pre-existing non-conforming use. The court affirmed certain conditions ordered as to the operation of such facility and reversed others. The court affirmed that appellee Zoning Hearing Board of Maxatawny Township did not unconstitutionally exclude the use of land for a trash transfer station.

Board of Supervisors v. McClimans


No. 1219 C. D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


142 Pa. Commw. 470; 597 A.2d 738; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 526

September 25, 1991, Decided  
September 25, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appealed From No. 118 of 1985, M.D.; State Agency; Common Pleas Court of Lawrence County; Judge Glenn McCracken, Jr., P.J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant board of supervisors requested review from an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lawrence County (Pennsylvania) holding that a zoning ordinance effected a taking of appellee miners' property without just compensation.


OVERVIEW: The trial court concluded that a zoning ordinance took appellee miners' mineral estate without just compensation because the property was zoned residential, and appellees could not gain access to their subsurface property. Appellant board of supervisors contended that the trial court applied the wrong evidentiary standard, that state and federal regulations superseded the ordinance, and that the ordinance did not prevent appellees from reaching their coal by underground mining. Appellees intended to strip mine the coal. Stat. tit. 52 §§ 13961-1396.31 did not supersede the zoning ordinances, and that the trial court conclusion that appellees' expert established the infeasibility of reaching the underground coal was proper.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment concluding that a zoning ordinance effected a taking of appellant miners' mineral estate. The trial court properly applied a preponderance of the evidence standard. The surface mining conservation and reclamation act did not supersede the zoning ordinances, Finally, the trial court's conclusion that appellees' expert established the infeasibility of reaching the underground coal was proper.

No. 1931 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


142 Pa. Commw. 353; 597 A.2d 271; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 506

September 9, 1991, Decided  
September 9, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted March 23, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. Docket No. 24-88-277; Wesbury United Methodist Community; State Agency Department of Public Welfare, Office of Hearings and Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the decision of the Office of Hearings and Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner nursing home sought review of an order of the Office of Hearings and Appeals (Pennsylvania), that adopted the recommendation of the examiner that respondent department of public welfare did not err by raising the reimbursement ceiling for nursing homes on April 1, 1988 rather than on January 1, 1988, on the ground that the inclusion of such a date in the 1987 Appropriations Act, 1987 P.L. 459, did not constitute substantive language.


OVERVIEW: After the governor signed the General Appropriation Act of 1987, P.L. 459, the budget office concluded sufficient funds did not exist to increase the net operating rate ceilings as of January 1, 1988 and advised that the increase could not start until April 1, 1988. The legislature amended the act and included a deficiency appropriation. The governor signed the supplemental act but instructed the budget secretary to disregard the conditional language dealing with the effective date. He based his decision on a conviction that the starting date was substantive language, which Pa. Const. art. 3, § 11prohibited in a general appropriation bill. Respondent department of public works issued an interim rate notice advising petitioner nursing home that the increase in net operating cost ceilings was retroactive to April 1, 1988. Petitioner filed an appeal with the Office of Hearing and Appeals contending the revised rates should have been retroactive to January 1, 1988. The examiner denied the petition. Petitioner argued the inclusion of the date did not constitute substantive language. The appellate court affirmed and held that the starting date in the act was substantive.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision and held that the adoption by respondent department of public works of the April 1, 1988 beginning date for the changed ceiling was effective under the authority of the Public Welfare Code. The court ruled that the office of hearing and appeals reached the right result by concluding that the January 1, 1988 starting date was substantive and was a nullity in an appropriation act under the constitution.

Liberty Mut. Fire Ins. Co. v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Ins.

L. 11, at 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1799.7. Petitioners contended that respondents applied incorrect ratemaking standards to petitioners' relief requests.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner insurers filed requests for extraordinary circumstances relief from private passenger automobile insurance rate reductions mandated by the Act of February 7, 1990, Pub. L. 11, at 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1799.7(b)(3). Petitioners' relief requests were denied by respondent insurance department, and by respondent insurance commissioner after a hearing.
