Boettger v. Miklich


No. 1094 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


142 Pa. Commw. 136; 599 A.2d 713; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 478


April 2, 1991, Argued  
August 22, 1991, Decided  
August 22, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied October 4, 1991. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted April 23, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. 1984-C-7831; Common Pleas Court of Northampton County; Judge Robert A. Freedberg.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, commonwealth and commissioner of the state police, sought review of the Common Pleas Court of Northampton County (Pennsylvania), which entered a judgment n.o.v for appellee citizen in appellee's action under the Wiretapping and Electronic Surveillance Control Act, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5701-5781.


OVERVIEW: A state trooper provided agents of the Internal Revenue Service (IRS) and the Pennsylvania Department of Revenue (PDR) with transcripts of appellee citizen's telephone conversations intercepted under the Wiretapping and Electronic Surveillance Control Act (act), 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5701-5781. Jeopardy tax assessments were filed against appellee. Pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5725, appellee brought an action in which he alleged that appellants, commonwealth and state police commissioner, violated the act when they disclosed the intercepted conversations to the tax authorities. The trial court granted appellee's motion for judgment n.o.v., and appellants sought review. The court reversed. The court stated that the trial court erred when it held that appellants were not afforded a good faith defense. The issue of a good faith defense depended upon whether the trooper violated a clearly established statutory or constitutional right of which a reasonable person would have known. The limits to which disclosure might be made under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5717(a) were at best unclear when the trooper transferred the intercepted information to the IRS and PDR.


OUTCOME: The judgment n.o.v. for appellants, commonwealth and commissioner of the state police, in appellee citizen's action under the Wiretapping and Electronic Surveillance Control Act was reversed because the trial court erred when it held that appellants were not afforded a good faith defense.

Subscribers of Erie Ins. Exchange v. Insurance Dep't of Commonwealth


No. 2266 C. D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 464


December 5, 1990, Argued  
August 15, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal Granted August 15, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] Appealed From No. R90-06-21; State Agency Insurance Commissioner of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner insurer sought review of a decision of respondent Insurance Department of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, which was affirmed by respondent insurance department commissioner, disapproving its extraordinary circumstances (except for underinsured motorist coverage) filing pursuant to the Act of February 7, 1990, P.L. 11 (Act 6), 75 Pa. Con. Stat. § 1799.7(b)(3).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner insurer submitted a filing for extraordinary circumstances pursuant to the Act of February 7, 1990, P.L. 11 (Act 6), 75 Pa. Con. Stat. § 1799.7 (b)(3), as to why it increased its rates. Respondent Insurance Department of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania disapproved of this filing, which decision was overruled in a limited fashion by respondent insurance department commissioner. The court vacated and remanded respondents' decision, finding that the Casualty and Surety Rate Regulatory Act, Act of June 11, 1947, P.L. 538, as amended, 40 P.S. §§ 1181-1199, applied to extraordinary circumstances filings. The court found no indication that petitioner faced administrative bias simply by respondent commissioner's statement that he believed that an insurer earning a 12 percent rate of return had not demonstrated extraordinary circumstances. The court also found that prosecutorial and adjudicatory roles cannot commingle in a rate-making matter because there had been no prosecution, as there was no allegation petitioner had violated a law or regulation.


OUTCOME: The court vacated and remanded respondent insurance department commissioner's decision for further consideration and proceedings, finding that the statute applied to extraordinary circumstances filings. The court found no indications of bias, nor did it find that petitioner insurer's due process rights had been violated by a commingling of prosecutorial and adjudicative functions, declaring that such commingling did not happen in rate cases.

Slick v. Bowie


Nos. 2603 C.D. 1990, 2718 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


141 Pa. Commw. 500; 596 A.2d 282; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 425


June 13, 1991, Argued  
July 29, 1991, Decided  
July 29, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied December 13, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. 90-11970; Common Pleas Court of Delaware County; Harry J. Bradley, Judge.
Bowie v. Slick, 9 Pa. D. & C.4th 91, 1991 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 344 (1991)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, superintendent of school district and members of the school board, challenged the decision of the Common Pleas Court of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which entered a peremptory judgment against appellants and enjoined them from levying school taxes.


OVERVIEW: Appellee taxpayer filed an action for mandamus to enjoin appellants, superintendent of school district and members of the school board, from collecting school taxes because appellees allegedly violated the Public School Code, 24 P.S. § 6-672 by including fringe benefits for teachers and the supervisory staff into the salary exception. The trial court granted the motion for peremptory judgment, ordered appellees to refrain from collecting school taxes, and refused to open the peremptory judgment. On appeal, the court reversed because fringe benefits were included in the salary exception in § 6-672. The court determined that fringe benefits were treated as wages to employees. Because salary and wages were substantially the same, the court concluded that salary under § 6-672 included payment for fringe benefits. The court's interpretation was supported by 24 P.S. § 5-513, which authorized school districts to provide insurance and pension benefits to their employees and to deduct the cost of benefit payments from the employees' salary.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment that entered a peremptory judgment against appellants, superintendent of school district and members of the school board, and enjoined them from levying school taxes because fringe benefits were included in the salary exception of the Public School Code.

Meredith v. Commonwealth, DOT


No. 2209 C.D. 1990

July 26, 1991, Decided  
July 26, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appealed From No. 1990 - 1054; Common Pleas Court of Cambria County; Judge F. Joseph Leahey.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, we affirm.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant licensee challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Cambria County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed appellee department of transportation's suspension of appellant's motor vehicle operating privileges after he was convicted for the second time of violating 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6308, which prohibited anyone under 21 years of age from purchasing, possessing, transporting, or consuming alcoholic beverages.


OVERVIEW: Appellant licensee's driving privileges were suspended by appellee department of transportation, pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6310.4, after appellant was convicted for the second time of violating 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6308, which prohibited anyone under 21 years of age from purchasing, possessing, transporting, or consuming alcoholic beverages. The trial court affirmed appellee's decision. Appellant challenged his suspension, and argued that § 6310.4 was unconstitutional. The court held that its review was limited in scope to a determination of whether appellant was in fact convicted and whether appellee had acted in accordance with the applicable law. Additionally, the court held that appellant was not permitted to collaterally attack his criminal conviction in the appeal of his license suspension. OUTCOME: The order of the trial court that affirmed appellee department of transportation's suspension of appellant licensee's driving privileges was affirmed because appellant could not collaterally attack his underlying criminal conviction by challenging his license suspension.

 

721 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Calabris v. Workmen's Compensation Appeal Bd. (American General Cos.)


No. 1932 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


141 Pa. Commw. 405; 595 A.2d 765; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 414


May 2, 1991, Argued  
July 24, 1991, Decided  
July 24, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appealed from No. A-96888; State Agency Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant employee sought review of the order of respondent State Agency Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board (Pennsylvania), which denied claimant disability benefits.


OVERVIEW: Claimant employee was discharged after an audit was performed in connection with the dismissal of a subordinate for misappropriating funds. Claimant filed a petition that alleged his depression was a work-related psychological injury, and respondent State Agency Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board dismissed claimant's petition. The court affirmed, and found that claimant failed to objectively prove an abnormal working condition. The audit did not increase claimant's responsibilities or work load. His injury resulted from his subjective reaction to the audit. The treating physician's testimony was incompetent to prove a causal connection between the injury and the audit because the physician did not have a complete grasp on claimant's work situation. The additional, substantive burden of proving an abnormal work condition was reasonable to prevent fraudulent psychiatric injury claims based on normal work conditions and did not violate claimant's equal protection and due process rights. Because the burden of proof was a rule to guide respondent and did not mandate nor prohibit any behavior, the vagueness doctrine did not apply.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of respondent State Agency Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board that denied claimant employee disability benefits because claimant failed to objectively prove that the audit at his work caused his depression. Claimant's depression resulted from his subjective reaction to the audit, and his doctor's testimony failed to show a causal connection.

 

722 of 892 DOCUMENTS


National Properties, Inc. v. Macungie


No. 2233 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


141 Pa. Commw. 342; 595 A.2d 742; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 405


June 10, 1991, Argued  
July 19, 1991, Decided  
July 19, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appealed from No. 87-E-105; Common Pleas Court of Lehigh County; Judge Carol K. McGinley.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant apartment complex owner sought review of a decision of the Common Pleas Court of Lehigh County, which rendered judgment for respondent borough in appellant's action to have respondent's refuse collection ordinance declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant apartment complex owner requested an exclusion for its 174 unit complex from appellee borough's garbage ordinance, which required collection by appellee's contractor. Appellee refused to grant exclusion and appellant filed an action to have the ordinance declared unconstitutional. The court found appellee's criteria for exclusion, specifically, production of more than six 21-gallon containers of garbage a week if it constituted an unreasonable burden on the collector, was reasonably related to the purpose of the ordinance because the criteria allowed appellee to control garbage collection and still exclude garbage generators that would overwhelm the system. The court found that appellee's fees, though high, were not unreasonable because collection for appellant was being subsidized by the other residents in the borough. Finally, the court found that appellant's treatment as one entity for the purposes of bid specifications and billing, and as 174 entities for purposes of the exclusion, was an unreasonable interpretation of the ordinance and remanded to the trial court to determine if collection of appellant's garbage constituted an unreasonable burden to the collector.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the constitutionality of appellee borough's trash collection ordinance because appellee's collection fees were reasonable and the criteria for exclusion were reasonable. The court reversed the finding that appellant apartment complex should be treated as 174 separate entities for exclusion purposes, and remanded for determination of the reasonableness of the burden created by appellee on the trash collector.

Ingram v. Department of Envtl. Resources


Nos. 1075 C.D. 1990, 1091 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


141 Pa. Commw. 324; 595 A.2d 733; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 406; 119 Oil & Gas Rep. 596


April 29, 1991, Argued  
July 19, 1991, Decided  
July 19, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied September 6, 1991, Reported at 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 501. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied May 19, 1992. Cross Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied May 19, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. EHB Docket No. 88-291-F; State Agency Environmental Hearing Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner former and current operators appealed an order of the Environmental Hearing Board (Pennsylvania), which denied the motion to dismiss respondent Department of Environmental Resources' action against petitioner estate and granted summary judgment for respondent against petitioner former operators and petitioner estate, arising from a compliance order issued by respondent to all petitioners.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Department of Environmental Resources issued compliance orders against petitioner former and current operators, including the estate of an operator. The Environmental Hearing Board denied petitioner estate's request to dismiss respondent's action against it, and substituted deceased's personal representatives as parties. The board granted summary judgment for respondent. The court affirmed on appeal. The court held that although petitioner former operators had sold the mine prior to the compliance order, they acknowledged that it was not in compliance with the Clean Streams Law, 35 P.S. § 691.1 et seq., while they operated it. The court held that 35 P.S. § 691.315(a) did not permit estoppel to be raised against the commonwealth, so respondent was authorized to take action when it did. The court held that the trial court's order substituting the personal representatives of deceased for petitioner estate was binding. The court held that deceased allowed discharges from the mine site to enter the tributary, and that it was not necessary to establish a causal link between mining activities and stream pollution for liability to attach under § 691.315(a).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Environmental Hearing Board's summary judgment for respondent Department of Environmental Resources against petitioner former operators, because although petitioner former operators had sold the mine six years before the compliance order, it was not in compliance while they operated it; the court affirmed summary judgment against petitioner estate, because deceased operator had not complied with the Clean Streams Law.

Prudential Property & Casualty Ins. Co. v. Department of Ins.


NO. 2368 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


141 Pa. Commw. 156; 595 A.2d 649; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 713


February 13, 1991, Argued  
July 10, 1991, Decided  
July 10, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEALED From No. R90-07-34. State Agency Department of Insurance


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner insurance companies appealed the order of respondent, Pennsylvania Department of Insurance, which denied petitioner's request for extraordinary circumstances relief pursuant to 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1799.7(b)(3) from the private passenger automobile rate reductions mandated by 1990 Pa. Laws 11, because petitioner claimed an after-tax rate of return of less than 12 percent entitled it to such relief.


OVERVIEW: Respondent, Pennsylvania Department of Insurance, denied petitioner insurance companies extraordinary circumstances relief under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1799.7(b)(3) from the rate reductions mandated by 1990 Pa. Laws 11 and petitioner appealed. Petitioner claimed that it was entitled to relief for an after-tax rate of return of less than 12 percent. On appeal, the court held that there was no basis for finding that the 12 percent threshold set by respondent was a "regulation," in that it was not promulgated through statutory rulemaking procedures or adjudications and did not have the force of law, and found that the commissioner's discretion was not bound by the threshold. There was no abuse of discretion in the commissioner's rate of return determination because it comported with the Casualty & Surety Rate Regulatory Act, 40 P.S. §§ 1181-1199, and was nonconfiscatory under U.S. Const. amends. V,XIV. The court remanded for further proceedings, however, because there was not adequate support in the record for the premium trend used by the commissioner and the commissioner erred in using the figure of 16 percent for petitioner's rate of reduction, when it was actually 16.8 percent.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order of respondent, Pennsylvania Department of Insurance, because the commissioner did not show adequate support in the record for her use of a premium trend and erred in applying a 16 percent rate reduction to the determination of petitioner insurance companies rate of return, when the actual reduction made by petitioners was 16.8 percent. The court remanded for further evidence and recalculation of the rate of return.

Philadelphia v. Duda


No. 962 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


141 Pa. Commw. 88; 595 A.2d 206; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 378


December 6, 1990, Argued  
July 8, 1991, Decided  
July 8, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied October 22, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appealed From No. 449 March Term, 1984; Common Pleas Court of Philadelphia County; Judge Diaz.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged a decision by the Common Pleas Court of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which awarded damages to appellees, injured child and parents, for a swimming pool injury at appellant's facility, and denied appellant's motion for a judgment notwithstanding the verdict.


OVERVIEW: Appellees, injured child and parents, recovered damages for injuries received by appellee injured child when she dove into a outdoor swimming pool at a recreational area operated by appellant city. On appeal, the court affirmed the award of damages and determined that appellant was not entitled to sovereign immunity under 68 P.S. § 471-1, which only applied to unimproved land. The court also determined that the real property exception to government immunity under 42 P.S. § 8546(b)(3), applied because the acts of the appellant made the property unsafe for the intended use of the property and was the supervening cause of the injury. The court determined that jury instructions regarding the standard of care for a young child were appropriate even though they were not the model jury instructions, because they adequately stated the law that applied to the evidence in the case. The court accepted the trial court's determination on juror perjury because appellant had failed to preserve the error by failing to request a transcript of voir dire and by failing to ask questions on the contested issue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the award of damages to appellees, injured child and parents, in their action against the appellant city for damages after a swimming pool accident because the city did not have statutory governmental or recreational immunity because the property was improved upon and appellant had the duty to maintain the improvements and protect users against foreseeable injuries.

Bishop Leonard Regional Catholic School v. Unemployment Compensation Bd. of Review


No. 1282 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


140 Pa. Commw. 428; 593 A.2d 28; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 335


February 13, 1991, Argued  
June 12, 1991, Decided  
June 12, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied November 12, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed from No. B-282301; Unemployment Compensation Board of Review.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, we reverse the decision of the Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employer challenged the decision of respondent Unemployment Compensation Board of Review (Pennsylvania), which granted unemployment compensation benefits to claimant whose employment was terminated by petitioner for claimant's marriage in violation of petitioner's religious policy. Respondent found that claimant's conduct did not arise to the level of willful misconduct.


OVERVIEW: Claimant was employed as a school teacher by petitioner employer, a catholic church. There was no employment contract acknowledging petitioner's rights to terminate the employment for violating a law of the church. Claimant informed petitioner of her intent to marry a non-catholic man, who was previously divorced, and whose previous marriage was not annulled. Petitioner warned claimant that the marriage would violate the church's policy and result in termination. Prior to the marriage, the employment was terminated. Petitioner challenged respondent Unemployment Compensation Board of Review's decision granting compensation benefits to claimant. On appeal, the court reversed and held that claimant's actions constituted willful misconduct because, although there was no employment contract, she was informed and aware that the marriage violated respondent's policy and would result in termination but proceeded with the marriage, and claimant was terminated because of her marriage not because of violating a law of the church. Also, claimant's conduct directly reflected on her ability to perform her duties. Further, denial of benefits was not an excessive entanglement of religion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed respondent Unemployment Compensation Board of Review's award of compensation benefits to claimant because her going forward with the marriage after being informed that the marriage violated petitioner employer's policy and would result in termination of her employment constituted willful conduct. The denial of claimant's request for unemployment compensation benefits was not an unconstitutional establishment of religion.

Southeastern Pennsylvania Transp. Authority v. Pennsylvania Public Utility Com.


Nos. 1708 C.D. 1990, 1721 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


140 Pa. Commw. 270; 592 A.2d 797; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 318

June 3, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied July 13, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appealed From No. I-870064; State Agency Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated appeal, petitioners, transportation authority and city, appealed from an order of respondent Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission, which found that petitioners had certain maintenance responsibilities for a bridge that was part of a separated rail-highway crossing.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, transportation authority and city, appealed respondent public utility commission's order requiring petitioners to maintain the bridge. An investigation by respondent revealed that no one was maintaining the bridge and commenced an investigation to determine ownership and to appoint maintenance responsibilities. After hearings to determine final maintenance responsibilities, an administrative law judge (ALJ) determined that petitioners were the owners of the bridge and assigned permanent maintenance responsibilities to petitioners. Respondent adopted the ALJ's decision with minor technical modifications. On appeal, the court found that petitioners were not exempt under 45 U.S.C.S. § 581(c)(5) from the imposition of maintenance responsibilities for the substructure and superstructure of the bridge. The court found that petitioners were not exempt from the maintenance responsibilities imposed by respondent under 55 P.S. § 600.342. The court concluded that because the chain of title to the bridge was unbroken, petitioners were the owner and were therefore responsible for primary maintenance. The court affirmed but remanded for modification of the division of ownership.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision entered by respondent public utility commission because the chain of title to the bridge was unbroken, petitioners, transportation authority and city, were the owners and were therefore responsible for primary maintenance. The court remanded for modification regarding a certain portion of the bridge to which the petitioner city had been assigned responsibility.

Department of Environmental Resources v. Rushton Mining Co.


No. 729 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


139 Pa. Commw. 648; 591 A.2d 1168; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 543


December 3, 1990, Argued  
May 10, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied July 15, 1991. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied November 25, 1991.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the Department of Environmental Resources, sought review of a decision by the Environmental Hearing Board which granted respondent coal mining companies' summary judgment motion, declaring several of the standard permit conditions invalid because petitioner failed to promulgate them in accordance with the Commonwealth Documents Law, 45 P. S. §§ 1201, 1202.


OVERVIEW: Respondent coal mining companies applied for permits to operate underground bituminous mines in Pennsylvania. Petitioner, the Department of Environmental Resources (DER), approved the permits, subject to respondent's compliance with standard permit conditions contained therein. Respondents filed separate appeals against DER challenging the additional conditions, and the Environmental Hearing Board (EHB) consolidated and heard the appeals. It determined that DER had not complied with the statutory requirements of promulgating new regulations, and as a result, the regulations were invalid. DER challenged the decision of the EHB, but the court affirmed the decision. Even though DER called their additions "conditions," the court found that they were actually regulations and as such were to be promulgated according to the Commonwealth Documents Law, 45 P. S. §§ 1201, 1202. Because the regulations had the binding force of law, the fact that they could be considered generic did not relieve the necessity to provide the proper notice pursuant to statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the Environmental Hearing Board (EHB), granting partial summary judgment to respondent coal mining companies against petitioner, the Department of Environmental Resources. The court limited its review to constitutional violations, errors of law, or findings without substantial evidence, but found no error because petitioner had failed to properly promulgate the regulations.

In re Petition of Clairton for Court Approval etc.


No. 718 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


139 Pa. Commw. 354; 590 A.2d 838; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 224


February 12, 1991, Argued  
April 29, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from No. GD 90-2026; Common Pleas Court of Allegheny County; James H. McLean, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, we affirm the decision of the common pleas court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, union and others, challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) that granted approval for an increased income tax assessed by appellee city.


OVERVIEW: Appellee city sought court approval of a payroll tax increase in excess of the legal limit. Appellee claimed it would run a deficit if the increase were not effected. Appellants, union and others, sought to intervene to challenge the ordinance. The trial court approved the increase, holding that appellants were precluded from challenging the constitutionality of the statute and that the trial court could not inquire into the propriety of the tax increase. Appellants sought review, and the court affirmed. The court held that appellants waived their constitutional arguments because they failed to follow the proper procedure for bringing such claims. The court held that the duty of the trial court was to determine the need for the tax increase, not to examine the budget and the wisdom of the legislative decisions. The court also held that appellee did not abuse its discretion in deciding to implement a tax increase.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's approval of the increased tax, holding that appellee city did not abuse its discretion in determining the tax increase; the trial court was charged only with ruling on the need for the tax increase, not appellee's decisions that created the need; and appellants, union and others, waived their constitutional arguments.

McMenamin v. Tartaglione


No. 693 C.D. 1991


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


139 Pa. Commw. 269; 590 A.2d 802; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 214


April 4, 1991, Argued  
April 23, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed without opinion by McMenamin v. Tartaglione, 527 Pa. 286, 590 A.2d 753, 1991 Pa. LEXIS 122 (1991)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appealed From No. 3713 March Term, 1991; Common Pleas Court of Philadelphia County; Judge Leon Katz.
McNenamin v. Tartaglione, 22 Phila. 133, 1991 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 25 (Pa. C.P., 1991)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant elector sought review of the order from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), dismissing his action in which appellant sought to have intervenor candidate be declared ineligible to seek the office of mayor in the municipal election for alleged violations of the Philadelphia Home Rule Charter and the Pennsylvania Election Code.


OVERVIEW: Intervenor candidate campaigned to run for the office of mayor of Philadelphia. Appellant elector sought to have him declared ineligible for this candidacy, claiming that he violated both the Pennsylvania Election Code and the Philadelphia Home Rule Charter by beginning his campaign while he was still holding the office of district attorney for the city. Though intervenor had begun the planning stages of his campaign during that time, he had not publicly announced his intent to seek the office or attempt to raise campaign funds before he resigned from the district attorney position. The lower court dismissed appellant's petition, and he challenged this decision. At the same time, intervenor filed a motion to quash, claiming improper jurisdiction. The court denied the motion to quash but affirmed the lower court's dismissal of appellant's action in equity. The court found that intervenor had not acted to deceive the voters in any way, nor had he used his prior office to further his campaign. His candidacy activities did not begin until after his resignation, and signatures on his candidacy petition counted even though they might have been collected before his announcement.


OUTCOME: The court denied intervenor candidate's motion to quash because jurisdiction was proper. The court further affirmed the lower court's determination of dismissal because there was no evidence of intervenor's improper behavior during his campaign or prior to announcing his candidacy, as was alleged by appellant elector, and the lower court properly upheld the reporter's privilege in not forcing him to reveal his sources.

Yackobovitz v. SEPTA


Nos. 179 C.D. 1990, 181 C.D. 1990, 456 C.D. 1990, 503 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


139 Pa. Commw. 157; 590 A.2d 40; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 202


September 11, 1990, Argued  
April 15, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected April 25, 1991. Reargument Denied May 23, 1991, Reported at 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 295. Application for Reargument Denied July 8, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. 3169 March Term, 1984; Common Pleas Court of Philadelphia County; Nelson A. Diaz, Jr., Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Consolidated direct appeals were taken from the judgment of the Common Pleas Court of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) that found appellants, Southeastern Pennsylvania Transportation Authority and city, liable in an action for injuries appellee wife sustained when she stepped into a pothole. Appellee, the Pennsylvania Department of Transportation, was absolved of liability but challenged the finding that it was in contempt.


OVERVIEW: Appellees, husband and wife, filed an action for injuries appellee wife sustained when she stepped into a pothole located in a street railway roadbed. Appellants, the Southeastern Pennsylvania Transportation Authority (SEPTA) and city, were found liable. Appellee, the Pennsylvania Department of Transportation (PennDot), was absolved of liability. The court affirmed the judgment as to appellants' liability. The court reversed the finding that PennDot was in contempt. Under a leaseback agreement between SEPTA and appellant city, SEPTA was responsible for leased property maintenance. The court found that pursuant to the agreement "leased property" included the roadbed between and adjacent to the street railway tracks, and that SEPTA was required to indemnify appellant city for the award. When agreeing to submit to "high/low" arbitration, SEPTA, PennDot, and appellant city agreed to pay one-third of the cost of the award, but PennDot refused to pay. Because the trial court failed to hold a hearing to determine whether PennDot believed that the agreement no longer existed, it was improper for the trial court to find PennDot in contempt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that found appellants, the Southeastern Pennsylvania Transportation Authority (SEPTA) and city, liable in an action for the injuries appellee wife sustained when she stepped into a pothole. Under the terms of a leaseback agreement, SEPTA was required to indemnify appellant city. The court reversed the finding that appellee, the Pennsylvania Department of Transportation, was in contempt.

Shah v. State Bd. of Medicine


No. 1195 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


139 Pa. Commw. 94; 589 A.2d 783; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 200


October 29, 1990, Argued  
April 11, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied May 22, 1991. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied October 31, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Petition for Review appealing an Order of the State Board of Medicine.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner physician sought review of an order of respondent, State Board of Medicine (Pennsylvania), which revoked his license to practice medicine and surgery.


OVERVIEW: Respondent, State Board of Medicine, revoked petitioner physician's license to practice medicine. Respondent concluded that petitioner's conduct was unprofessional and immoral as proscribed by 63 P.S. § 422.41(8)(i) and 49 Pa. Code § 16.61(b)(2), and that he had abused a patient in violation of 49 Pa. Code § 16.61(a)(16). Petitioner contended that his due process rights were violated, that respondent lacked impartiality and improperly commingled the adjudicative and prosecutorial functions, and that he was prejudiced by the unjustified delay in bringing the action. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that petitioner's due process rights were not violated because he was afforded a meaningful hearing before his license was revoked permanently. The court held, however, that the facts were sufficient to establish prejudice against petitioner due to the unjustified delay in bringing the action against him because the passage of time had diminished the victim's memory to a point that it had rendered her testimony so fluid that petitioner was severely prejudiced in preparing his defense, and several key witnesses were unavailable to testify.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of respondent, State Board of Medicine, which had revoked petitioner physician's license to practice medicine and surgery. The court held that the facts established prejudice against petitioner due to the unjustified delay in bringing the action against him.

Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Account of John B. McCue - File No. 193-16-2603 / Account of Robert F. Burkardt-File No. 192-18-0695, and Account of William C. Fisher, File No. 191-10-6462 State Employes' Retirement Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner veterans sought review of a decision by respondent State Employee's Retirement Board (Pennsylvania), which denied their requests to purchase retirement credits for active military service.


OVERVIEW: After active service, petitioner veterans were employed by the commonwealth and entered the Pennsylvania State Employee's' Retirement System (retirement system). Before retirement, petitioners sought to purchase retirement credit for military service but their requests were denied, pursuant to 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5304(b). After petitioners retired, a district court ruled that § 5304(b) was unconstitutional because it conflicted with 10 U.S.C.S. § 1336 and petitioners again sought to purchase service credit. The court held that the retirement system did not intentionally or negligently misrepresent the law to petitioners.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision by respondent State Employee's Retirement Board, which denied petitioner veterans' requests to purchase retirement credits for active military service. The court held that the caselaw that determined the unconstitutionality of the pertinent statute, which had prevented petitioner's from purchasing the credits, was not to be applied retroactively.

Stoner v. Merion


No. 1351 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


138 Pa. Commw. 257; 587 A.2d 879; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 114


December 6, 1990, Argued  
February 28, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied February 18, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Common Pleas Court, Montgomery County; No. 89-07613; Joseph A. Smyth, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas, Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which sustained the preliminary objections of appellee township and dismissed appellants' mandamus and damages counts, in appellants action against appellee township. Appellee township's board of commissioners had refused to approve appellants' subdivision request.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landowners applied for subdivision of a residentially zoned parcel of land. Appellee township's board of commissioners rejected the application. The trial court sustained preliminary objections by appellee and dismissed appellants' mandamus and damages counts. On appeal, the court reversed in part, affirmed in part, and remanded, holding that appellee's reasons for rejection were not sustainable as a matter of law. The court held that the Municipalities Planning Code, 53 P.S. §§ 10101-11202, expressly retained the mandamus remedy when entitlement to the issuance of a land use permit was clear. Because appellants had fully complied with zoning requirements, appellee had a nondiscretionary duty to approve their subdivision request. The court held that while mandamus damages were recoverable under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8303, appellants could have recovered only the damages that were incidental to the specific relief sought. Finally, the court held that compensation for temporary loss of the use of land was not recoverable, as there was a specific remedy for damages resulting from the breach of a nondiscretionary duty.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of the mandamus count, because appellant landowners had complied with township code; sustained the preliminary objections to the claim for interference with contract; and remanded for appellee township to grant approval of the preliminary plan and with processing of final subdivision plan when presented. The damage claim was to be confined to any loss of use from the period that appellee withheld approval.

Willits Woods Assoc. v. Zoning Bd. of Adjustment


No. 1202 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


138 Pa. Commw. 62; 587 A.2d 827; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 80


December 7, 1990, Argued  
February 12, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied April 8, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Common Pleas Court, Philadelphia County; No. 299 September Term, 1986; Nicholas D'Alessandro, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant developer challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which rejected appellant's contention that the City of Philadelphia Zoning Code was unconstitutional in that it allegedly excluded mobile home park development.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed an order which rejected appellant developer's contention that the City of Philadelphia Zoning Code was unconstitutional in that it allegedly excluded mobile home park development. The court ruled that the zoning code was not de jure or de facto exclusionary of mobile home parks, as mobile home park development was provided for in seven other residentially zoned districts, but that appellant never sought permits for those allowed residential areas. The court found that the fact that the zoning code did not contain specific provision addressing mobile home park development was not, in and of itself, a basis for finding an unconstitutional exclusion of that use. The court also ruled that appellant did not meet his burden of proving the zoning code was unconstitutional by reason of the alleged de jure exclusion of any reference to a mobile home or trailer park district.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an order which rejected appellant developer's contention that the City of Philadelphia Zoning Code was unconstitutional. The court found that the zoning code was not de jure or de facto exclusionary of mobile home parks, as mobile home park development was provided for in areas other than those in which appellant wished to develop.

 

736 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Curtis v. Cleland


No. 46 T.D. 1988


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


137 Pa. Commw. 537; 586 A.2d 1029; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 79


October 3, 1990, Argued  
February 11, 1991, Decided  
February 11, 1991, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county commissioners filed a motion for a judgment on the pleadings in their action against respondent judge. Petitioners argued that they, not respondent, had control over the disposition of IV-D funds.


OVERVIEW: Respondent judge issued an administrative order directing the county controller to use IV-D funds to pay court employees a one-time salary bonus. Petitioner county commissioners were held in contempt when they refused. Petitioners then filed a petition seeking a declaratory judgment that IV-D funds were under their control. Respondent's preliminary objections were overruled, and petitioners sought a judgment on the pleadings. The court granted petitioners' motion, holding that respondent could seek control over the funds only if petitioners acted arbitrarily. The court found that the issue was justiciable under the Declaratory Judgments Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 7531-7541. The court held that the action was not barred by res judicata or collateral estoppel, as the issue in the previous proceeding regarding contempt was not the same issue in this proceeding. The court found that the judiciary's control of county funds was secondary to that of the legislature or county salary board. As the bonus payments constituted a payment from the county treasury, under 16 P.S. § 1620, petitioners had the right to approve or disapprove the payment.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner county commissioners' motion for a judgment on the pleadings in its action against respondent judge over the control of IV-D funds. The court held that payments from IV-D funds constituted a payment from the county treasury, and thus control over the funds rested with petitioners. The court held that only in the event petitioners acted arbitrarily could respondent have sought control over the funds.

 

737 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Ohio Casualty Ins. Co. v. Insurance Dep't of Commonwealth


No. 1368 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


137 Pa. Commw. 299; 585 A.2d 1160; 1991 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 38


October 31, 1990, Argued  
January 18, 1991, Decided  
January 18, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Pennsylvania Insurance Commissioner; Docket No. R90-05-42.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner insurers appealed from an order of the Pennsylvania Insurance Commissioner, which dismissed without a hearing petitioners' request for review of an extraordinary circumstances filing because petitioners did not first file compliance rate filings under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1799.7 of the Motor Vehicle Financial Responsibility Law.


OVERVIEW: In response to the addition of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1799.7 to the Motor Vehicle Financial Responsibility Law, which required the reduction of insurance rates for certain private passenger motor vehicles, petitioner insurers filed a request for review of extraordinary circumstances under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1799.7(b)(3). Respondent insurance commissioner denied the filing because petitioners did not first make compliance rate filings under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1799.7(b)(1) and (b)(2). Petitioners challenged the order. The court affirmed the order of respondent. The court held that according to the language of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. 1799.7(b)(3), insurers aggrieved by the rate reductions might seek relief in extraordinary circumstances. Thus, the court concluded that before relief could be sought, the insurer must make rate filings under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1799.7(b)(1) and (b)(2). Therefore, petitioners were not entitled to relief under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. 1799.7(b)(3) because they did not first make compliance rate filings under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1799.7(b)(1) and (b)(2).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of respondent insurance commissioner, which dismissed petitioners' request for review of an extraordinary circumstances filing. . The court held that compliance rate filings were mandatory prerequisites to a request for review of extraordinary circumstances.

Cons. Stat. § 1797, the Pennsylvania Motor Vehicle Financial Responsibility Law, requesting declaratory an injunctive relief. Respondent insurance commissioner filed preliminary injunctions.

Reeves v. Pennsylvania Game Com.


No. 230 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


136 Pa. Commw. 667; 584 A.2d 1062; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 681


September 10, 1990, Argued  
December 18, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Docket No. P.H. 89-101; Pennsylvania Game Commission.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed a decision from respondent Pennsylvania Game Commission, which found that he had forfeited his vehicle when he pled guilty to assisting in the killing of a deer by using an artificial light in violation of the Game and Wildlife Code, 34 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2310(a)(2).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner pleaded guilty to spotlighting deer. When respondent Pennsylvania Game Commission failed to return petitioner's vehicle, he made inquiry and was told he had forfeited the vehicle by signing the receipt acknowledging guilt. Petitioner sought judicial review. He argued he was denied procedural due process. Under 34 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2310(d) and § 102, respondent was vested with the authority to enforce the forfeiture of the vehicle used in violation of the law. The court ruled that the procedure was in rem and required an adjudication, preceded by notice and an opportunity to be heard. In this case, a forfeiture hearing was never conducted and the mitigation hearing which was held did not comply with due process. The court ruled the statute was defective because it failed to provide for due process and only the legislature had the power to amend the statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision which found that petitioner had forfeited his vehicle due to its use in spotlighting deer, because the statute failed to afford him procedural due process and the Game and Wildlife Code failed to provide for in rem forfeiture proceedings, thereby making the forfeiture unconstitutional.

Pennsylvania Chiropractic Federation v. Foster


No. 148 Misc. Docket 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


136 Pa. Commw. 465; 583 A.2d 844; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 657


September 12, 1990, Argued  
December 5, 1990, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, chiropractic board, chiropractors, and patient, filed a complaint in equity for declaratory and injunctive relief against respondent, insurance commissioner, challenging 1990 Pa. Laws 11 and its amendment of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1797.


OVERVIEW: Respondent, insurance commissioner, filed preliminary objections to the petition for review of petitioners, chiropractic board, chiropractors, and patient, who sought declaratory and injunctive relief from the amendment of § 1797 of the Motor Vehicle Financial Responsibility Law, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1797 by 1990 Pa. Laws 11. The court sustained preliminary objections one and eight, sustained in part and overruled in part objection four, and overruled objections two, three, five, six, seven, and nine. The court held that the amendments were all related to the single purpose of restructuring the regulation of motor vehicle insurance in the state and reducing the cost of insurance to policy holders and thus petitioners failed to state a violation of Pa. Const. art. III, § 3. The court held that respondent was a state actor subject to due process and equal protection constraints. The court held that 1990 Pa. Laws 11 would have some impact on existing doctor/patient relationships, which was a protected interest. The court held that there was an actual controversy between the parties. The court held that there were no indispensable parties not joined in the action.


OUTCOME: Order entered which sustained respondent, insurance commissioner's, preliminary objections one and eight, sustained in part and overruled in part objection four, and overruled objections two, three, five, six, seven, and nine because all amendments were related to a single purpose and thus proper, respondent was a state actor, and a patient/doctor relationship was a protected interest which would be affected by the legislation.

Nationwide Mut. Ins. Co. v. Insurance Dep't of Commonwealth


No. 321 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


136 Pa. Commw. 435; 583 A.2d 507; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 653


September 12, 1990, Argued  
December 3, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected December 18, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Filing I.D. No. 5366, Docket No. R90-01-07; Insurance Department of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: We reverse the order of the department and hold that Nationwide's rate filing of November 12, 1989 is deemed approved and in effect as of May 1, 1990.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Insurance Department of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania moved to dismiss the petition filed by insurer to review an action by respondent which deemed a request for rate increases withdrawn.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner insurer submitted a request for a rate increase. Petitioner received a letter requesting more information. Thirty days thereafter, insurer sent a partial response. Respondent notified petitioner that its rate filing was deemed withdrawn by operation of law. The next day insurer petitioned for judicial review and filed a second request for a rate increase. Respondent moved to dismiss because insurer had not appealed the deemed withdrawal to the insurance commission. The court ruled the deemed withdrawal was a final adjudication and that it had jurisdiction. It also ruled the second filing did not render the question moot because the effective dates were different. In considering the merits, the court found that the letter requesting information could not trigger the deemed withdrawal pursuant to 31 Pa. Code § 129.12 because the letter failed to state it was a formal request for information under 31 Pa. Code § 129.11 and that a reply was required within 30 days. Because respondent improperly deemed the rate filing withdrawn or to take timely action, the rate increase was deemed approved.


OUTCOME: The court denied respondent Insurance Department's motion to dismiss insurer's petition because it found respondent's decision to deem petitioner's request for a rate increase deemed withdrawn was a final adjudication. Since respondent failed to formally request information, it could not deem withdrawal of petitioner's request and the court ruled that the request was deemed approved.

Finucane v. Pennsylvania Milk Marketing Bd.


No. 427 C. D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


136 Pa. Commw. 272; 582 A.2d 1152; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 626


September 11, 1990, Argued  
November 27, 1990, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the Preliminary Objections of the Board are sustained.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Pennsylvania Milk Marketing Board (Board) filed preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer to the petition for review brought by petitioner consumers which challenged the constitutionality of the retail minimum price requirements of the Milk Marketing Law, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 31, §§ 700j-802(4).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner consumers filed an action seeking a declaratory judgment regarding the constitutionality of the retail minimum price requirements of the Milk Marketing Law (Law), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 31, § 700j-802(4). Petitioner consumers contended that retail minimum price controls of milk were an unconstitutional violation of their substantive due process rights because such price controls were an unreasonable exercise of police power, unduly oppressive, and without a real and substantial relation to the objectives of the law. The Pennsylvania Milk Marketing Board (Board) filed preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer. The court sustained the Board's demurrer. The court determined the law's legislative purpose was for the protection of the public health and welfare and for the prevention of fraud. The court ruled that retail minimum price controls were a rational means to be employed by the legislature to achieve the determined objections. Thus, the retail minimum price control scheme could not be said to be arbitrary, discriminatory, or irrelevant to the legislative policy. The law was rationally related to the objectives of the law and passed constitutional muster.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the Pennsylvania Milk Marketing Board's preliminary objections to petitioner consumers' suit challenging the constitutionality of the retail minimum milk price because the retail minimum price control scheme provided by statute was constitutional.

Nationwide Mut. Ins. Co. v. Insurance Dep't of Commonwealth


No. 51 Misc. Docket 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


135 Pa. Commw. 652; 582 A.2d 93; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 592


June 13, 1990, Argued  
October 31, 1990, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents Pennsylvania Department of Insurance and commissioner filed preliminary objections, based on lack of jurisdiction and mootness, to an original action filed by petitioner insurance carriers. Petitioners sought declaratory relief against respondents' action deeming petitioners' first rate change requests withdrawn.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner insurance carriers submitted a rate change request to respondent Pennsylvania Department of Insurance. Respondent department deemed the request withdrawn after petitioners failed to provide requested information within the time limit. Petitioners filed an action against respondents department and commissioner seeking a declaration that their first rate filing was effective. Respondents filed preliminary objections on the basis of jurisdiction and mootness. The court granted respondents' preliminary objections because it did not have original jurisdiction over agency determinations. Respondents' actions canceling the hearing on the disputed deemed-withdrawal issue and closing the docket constituted a final appealable order. An appeal was filed, and a determination on the merits was pending. The court had no authority to grant equitable relief where there was an adequate statutory remedy. However, the court found respondents' mootness argument without merit because 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1799.7(d), prohibiting rate requests filed prior to July 1, 1990, was unconstitutional insofar as it purported to roll back rates approved before the date of enactment.


OUTCOME: The court granted respondents Pennsylvania Department of Insurance and commissioner's preliminary objections to petitioner insurance carriers' original action seeking declaratory relief because the court did not have original jurisdiction over agency determinations, and a determination on the merits was already pending on appeal. The issue of whether the rate request was properly withdrawn was not moot.

Mihok v. Department of Public Welfare


No. 19 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


135 Pa. Commw. 265; 580 A.2d 905; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 535

September 25, 1990, Decided

OVERVIEW: Petitioner claimant was employed by a state mental hospital when she injured her foot during the course of her employment. Petitioner was placed on disability leave until she returned to work. Petitioner was placed on light duty but then called in sick for an indefinite period of time because of her disability. The hospital determined that petitioner recovered from her work-related injury; therefore, her benefits under Act 534, 61 P.S. § 951 were terminated. Petitioner sought review of the decision, and the department of public welfare denied her appeal. The court held that her disability benefits would be restored from the date of termination to the date of the hearing. The court affirmed the remainder of the judgment because there was sufficient evidence that petitioner no longer suffered a work-related injury.

The court ordered benefits restored from the date of termination to the date of the hearing because there was sufficient evidence that petitioner no longer suffered from a work-related injury.

 

747 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Harristown Dev. Corp. v. Commonwealth, Dep't of General Services


No. 5 Misc. Docket 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


135 Pa. Commw. 177; 580 A.2d 1174; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 509


November 15, 1989, Argued  
September 14, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Vacated by Order of the Court September 21, 1992.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner non-profit corporation filed a motion for summary judgment seeking declaratory and injunctive relief to prevent respondents, state and senator, from applying 71 P.S. § 632(d) to it, thus allowing respondent state to consider petitioner an agency, which would subject it to the provisions of the Sunshine Act and Right-to-Know Law.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner non-profit corporation was established to combat urban blight. The legislature's later amendments to 71 P.S. § 632(d) required that all non-profit corporations who leased space to respondent state for a certain rental amount or higher be considered agencies, thus making them subject to the provisions of both the Sunshine Act and the Right-to-Know Law, which would require that such corporations open its books and financial records to the public. Petitioner sought declaratory and injunctive relief to prevent respondents from applying § 632(d) to it. The court held that petitioner was not an agency pursuant to the Sunshine Act, 65 P.S. § 273, where it was not a body authorized to take official action, nor was it an agency under the Right-to-Know Law, 65 P.S. § 66.1, where it exercised no governmental authority and took no official action; therefore, the legislature could not transform petitioner into an agency through legislation. Finally, the court held that the amendments to § 632(d) were unconstitutional where petitioner was singled out for regulation. As such, the court granted petitioner's motion for summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner non-profit corporation's motion for summary judgment seeking declaratory and injunctive relief, holding that where petitioner was not an agency pursuant to the Sunshine Act or Right-to-Know Law, the legislature could not transform petitioner into an agency through legislation.

National Solid Wastes Management Ass'n v. Casey


No. 5 Misc. Docket 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


135 Pa. Commw. 134; 580 A.2d 893; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 498


May 2, 1990, Argued  
September 11, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied October 11, 1990.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Governor of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania and Pennsylvania Department of Environmental Resources filed preliminary objections, including a demurrer, to petitioner association's action for review of an executive order. Respondents argued that petitioner did not effect proper service of its petition, it did not exhaust its administrative remedies, it had no standing, and it had stated no cause of action.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Governor of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania and Pennsylvania Department of Environmental Resources filed preliminary objections, including a demurrer, to petitioner association's action for review of an executive order. Although petitioner at first did not comply with Pa. R. App. P. 1514(c), it cured an amendable defect with proper service by certified mail. Petitioner did not have to exhaust administrative remedies. The executive order could properly be challenged under the Declaratory Judgment Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 7531-7541, because petitioner presented issues premised on a constitutional violation and violations of the legislated regulatory scheme. Because petitioner had pled not only a discernibly adverse effect upon its members, but had also pled the immediacy of the order's effect, it had standing to bring the declaratory judgment action. However, neither petitioner nor its members had standing to seek mandamus. Concerning the declaratory judgment count, petitioner pled sufficient facts to withstand the demurrer, but on a separation of powers count, the court granted the demurrer. The court overruled the preliminary objections in part and sustained in part.


OUTCOME: The court overruled the preliminary objections in part and sustained them in part. Petitioner association belatedly made proper service by certified mail. Petitioner did not have to exhaust administrative remedies. The immediate adverse effect of the order provided standing to bring the declaratory judgment action, but not for mandamus. Petitioner pled sufficient facts to withstand a demurrer on one count, but it had not done so on another count.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant motorist challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the action of the Pennsylvania Department of Transportation suspending appellant's operating privileges under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1538(c).


OVERVIEW: Appellant motorist had his operating privileges suspended for 30 days due to his accumulation of 8 points under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1538(c). 750 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Nationwide Mut. Ins. Co. v. Foster


Nos. 1144 C.D. 1989, 1676 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


134 Pa. Commw. 585; 580 A.2d 436; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 485


April 6, 1990, Submitted on Briefs  
September 5, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Nos. PI88-10-2 and PI89-04-01; Commissioner of Insurance, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons set forth above, the orders of the Commissioner in these consolidated cases are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner insurer challenged the order of the Commissioner of Insurance, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, that concluded that it had violated 40 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1008.3(b) in cancelling the automobile insurance policies of respondent insureds based upon the involvement of each in two accidents during the three-year period preceding the renewal date and ordering that the policies be renewed.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner insurer refused to renew automobile insurance policies of respondent insureds after each had been involved in two automobile accidents within a three-year period prior to the renewal date. Respondents requested a review by the state insurance department, which concluded that petitioner had violated 40 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1008.3(b), which precluded cancellation based upon one accident within the three-year period preceding the renewal date, and ordered policy reinstatement. Petitioner sought judicial review, arguing that the administrative agency erred in interpreting the work "accident" to mean only those incidents where the insurer was required to pay a claim. In affirming the administrative ruling, the court concluded that the statutory construction was not clearly erroneous but was reasonable. Cancellation or refusal to renew was statutorily precluded where the incidents were not the fault of the insured or the insurer was not required to make any payment. Where only one of the two incidents for each policyholder was one wherein petitioner was required to make payment or where the insured was liable, petitioner's refusal to renew the policy was improper.


OUTCOME: The order of the insurance commissioner finding that petitioner insurer had improperly refused to renew the automobile insurance policies of respondent insureds and ordering the policies to be renewed was affirmed, as petitioner's cancellation of the policies on the basis of incidents where the respondents were not at fault and where petitioner was not required to pay a claim was improper.

Scurfield Coal, Inc. v. Commonwealth


No. 102 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


136 Pa. Commw. 1; 582 A.2d 694; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 606


June 11, 1990, Argued  
August 16, 1990, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Memorandum decision redesignated as opinion November 13, 1990. Ordered published November 13, 1990.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Designated as Opinion to be Reported November 13, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Nos. 37, 38 and 320 Criminal 1988, and No. 141 Criminal 1989. Common Pleas Court, Somerset County; Eugene A. Creany, Senior Judge, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For these reasons, the order of the court finding Scurfield guilty of the violations must be affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant manufacturer challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Somerset County (Pennsylvania) that found it guilty of four separate summary offenses in violation of the Air Pollution Control Act and imposed fines.


OVERVIEW: Appellant manufacturer received citations charging it with nuisance and violation of the Air Pollution Control Act in operating its facility. After a de novo trial, appellant was found guilty, and the trial court imposed fines. Appellant challenged the adverse judgment, arguing that the nuisance ordinance was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad and that the issuance of a citation based upon an officer's observation of a single could of dust was insufficient to support a charge of violating air pollution standards. In affirming, the court concluded that the language of the nuisance ordinance clearly set forth what conduct constituted a nuisance and was not so ambiguous that it would cause improper applications thereof. As such, appellant's overbreadth and vagueness challenge failed. Further, a violation of § 123.2 of the Department of Environmental Resources regulations was committed when particle matter was omitted into the outdoor atmosphere that was visible outside of the individual's property. No special equipment was necessary to uphold a conviction, and the observation of a cloud of dust was sufficient to establish a violation of the air pollution regulation.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was affirmed because the nuisance ordinance was not vague and overbroad where it adequately set forth the prohibited conduct. Further, where the air pollution regulations provided for a penalty whenever particle matter was emitted from a facility and was observable to passersby, the single cloud of dust emitted from appellant's facility was sufficient to support a conviction.

Foose v. Commonwealth, State Bd. of Vehicle Mfrs., Dealers & Salespersons


No. 1489 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


135 Pa. Commw. 62; 578 A.2d 1355; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 434


April 6, 1990, Argued  
August 8, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied September 19, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Docket No. 86-60-0075, State Board of Vehicle Manufacturers, Dealers and Salespersons.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner dealership owner challenged an order of respondent, the State Board of Vehicle Manufacturers, Dealers and Salespersons (Pennsylvania), which suspended his vehicle dealership license pursuant to § 10(4) of the Board of Vehicles Act, 63 P.S. § 818.10(4).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner operated a licensed vehicle dealership. Although convicted of conspiracy to distribute cocaine and possession with intent to distribute cocaine in violation of federal law, petitioner continued to operate his dealership. Petitioner's application for a renewal of his license was denied after respondent administrative agency concluded that his convictions for drug offenses constituted crimes involving moral turpitude and provided a basis for license revocation under § 104 of the Board of Vehicles Act, 63 P.S. § 818.10(4). Petitioner sought judicial review. In affirming respondent's denial of the license renewal, the court concluded that respondent's interpretation of the term "moral turpitude" was not improper. Moral turpitude had been deemed anything contrary to honesty or good morals, and the organizing and selling of a controlled substance was an offense against public morals. Respondent was charged with regulating a business that affected the public welfare, and petitioner's past conduct was relevant to the issue of whether he possessed the integrity and good moral character necessary for the issuance of a license.


OUTCOME: The decision of respondent state agency denying petitioner vehicle dealership licensee's application for a license renewal was affirmed where petitioner's conviction of drug offenses constituted crimes involving moral turpitude, and respondent was permitted to revoke a license where the licensee demonstrated a lack of integrity and good moral character.

 

753 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Teal v. Haverford


No. 1914 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


134 Pa. Commw. 157; 578 A.2d 80; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 398


March 5, 1990, Argued  
July 23, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal Denied April 30, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from No. 87-343, Common Pleas Court, Delaware County; John V. Diggins, Senior Judge, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant township resident sought review of an order of the Common Pleas Court of Delaware County (Pennsylvania) that sentenced appellant to fines and costs for violating a township ordinance prohibiting the storing of disabled motor vehicles upon any private property located within appellee township.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found appellant township resident had violated a township ordinance prohibiting the storing of disabled motor vehicles upon any private property located within appellee township and sentenced appellant to fines and costs. Appellant sought review and asserted that appellee did not have the authority to declare or define the storage of junked or abandoned automobiles as nuisances per se. The court agreed and held that an ordinance that sought to abate the storage of wrecked, junked, or abandoned vehicles could not declare the mere presence of such vehicles on any given property to be a nuisance per se. The court held that appellee had the authority to prohibit, remove, or cause to be removed disabled vehicles on private property if they were, in fact, nuisances to the public. The court noted that where a reasonable interpretation of an ordinance could be made to save its constitutionality, the court was obliged to adopt it. The court therefore held that the ordinance would be interpreted as requiring appellee to prove a nuisance in fact to sustain a conviction under the ordinance. The court reversed the order imposing judgment of sentence upon appellant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the imposition of judgment of sentence upon appellant township resident and held that the ordinance that appellant allegedly violated would be interpreted as requiring appellee township to prove a nuisance in fact in order to sustain a conviction under the ordinance.

Givnish v. Commonwealth, State Bd. of Funeral Directors


No. 419 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


134 Pa. Commw. 146; 578 A.2d 545; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 393


September 12, 1989 and May 2, 1990, Argued  
July 20, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued, May 2, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Petition for Review; (State Board of Funeral Directors); Order dated Feb. 9, 1989; No. 87-48-03563.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, State Board of Funeral Directors (Pennsylvania) and the petitioner funeral director sought relief from an order of the respondent, which was reversed and had revoked the license of the petitioner to practice as a funeral director and funeral supervisor and imposed a fine upon him, and reconsideration. Reconsideration of the prior decision in this case was also granted.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was a licensed funeral director and sold pre-arranged funeral service agreements. Respondent funeral directors board (board) filed an order to show cause against petitioner alleging his violation of their regulations by using a contract for pre-arranged funeral services which provided that 30 percent of the total purchase price for funeral services selected would be retained by the funeral home for consulting and arranging the funeral plans. Petitioner filed an answer denying the allegations. Respondent board issued an order revoking petitioner's funeral director and funeral supervisor licenses and fined him. Petitioner sought a stay which was granted pending his appeal's outcome. Respondent board filed a motion to strike petitioner's brief. The court concluded that the applicable regulation required that 100 percent of the funds collected by funeral directors for pre-need services and merchandise incidental to such services be placed in trust. The court reversed the revocation of petitioner's funeral director license and reinstated the petitioner's license., but affirmed the fine. Respondent board's motion to strike was granted in part and denied in part.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the respondent board's revocation of petitioner's funeral director license and reinstated the petitioner's license. The court affirmed the imposition of the fine. The court agreed with the petitioner that the penalty imposed initially by the respondent board was excessively harsh. Respondent board's motion to strike was granted in part and denied in part.

Skupien v. Gallitzin


No. 2045 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


134 Pa. Commw. 115; 578 A.2d 577; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 392


June 11, 1990, Submitted on Briefs  
July 19, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from No. 1987-3097; Common Pleas Court, Cambria County; Gerard Long, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners sought review of an order of the Common Pleas Court, Cambria County (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee borough's preliminary objections to appellant's petition for declaratory judgment. Appellants contended that an ordinance that regulated payments for water service within the borough was unconstitutional because it imposed personal liability on landowners for their tenant's water utility payments.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landowners sought review of a court order that granted appellee borough's preliminary objections to appellant's petition for declaratory judgment. Appellants argued that an ordinance enacted by appellee was unconstitutional because it imposed personal liability on landowners for their tenant's water utility payments. The ordinance made an owner leasing property personally liable for water service provided to tenants under a contract for service between the tenant and the borough water system. The court affirmed and held that under 53 P.S. § 7107, municipalities had the power to enter liens against property for which water service had been rendered; a lien against property was an action in rem and did not authorize direct personal liability. The court held that the borough had the power to regulate the collection of unpaid water bills under 53 P.S. § 46202. Therefore, the court held that the ordinance did no more than the state law allowed. The court held that the ordinance was constitutional because the legislation had a substantial relationship to the asserted governmental interest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed and held that the ordinance was constitutional and within appellee borough's power to regulate collection of unpaid water bills under state law.
