Lutz v. Commonwealth


No. 1839 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


133 Pa. Commw. 673; 577 A.2d 957; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 374


February 6, 1990, Argued  
July 12, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from No. 1222 C 1982; Westmoreland Joseph A. Hudock, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the common pleas court order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order of a Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania) Common Pleas Court order dismissing post-verdict motions on his criminal conviction for violating provisions of the Solid Waste Management Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 35, §§ 6018.101 -- 6018.1003, and Crimes Code, Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 101 -- 9183. Appellant had been cited for violating a law against dumping solid waste without a permit.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged a lower court order that dismissed post-verdict motions on his criminal conviction for violating provisions of the Solid Waste Management Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 35, §§ 6018.101 -- 6018.1003, and Crimes Code, Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 101 -- 9183. Appellant had been cited for violating a law against dumping solid waste without a permit on his farm and obstructing environmental agents. State environmental agents made an unannounced, warrantless inspection of appellant's farm, took a soil sample, and had a camera. Appellant forcibly removed the agents' camera and soil sample when he ordered them from the property. Appellant filed a pretrial motion to dismiss the criminal charges contending the warrantless entry violated U.S. Const. amend. IV. The criminal charges were dismissed, then reinstated in the lower court by application of United States Supreme Court precedent allowing use of the open fields doctrine. Appellant was convicted, then filed post-conviction motions contending the warrantless entry violated Pa. Const. art. I, § 8. The trial court found appellant waived this argument by not raising it in a pre-trial motion. Appellant sought review. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court dismissing the appellant's post-verdict motions. The court found that the evidence supported the finding that appellant's seizure of the state environmental regulation agents' camera and bottles occurred before he as ordered the agents off the property. Thus, the court did not err in dismissing post-trial motions and upholding his conviction.

State Farm Mut. Auto. Ins. Co. v. Insurance Dep't


No. 652 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


133 Pa. Commw. 644; 577 A.2d 951; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 361


June 13, 1990, Argued  
July 10, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Letter dated March 6, 1990, of the Insurance Commissioner, Insurance Department of Pennsylvania.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner insurer appealed from a letter of the respondent, Pennsylvania Insurance Department denying them a hearing on the mandatory rollback of its rates pursuant to amendments to the Motor Vehicle Financial Responsibility Law, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 1701-1798.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner challenged the rollback provision only as it applies to a specific window. Petitioner had requested that respondent state insurance department grant it a hearing as to this rollback provision. Respondent denied a hearing. Petitioner appealed. The court found that petitioner had an interest, via its contract in the approved rate filing, and in receiving a fair return from policyholders for the coverage it provides them. The court noted that there was nothing wrong with petitioner's profit-making goal provided it did not achieve its ends by charging rates which would be excessive under the Rate Act (Act 6) , 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 1701-1798. The court defined the government's interest was to curb escalating insurance costs. The court balanced the competing interests and looked at the procedural safeguards. The court affirmed the letter of the respondent denying a hearing to petitioner inasmuch as she lacked authority to conduct a hearing. The court concluded, however, that petitioner insurer was entitled to relief. The court also found the amendments to Act 6 were unconstitutional on its face insofar as it purported to roll back rates approved before Act 6 was effective.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the letter of the respondent insurance commissioner denying a hearing to petitioner insurer inasmuch as she lacked authority to conduct a hearing. The court also found the amendments to Act were unconstitutional on its face insofar as it purports to roll back rates which were approved before Act 6 became effective, and that petitioner insurer had a contract interest in receiving a fair return from policyholders.

Thomas v. Unemployment Compensation Bd. of Review


No. 209 C.D. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


133 Pa. Commw. 623; 577 A.2d 940; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 360


June 14, 1990, Submitted on Briefs  
July 5, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Quash Granted March 28, 1991, Reported at 1991 Pa. LEXIS 88.


PRIOR HISTORY: Petition for Review; No. B-27793; Unemployment Compensation.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant filed a petition for review from Unemployment Compensation Board of Review (Pennsylvania) alleging the Unemployment Compensation Law was unconstitutional under the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. Claimant was a parochial school teacher who had been denied unemployment compensation. School teachers that worked for public or private entities were able to receive unemployment compensation benefits.


OVERVIEW: Claimant was employed as a teacher at an elementary school which was owned and operated by a parish. Claimant filed for unemployment compensation benefits, but because he was employed by a religious organization, both the referee and the respondent, unemployment compensation review board (board), found that he was not entitled to benefits pursuant to Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 43, § 753. Claimant sought review alleging that the Unemployment Compensation Law was unconstitutional under the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court applied United States Supreme Court precedent that unless a classification was inherently suspect, such as those based on race, color, creed or national origin, a classification's validity was tested by assessing whether the classification has some reasonable basis. In this case, the reasonable basis was to ensure that the free exercise clause was not frustrated as United States Supreme Court precedent had found that Congress had not intended for parochial schools to pay unemployment taxes. The court found no violation of the claimant's equal protection rights and affirmed the decision of the respondent board.


OUTCOME: Claimant's equal protection rights have not been violated and the decision of the Unemployment Compensation Board of Review was affirmed.

Antonucci v. Workmen's Compensation Appeals Bd. (U.S. Steel Corp.)


No. 1560 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


133 Pa. Commw. 273; 576 A.2d 401; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 304


February 7, 1990, Argued  
May 29, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal Denied November 11, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from No. A-95788 Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner claimant sought review of a decision from respondent Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an order denying her death benefit claim under 77 P. S. § 411(1). Petitioner contended that the statutory time limitation that precluded appellant's claim violated Pa. Const. art. III, § 18, and the due process and equal protection provisions of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Pa. Const. art. I.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner claimant filed a fatal claim petition that resulted from the death of her husband, which the parties stipulated was caused by a death related injury. Respondent workmen's compensation appeal board affirmed an order that denied the claim based upon the time limitation imposed by 77 P. S. § 411(1), which required that the death had to occur within 300 weeks after the injury. Petitioner sought review, contending the statute was in violation of Pa. Const. art. III, § 18, and the due process and equal protection provisions of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Pa. Const. art. I. The court affirmed, finding that Pa. Const. art. III, § 18, permitted such time limitations, provided that they applied equally to corporations and individuals. The court determined that 77 P. S. § 411(1) was neither arbitrary nor unreasonable and bore a reasonable relationship to the legitimate state objective of preserving the compensation fund for those most entitled to it by lessening the opportunity for intervening causes to become determinative factors in fatal claim petitions, thus concluding that there was a rational basis for treating work-related injuries differently from occupational ones.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision that denied the death benefit claim of petitioner claimant, finding that the claim was barred by the statutory time period within which such claims had to be filed. The time period was constitutional, because it lessened the opportunity for intervening causes to weaken the causal connection between injury and death and preserved the compensation fund for those most entitled to it.

Muehlieb v. Philadelphia


No. 611 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


133 Pa. Commw. 133; 574 A.2d 1208; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 286


March 5, 1990, Argued  
May 14, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from No. 2640 September Term 1988, Common Pleas Court, Philadelphia County; SAMUEL M. LEHRER, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant dog owner sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which restricted the number of dogs appellant could house at her residential premises, contending that the kennel license issued to her pursuant to the Dog Law, 3 P. S. §§ 459-101-460-1205, preempted application of Philadelphia, Pa., Code § 10-103(8) and that her kennel operation did not constitute a public nuisance.


OVERVIEW: Appellee city brought an equitable action against appellant dog owner to restrain her from maintaining her residential premises in violation of Philadelphia, Pa., Code § 10-103(8), which restricted the number of dogs allowed in such premises, averring that appellant's home constituted a public nuisance because it unreasonably interfered with the rights of her neighbors. Appellant contended that the kennel license issued to her pursuant to the Dog Law, 3 P. S. §§ 459-101-460-1205, preempted enforcement of the local ordinance against her. The trial court granted the relief sought by appellee and appellant sought review. The court affirmed the decision, finding no intention by the state legislature in enacting the Dog Law, 3 P. S. §§ 459-101-460-1205, to make it so pervasive as to preclude respondent from exercising its police powers to limit the number of dogs which could be housed in a residential setting. The court determined that appellant's kennel operation constituted a public nuisance because it operated in violation of Philadelphia, Pa., Code § 10-103(8) and interfered with the rights of adjoining property owners.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that restricted the number of dogs appellant dog owner could house at her residential premises, finding that the kennel license issued to her by the state did not preempt the city from enforcing its animal control law against her that restricted the number of dogs allowed. The court concluded that appellant's kennel operation was a public nuisance because in interfered with the rights of adjoining property owners.

Ketterer v. Commonwealth, DOT


No. 952 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


133 Pa. Commw. 92; 574 A.2d 735; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 281


December 15, 1989, Argued  
May 10, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from No. 4854 January Term, 1984 Common Pleas Court, Philadelphia County; BERNARD J. GOODHEART, Judge
Ketterer v. Commonwealth DOT, 20 Phila. 95, 1989 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 69 (Pa. C.P., 1989)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant motorcyclist sought review of an order from the Common Pleas Court, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), granting summary judgment in favor of appellee, the state department of transportation, in appellant's action seeking damages for injuries he sustained when he lost control of his motorcycle after he encountered potholes on a highway.


OVERVIEW: Appellant motorcyclist lost control of his motorcycle when he encountered potholes on a highway. Appellant filed a complaint against appellee, the state department of transportation, seeking to recover damages for his injuries. Appellee filed a motion for summary judgment on the ground that appellant had failed to provide appellee with actual written notice of the pothole defects prior to the accident as required by 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8522(b)(5). The trial court granted the motion, and appellant sought review, challenging the constitutionality of the notice requirement. The court noted that a statute was cloaked with a strong presumption of constitutionality, and one who attacked it bore the burden of demonstrating that the legislation clearly, palpably, and plainly violated the constitution. Appellant failed to show how the statute violated his constitutional rights. In addition, the statute's notice requirement had a legitimate basis, in that it gave appellee sufficient notice of a condition so that it was able to cure the defect. Accordingly, the decision of the trial court was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court granting appellee state agency's motion for summary judgment in appellant motorcyclist's personal injury action was affirmed, because no fundamental constitutional right of appellant was affected by the statutory requirement that appellee be informed of a defect by actual written notice.

Shiomos v. Commonwealth, State Employes' Retirement Bd.


No. 18 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


132 Pa. Commw. 379; 572 A.2d 1311; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 208


March 7, 1990, Argued  
April 2, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal Granted June 10, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Order dated January 25, 1989, Administrative Office of Pennsylvania Court.



DISPOSITION: Shiomos' actions shows a mutual consent to the application of the Forfeiture Act to his pension benefits. Accordingly, the order of the Board is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a former judge convicted of extortion, appealed an order from respondent, the Pennsylvania State Employees' Retirement Board, forfeiting his retirement benefits. Petitioner contended that, as applied to petitioner, 43 P.S. § 1313 was an impairment of contract in violation of Pa. Const. art. 1, § 17.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, a judge convicted of extortion following his removal from office, appealed an order from respondent, the state employees' retirement board, which forfeited petitioner's retirement benefits pursuant to 43 P.S. § 1313. Petitioner contended that the Forfeiture Act, as applied to him, was an unconstitutional impairment of contract under Pa. Const. art. 1, § 17. The court affirmed the order of forfeiture. The court concluded that petitioner consented to the application of the Act to his pension rights when be began serving his second term of office and each time he accepted an assignment as a senior judge, following his retirement and his election to accept senior status. 43 P.S. § 1313(c) provided that each time a public officer was elected, appointed, promoted, or otherwise changed a job classification, there was a termination and renewal of the contract for purposes of the Act. Moreover, the JARA Continuation Act of 1980, 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5953(a)(2), amended the Retirement Code to provide that all benefits were subject to the provisions of the Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of respondent, the state employees' retirement board, which forfeited the retirement benefits of petitioner, a former judge convicted of extortion. The court concluded that petitioner's actions showed a mutual consent to the application of the forfeiture law to his pension benefits.

Resident Electors of Pennsbury School Bd. v. Pennsbury School Bd.


No. 2406 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


132 Pa. Commw. 362; 572 A.2d 1303; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 197


February 8, 1990, Argued  
March 26, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from No. 89-679-13-6, Common Pleas, Court, Bucks, County; KENNETH G. BIEHN, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, voters, sought review of an order from the Common Pleas Court, Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which compelled reapportionment of the school district and adopted a phased-in implementation plan. Appellants argued that the order violated Section 303 of the School Code, 24 P.S. § 3-303, and the constitutional "one person, one vote" principle.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, voters, filed suit pursuant to Section 303 of the Public School Code of 1949, 24 P.S. § 3-303, to correct an imbalance in population among three regions which comprised the school district. The trial court adopted a reapportionment plan and ordered that it be phased in, beginning with a special election to fill the remaining years of the terms of three incumbents who no longer resided within the district from which they were elected. Appellants sought review and argued that the plan should be implemented immediately, electing an entire new board using the new voting regions at the first opportunity. The court found that the trial court's action struck a balance between two competing provisions of Section 303. Subsection (a) provided for staggered four year terms; subsection (b)(3) provided that three members should be elected every two years for terms of six years. The court held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion by devising an implementation plan that insured that each district would be represented by three resident directors and left intact the statutory scheme for staggering the elections. Therefore, the court affirmed the trial court's order.


OUTCOME: The court held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion by ordering the school district reapportionment plan to take effect beginning with a special election to correct immediately apparent deficiencies, while maintaining staggered elections for the full body of the school board. Accordingly, the court affirmed the trial court's order.

Manross v. Commonwealth, Unemployment Compensation Bd. of Review


No. 1578 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


132 Pa. Commw. 129; 572 A.2d 49; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 188


January 12, 1990, Submitted

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee appealed the decision from the Unemployment Compensation Board of Review (Pennsylvania), which denied him benefits under §§ 3 and 402(e) of the Unemployment Compensation Law, 43 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 752, 802(e). Petitioner argued that respondent Unemployment Compensation Board of Review erred in analyzing the case under § 402, Pa. Cons. Stat. § 802(e), which governed work-related misconduct.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner employee worked as a truck driver. While off-duty, petitioner was arrested and convicted for driving under the influence, and his license was suspended. Because employer had no work for petitioner other than that which required a valid driver's license, employer temporarily suspended petitioner from employment until his license was restored. The lower court denied petitioner benefits under §§ 3 and 402(e) of the Unemployment Compensation Law, 43 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 752, 802(e). Petitioner argued that respondent Unemployment Compensation Board of Review erred in analyzing the case under § 402, Pa. Cons. Stat. § 802(e), which governed work-related misconduct. The court found that § 402(e) of the Unemployment Compensation Law, 43 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 802(e) was a proper basis upon which to affirm the lower court's decision.


OUTCOME: The lower court's decision that denied petitioner employee benefits under the Unemployment Compensation Law was affirmed. The court held respondent Unemployment Compensation Board of Review did not err in applying the relevant statute because petitioner employee's separation from employment was connected to his work because he failed to maintain a valid driver's license.

In re Scranton


Nos. 1962 C.D. 1989, 1999 C.D. 1989, 2000 C.D. 1989, 2060 C.D. 1989, 2061 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


132 Pa. Commw. 175; 572 A.2d 250; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 167


February 6, 1990, Argued  
March 8, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied April 19, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Nos. 89-CIV-94, 89-CIV-95, 89-CIV-583, 89-CIV-1940, 89-CIV-1950, 89-CIV-1951 and 89-CIV-1952, Common Pleas Court, Lackawanna County, Judge CHESTER T. HARHUT.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons set forth in this opinion, the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Lackawanna County in dismissing Condemnees' preliminary objections is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated appeal, condemnees sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Lackawanna County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed preliminary objections filed by condemnees to declarations of taking filed by the Scranton Redevelopment Authority to condemn properties they owned in the City of Scranton.


OVERVIEW: The trial court dismissed preliminary objections filed by condemnees to declarations of taking filed by the Scranton Redevelopment Authority to condemn properties condemnees owned in the City of Scranton. On appeal, the court stated that in eminent domain cases, its scope of review was limited to a determination of whether the trial court committed an abuse of discretion or an error of law. The court held that there was more than substantial evidence in the record that the area was certified as blighted, and that the purpose of the Redevelopment Plan was to eliminate this blight. Further, the court held that condemnees' claim could not be raised in preliminary objections. Preliminary objections filed pursuant to § 406(a) of the Eminent Domain Code, 26 P.S. § 1-406(a), only authorized challenges to the condemnor's power or purpose to take the property which included compliance with statutorily mandated procedures, as well as technical challenges such as service or sufficiency of bond. It did not authorize challenges to the condemning authority's conduct in the settlement offers to other parties. The court affirmed the trial court's order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court, which dismissed preliminary objections filed by condemnees to declarations of taking filed by the Scranton Redevelopment Authority to condemn properties they owned in the City of Scranton. There was substantial evidence in the record that the area was certified as blighted, and the condemnees' claim could not be raised in preliminary objections.

White v. Commonwealth, Medical Professional Liability Catastrophe Loss Fund


No. 2839 C.D. 1987


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


131 Pa. Commw. 567; 571 A.2d 9; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 163


December 13, 1989, Argued  
March 7, 1990, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, Petitioner's motion for summary judgment is denied, and the motions for summary judgment filed by Respondents are granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an original proceeding, petitioner doctor filed a summary judgment motion on his claim that the Health Care Services Malpractice Act, 40 P. S. § 1301.701(e)(1), requiring doctors to pay an annual surcharge in order to practice medicine in the state, was a revenue generating tax beyond the police power of the state. Respondent state agencies responded with a summary judgment motion.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner doctor challenged the Health Care Services Malpractice Act, 40 P. S. § 1301.701(e)(1), which required doctors who had a majority of their practice in Pennsylvania to pay a surcharge into a fund used to reimburse patients for malpractice. Petitioner claimed that the surcharge was a revenue-producing tax measure rather than a license fee or other permissible exercise of the Commonwealth's police power. The court disagreed and granted the motion for summary judgment filed by respondent state agencies. The court found that the surcharge funds were held in trust to compensate victims of medical malpractice and were not revenues used for general public purposes. The surcharge was applicable to doctors with a majority of their practice in the state and was a condition of practice in the state. In addition, the body administering the surcharge regulated petitioner to the extent it required participation in the program to alleviate a shortage of medical malpractice coverage. Finally, the surcharge was used in administering the fund, but also to pay actual claims to patients. Thus, the surcharge met the requirements of a license fee.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion for summary judgment filed by respondent state agencies on petitioner doctor's claim that the Health Care Services Malpractice Act was a revenue generating tax beyond the police power of the state. The surcharge challenged by petitioner was not an impermissible tax, but instead was a license fee imposed upon doctors to provide coverage for patients in the event of medical malpractice.

Pennsylvania State Lodge of Fraternal Order of Police v. Commonwealth


No. 232 Misc. Docket 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


131 Pa. Commw. 611; 571 A.2d 531; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 155


November 15, 1989, Argued  
March 5, 1990, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner police association filed a declaratory action against respondent, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, alleging that various pension laws providing for the establishment and maintenance of pension plans for municipal police officers were in conflict with Pa. Const. art. III, § 31, and Act 111, 43 P.S. §§ 217.1-217.10.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Commonwealth of Pennsylvania passed various statutes providing for the establishment and maintenance of pension plans for municipal police officers. Petitioner police association filed a declaratory action in the court alleging that the statutes conflicted with Pa. Const. art. III, § 31, authorizing collective bargaining between municipalities and police officers, and with Act 111, 43 P.S. §§ 217.1-217.10, implementing Pa. Const. art. III, § 31. Respondent raised preliminary objections, and the court sustained the issue of jurisdiction and dismissed petitioner's complaint. The court provided that standing was required for subject matter jurisdiction. There was no term in any contract between the parties that had not been enforced due to the statutes in question. Without detailing an injury arising from the statutes, petitioner failed to establish standing. Furthermore, declaratory relief was not available unless an actual controversy existed or was imminent or inevitable. Because there was no possibility of any controversy between the parties in the immediate future, the court was not empowered to declare rights.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed petitioner police association's declaratory action against respondent Commonwealth of Pennsylvania because the court lacked subject matter jurisdiction. Petitioner lacked standing because it sustained no injury arising from the statutes in question, and declaratory relief was not available because there was no possibility of controversy between the parties in the immediate future.

Kostyk v. Commonwealth, DOT


No. 2817 C.D. 1988


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


131 Pa. Commw. 455; 570 A.2d 644; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 139


December 13, 1989, Argued  
February 23, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Court of Common Pleas Court, Delaware County; Judge JOSEPH F. BATTLE, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant licensee sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas, Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which upheld appellee department of transportation's suspension of appellant's driver's license pursuant to the Pennsylvania Vehicle Code, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547, for failure to submit to a blood alcohol test following appellant's arrest for driving while under the influence of alcohol.


OVERVIEW: Appellant licensee was arrested for driving while under the influence of alcohol and refused to submit to a blood alcohol test. Pursuant to the Pennsylvania Vehicle Code, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547, appellee department of transportation suspended appellant's driver's license for one year. Appellant challenged the suspension of his license and the trial court affirmed. Appellant then brought an appeal and the court affirmed the suspension. The court held that § 1547 did not violate either U.S. Const. amend. IV or XIV because appellant had no choice of the test that was to have been used and the selection of a test was within the police officer's discretion. The court determined that the state could regulate the use of its highways, and in doing so could require chemical testing once a person was arrested for driving while under the influence and that person had no constitutional right to refuse a test and had no constitutional right to request a specific test. The court also held that appellee did not have to prove that the chemical test required under § 1547 would have been conducted as proscribed in the statute because appellant refused to take the test.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the suspension of appellant licensee's driver's license for failure to submit to a blood alcohol test and held that the Pennsylvania Vehicle Code did not violate the Fourth Amendment or the Fourteenth Amendment, appellant did not have the right to choose the method of testing, and appellee did not have to establish that the test would have been given in the statutorily proscribed manner.

 

770 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Crowell v. Philadelphia


Nos. 2886 C.D. 1988, 3066 C.D. 1988


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


131 Pa. Commw. 418; 570 A.2d 626; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 119


September 15, 1989, Argued  
February 16, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal Granted November 7, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County; Judge PAUL RIBNER, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which denied its motion for post-trial relief and entered judgment against defendants city and driver and in favor of plaintiffs, victims of car accident, in a personal injury case. The trial court found that plaintiffs' claims against defendant city were not barred by 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8541. Plaintiffs cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, victims of car accident, filed a personal injury action after car accident that caused death and serious injuries to plaintiffs. Just before defendant driver reached a turn in the road where the accident occurred, there was a traffic directional sign, which had been erected by defendant city, warning defendant driver that the road ahead curved to the left. In fact, the road curved to the right. Defendant driver followed the traffic directional sign, curved to the left, crossed over the median, and struck plaintiffs' vehicle. In a separate criminal action, defendant driver pleaded guilty to driving under the influence of alcohol. The jury attributed 80 percent of the liability to defendant driver and the remaining 20 percent to defendant city. Thereafter, the trial court molded the jury verdict and denied defendant's city's motion for post-trial relief. The court reversed and held that defendant city was entitled to the immunity granted by 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8541. The court found that the dangerous condition of the traffic directional sign, standing alone, did not cause harm to plaintiffs and that defendant city merely facilitated plaintiffs' injuries.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of defendant city's post-trial motion and found that the personal injury claims brought by plaintiffs, victims of car accident, were barred against defendant city by governmental immunity. The court affirmed the judgment entered against defendant driver. The court dismissed the cross-appeal of plaintiffs.

Suburban Cable TV Co. v. Commonwealth


Nos. 1606 C.D. 1979, 1607 C.D. 1979, 1608 C.D. 1979, 1609 C.D. 1979, 1610 C.D. 1979, 1243 C.D. 1984, 215 C.D. 1985, 216 C.D. 1985, 1510 C.D. 1985, 2757 C.D. 1988


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


131 Pa. Commw. 368; 570 A.2d 601; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 112


September 13, 1989, Argued  
February 14, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Pennsylvania Board of Finance and Revenue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner cable television companies sought review of decisions by the Pennsylvania Board of Finance and Revenue, which denied petitioners' requests for capital stock tax and sales and use tax exemptions.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner cable television companies sought capital stock tax and sales and use tax exemptions from the Pennsylvania Board of Finance and Revenue (Board). The Board denied the exemptions and petitioners sought judicial review. The court affirmed in part, concluding that petitioners were not entitled to manufacturing exemptions applicable to the capital stock tax, 72 P.S. § 7602, or the sales and use tax, 72 P.S. § 7201, because their provision of cable television services did not constitute "manufacturing" within the meaning of those sections. The court reversed in part, determining that petitioners were entitled to "processing" exemptions under §§ 7602, 7201 because they were engaged in the broadcasting of television programs and they qualified as licensed commercial or educational stations. The court also found that the Board erred when it refused to accept a tax exemption petition that was timely filed under 72 P.S. § 7234, but was filed with the wrong administrative tribunal. By analogy to the rule applicable to civil complaints, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5103, the court held that the Board should have treated the petition as if it was correctly filed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part the decisions of the Pennsylvania Board of Finance and Revenue (Board) because petitioner cable television companies were not entitled to manufacturing exemptions applicable to the capital stock tax or the sales and use tax. The court reversed in part because petitioners were entitled to processing exemptions and the Board erred when it refused to accept an exemption petition that was filed with the wrong tribunal.

 

772 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Hammond v. Commonwealth, Unemployment Compensation Bd. of Review


No. 663 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


131 Pa. Commw. 166; 569 A.2d 1013; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 86


December 12, 1989, Argued  
February 6, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Unemployment Compensation Board of Review.



DISPOSITION: For these reasons, we affirm.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed from a decision of respondent Unemployment Compensation Board of Review (Pennsylvania) reversing a referee's decision that petitioner was eligible for benefits because his rationale for voluntarily leaving his employment was of a necessitous and compelling nature.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner moved from Mississippi to Philadelphia to take a lecturer position at the University of Pennsylvania. After considering the possibility of moving to Philadelphia, his wife chose to remain in Mississippi because of the couple's separation and in order to pursue her education. Thereafter, petitioner's wife was offered a position in her hometown in Oregon. She accepted the position and claimant then quit his job in order to move to Oregon with his family. Petitioner sought unemployment benefits and the referee found for petitioner on the basis that he met the burden of proving that his reasons for voluntarily terminating his job were of a necessitous and compelling nature. Respondent Unemployment Compensation Board of Review reversed, finding that the rationale put forth by petitioner was insufficient to satisfy 43 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 802(b). The court affirmed, finding that petitioner's wife's move was for personal reasons, therefore, he was ineligible for benefits.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of respondent Unemployment Compensation Board of Review, reversing the referee and denying petitioner's benefits because, where his wife moved to another state for personal reasons, he could not meet his burden of proving it was necessary that he move due to his wife having been transferred.

 

773 of 892 DOCUMENTS


West Shore Sch. Dist. v. Pennsylvania Labor Relations Bd.


No. 15 Misc. Dkt. 1990


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


131 Pa. Commw. 476; 570 A.2d 1354; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 143


January 23, 1990, Heard  
February 1, 1990, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner school district filed a petition for review for declaratory relief against respondent Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board and teachers' union to enjoin the adjudications of two unfair labor practices filed against petitioner. Petitioner then sought a preliminary injunction contending that respondent Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board no longer existed and was not constitutionally reestablished.


OVERVIEW: Under the Pennsylvania Sunshine Act, 71 P.S. §§ 1795.1-1795.14, petitioner school district had filed a petition for review for declaratory relief against respondent Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board (board) and teachers' union to enjoin the adjudication of two unfair labor practice charges. It then sought a preliminary injunction and claimed that respondent board no longer existed and had not been constitutionally reestablished. Before the sunset provision became effective the legislature extended the respondent board for one year. Before that year was up the state senate and state legislature by resolution reauthorized the agency. The court denied the preliminary injunction. The court found that 71 P.S. § 1795.7(b), which authorized continuance of an agency by resolution approved in the senate and house was unconstitutional. This in effect, along with an earlier decision that the one-year extension was unconstitutional, rendered the entire law unconstitutional because the legislative intent was no longer expressed in the remaining provisions. Thus, the right to relief was no longer clear, and the preliminary injunction was denied.


OUTCOME: The court denied the preliminary injunction holding that under the Sunshine Act the provision that allowed a state senate and house resolution to extend the agency was unconstitutional. The court found that with this provision being unconstitutional, the legislative intent was no longer expressed in the entire act, thus making the Sunshine Act unconstitutional.

The court found that petitioner did not have standing to file the request because its functions were terminated by statute before the petition was filed.

Feingold v. Commonwealth


No. 673 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


130 Pa. Commw. 602; 568 A.2d 1365; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 47


December 14, 1989, Argued  
January 22, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from State Board of Chiropractic.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the order of the Board is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner naturopath challenged an order of respondent, State Board of Chiropractic (Pennsylvania), which fined him for the practice of chiropractic medicine without a license.


OVERVIEW: Respondent, State Board of Chiropractic, fined petitioner naturopath for the practice of chiropractic medicine without a license. The court affirmed, holding that the decision of respondent was in accordance with the weight of the evidence. In so ruling, the court noted that petitioner held neither a chiropractic license nor an unlimited medical license. Although petitioner claimed he was a naturopath, he also stated that there was no real distinction between naturopathy and chiropractic. The court also held that petitioner was not denied equal protection because there was no exemption for naturopaths from the requirement of an unlimited medical license as there was for chiropractors. In so ruling, the court stated that the legislature was not required to enact individual statutes covering every specialty or methodology. Furthermore, nothing prevented petitioner from obtaining an unlimited medical license or a chiropractic license.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of respondent, State Board of Chiropractic, which fined petitioner naturopath for the practice of chiropractic medicine without a license. The court found that there was no real distinction between the practice of naturopathy and chiropractic and nothing prevented petitioner from obtaining either an unlimited medical license or a chiropractic license.

Blackwell v. State Ethics Comm'n

Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


130 Pa. Commw. 646; 569 A.2d 378; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 13

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city council members sought judgment on the pleadings in their action for injunctive relief and declaratory judgment against respondent Pennsylvania State Ethics Commission. Respondent filed a motion for summary judgment.

However, pursuant to the Sunset Act, 71 P.S. § 1795.4(4) respondent was presumptively reestablished by a legislative committee. Thereafter, petitioners filed a motion for judgment on the pleadings, arguing that respondent was precluded from investigating them because the Sunset Act terminated respondent and a postponement of termination under the Sunset Act, § 1795.4(4) was ineffective. Waste Conversion, Inc. v. Commonwealth


No. 647 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


130 Pa. Commw. 443; 568 A.2d 738; 1990 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 14


November 15, 1989, Argued  
January 8, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied July 2, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Common Pleas Court, Lycoming County; Honorable Thomas C. Raup, PRES. JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the trial court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the order of the Common Pleas Court, Lycoming County (Pennsylvania), which denied appellant's post-verdict motions and imposed a fine for appellant's conviction of a violation of the Solid Waste Management Act, 35 P.S. § 6018.610(8)(I). Appellant argued that 35 P.S. § 6018.610(8)(i) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant hired an independent contractor to transport processed waste to disposal facilities. Appellant's employees loaded a truck owned and driven by an independent hauler hired by the independent contractor with an amount of non-hazardous waste that was over the legal limit. In order to avoid detection of the excessive weight, the driver re-the load by raising the bed of the trailer, causing waste to slide out and onto the side of routed to a back road. Unable to negotiate a hill, the driver attempted to redistribute the road. The waste was traced to appellant and appellant was found in violation of the Solid Waste Management Act (Act). The trial court denied appellant's post-trial motions in which it argued that the section of the Act under which appellant was convicted, 35 P.S. § 6018.610(8)(i), was unconstitutional. The court affirmed and held that 35 P.S. § 6018.610(8)(i) was constitutional. Appellant could not have absolved itself of its responsibilities under the Act by transferring the responsibility to independent contractors and claiming that it had no control over them. The court further held that impossibility was not a defense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that denied appellant's post-trial motions and imposing a fine for appellant's conviction for violating a waste management statute. The court found that appellant's failure to maintain control over the independent contractor that hauled the waste was not a defense.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Judicial Conduct Board (Pennsylvania) filed a complaint against respondent judge for violation of the Pennsylvania Rules Governing Standards of Conduct of District Justices.


OVERVIEW: The Pennsylvania Judicial Conduct Board (board) filed a complaint against respondent judge for remarks made by respondent at a continuing education program. The board alleged that the remarks were in violation of the Pennsylvania Rules Governing Standards of Conduct of District Justices. The court found that the board did not establish by clear and convincing evidence that Rules Governing Standards of Conduct of District Justices Rule 1 did not set forth a rule of conduct but rather was a statement of purpose and a rule of construction. In re Cicchetti

COMMONWEALTH OF PENNSYLVANIA, COURT OF JUDICIAL DISCIPLINE


697 A.2d 297; 1997 Pa. Jud. Disc. LEXIS 5



March 31, 1997, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Judicial Conduct Board filed a complaint against respondent retired judge, alleging that he committed sexual misconduct, violated the prohibition against court-appointed employees engaging in partisan political activity with regard to appellee's judicial retention election, and filed false affidavits with two campaign expense reports in violation of the Election Code, 25 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3249.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff Judicial Conduct Board filed a complaint against respondent retired judge for committing sexual misconduct against six women, had court-appointed employees engage in partisan political activity with regard to respondent's judicial retention election, and filed false affidavits with two campaign expense reports in violation of the Election Code, 25 Pa. OUTCOME: The court held that respondent retired judge violated the state code of judicial conduct and therefore was subject to discipline by engaging in sexual misconduct bring disrepute upon the judicial office itself; having court-appointed employees engage in partisan political activity with regard to his judicial retention election; and filing false affidavits in regard to two campaign expense reports in violation of the election code.

Reading Soc'y of Model Eng'rs v. Berks County Bd. of Assessment Appeals


no. 98-8039


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF BERKS COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1999 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 18; 49 Pa. D. & C.4th 291



December 15, 1999, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a private organization, applied for exemption from payment of real property estate taxes. Appellee county board of assessment appeals denied appellant's application, and appellant sought judicial review.


OVERVIEW: A private organization was created to promote an interest in railroading. The organization was chartered under state law as a nonprofit corporation, and it obtained tax exempt status under U.S. I.RC. § 501(a). Members were charged annual dues and the organization opened its property to the public during certain times of the year without charging admission. The court held that (1) the Pennsylvania Institutions of Purely Public Charity Act (IPPCA), 10 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 371 et seq., which provided criteria for determining if an institution was a public or private charity for purposes of exemption from local real estate taxation under the Pennsylvania General County Assessment Law (GCAL), 72 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5020-204(a)(3), did not violate Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 2; (2) appellants met the criteria for qualifying as a purely public charity outlined in IPPCA §§ 375(b), 375(c), 375(f), but not in §§ 375(d), 375(e); and (3) because appellant failed to meet all five of the criteria specified in IPPCA § 375(a), it was not an institution founded, endowed, and maintained by public or private charity, and did not qualify for exemption from local taxation under the GCAL.


OUTCOME: The court denied the private organization's appeal.

PennDOT v. Hosler


no. 1999-C-05464


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF NORTHAMPTON COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1999 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 53; 44 Pa. D. & C.4th 154



December 10, 1999, Decided

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the decision of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Transportation, Bureau of Driver Licensing, to suspend appellant's operating privileges for a period of one-year as a result of his guilty plea to operating a vehicle while under the influence of liquor or drugs in New Jersey in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 39:4-50(a).


OVERVIEW: Appellant, a licensed Pennsylvania driver, operated a vehicle while under the influence of liquor or drugs (DUI) in New Jersey contrary to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 39:4-50(a). The New Jersey Division of Motor Vehicles electronically reported appellant's conviction to respondent, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Transportation, Bureau of Driver Licensing. The report of conviction did not contain the identity of the court, or an identification of the plea, or whether the conviction resulted from a forfeiture of security. Respondent suspended appellant's operating privileges for one-year. Appellant sought review, contending that the compact, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1581, was invalid and the report of conviction was not sufficient to support the suspension under the compact. The court reversed, because the report of conviction was deficient and failed to comply with the reporting requirements of the compact; and the December 21, 1998 amendment to the compact, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1584, was unconstitutional in that it denied due process.


OUTCOME: Suspension of appellant's drivers license was reversed because the report of the conviction failed to comply with the reporting requirements of the compact; and the December 21, 1998 amendment to the compact was unconstitutional in that it denied due process.

PennDOT v. Longstreth


no. 1999-821


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF MERCER COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1999 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 95; 43 Pa. D. & C.4th 213



October 28, 1999, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the appellee Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Transportation, Bureau of Driver Licensing to suspend his driver's license.


OVERVIEW: This matter came before the court on appellant's challenge from the appellee Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Transportation, Bureau of Driver Licensing's (Department) notice of license suspension. The appeal was sustained. The stated authority for the suspension of appellant's driving privileges was the driver's license compact, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1581. The court rejected the Department's claim that 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1584 lessened and/or revoked the requirements for reporting an out-of-state conviction. Since a report of defendant's conviction from Ohio failed to include the requisite information in accordance with the driver's license compact, the Department was without basis to suspend appellant's operating privileges.


OUTCOME: Appeal was sustained. Appellee was without authority, pursuant to interstate driver's license compact, to suspend appellant's driver's license.

PennDOT v. Harrington


no. 99-02476


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF CHESTER COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1999 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 135; 42 Pa. D. & C.4th 153



June 30, 1999, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution appealed the trial court's order that sustained defendant's statutory appeal of the suspension of defendant's driver's license and reinstated defendant's driving privileges pursuant to the Driver's License Compact 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 1581 and 1584.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a resident of Pennsylvania, was arrested in New Jersey and charged with driving under the influence. Subsequently, New Jersey sent notice to Pennsylvania regarding defendant's conviction for driving under the influence. The notice failed to identify the convicting court and failed to indicate whether a plea of guilty or not guilty was entered or the conviction resulted from forfeiture of bail or security. The prosecution then notified defendant that his Pennsylvania driver's license would be suspended for one year pursuant to 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731. The court affirmed its reinstatement decision and struck down an amendment to the Driver's License Compact, 75 Pa. Const. Stat. § 1584, as unconstitutional. The court held that § 1584 diluted the notice requirement of § 1581 to the point where due process was violated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed its order that sustained defendant's statutory appeal of the suspension of defendant's driver's license and reinstated defendant's driving privileges. The court struck down an amendment to the state's Driver's License Compact because it found that the amendment violated due process.

In re Pa. Diet Drugs Litig.


no. 9709-3162


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF PHILADELPHIA COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1999 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 171; 41 Pa. D. & C.4th 78



March 12, 1999, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs moved for class certification in their suit asserting a claim of medical monitoring against defendants, the manufacturers of diet drugs whose use caused heart disease in some users.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs filed suit against defendants, manufacturers of diet drugs, asserting a medical monitoring claim. They moved for class certification on behalf of all state residents who used the drugs. In certifying the class as all residents who had used the drugs but who had not been diagnosed with heart disease, the trial court concluded that the class was sufficiently numerous that joinder of all of its members would be impracticable, that common questions of law and fact existed, that the claims raised were typical of those belonging to absent class members, that plaintiffs and their counsel would adequately protect the interests of the class, and that allowing the suit to proceed as a class action would provide a fair and efficient method of adjudication. In excluding from the class those persons who used the drugs and had been diagnosed with heart disease, the trial court concluded that it was proper for the class to include only asymptomatic persons, as including both symptomatic and asymptomatic persons could affect the ability of symptomatic individuals from seeking compensatory damages for injuries caused by defendants' negligence.


OUTCOME: Plaintiffs' motion for class certification was granted where plaintiffs met the procedural criteria necessary to class certification, but the class was limited to those persons who were asymptomatic, thereby enabling those symptomatic individuals to pursue claims against the defendants for their injuries suffered as a result of product use.

Commonwealth v. Philip Morris, Inc.


April Term 1997, no. 2443


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF PHILADELPHIA COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1999 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 45; 40 Pa. D. & C.4th 225



February 26, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by Commonwealth by Fisher v. Phillip Morris, Inc., 736 A.2d 693, 1999 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 621 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 1999)
Affirmed by Commonwealth by Fisher v. Phillip Morris, Inc., 736 A.2d 705, 1999 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 624 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 1999)
Decision reached on appeal by Commonwealth v. Philip Morris USA, Inc., 2014 Phila. Ct. Com. Pl. LEXIS 221 (2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a group of hospitals and anti-tobacco organizations, moved to intervene in plaintiff state's lawsuit against defendant tobacco manufactures.


OVERVIEW: After the trial court approved consent decrees and final judgments in plaintiff state's suit against defendant tobacco manufacturers, petitioners, a group of hospitals and anti-tobacco organizations, moved to intervene and sought an interpretive opinion regarding whether future claims against the tobacco defendants would be barred or affected by the release provisions in the parties' settlement agreement. In denying the petitions to intervene, the trial court concluded that petitioners sought an interpretive opinion without asserting a claim against the defendants. Because petitioners sought determination of an issue that was not actual, their petition was not ripe, and they failed to present an actual case or controversy. Based upon the long-standing tenet of jurisprudence against providing advisory opinions, the petitions were denied. While the petitioners asserted a right to intervene based upon a legally enforceable interest, the trial court concluded that intervention could not be allowed where the alleged harm was not actual but was, at best, conjectural.


OUTCOME: The petitions to intervene were denied, as the petitioners asserted no claim against the defendants but sought an advisory opinion regarding the effect of the terms of the parties' settlement agreement. Because the petitioners failed to present an actual case or controversy and because there was a long-standing tenet of jurisprudence against providing advisory opinions, intervention could not be permitted.

SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA, DISCIPLINARY BOARD

791 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Morales


no. 1289/97


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF BERKS COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1998 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 10; 40 Pa. D. & C.4th 456



December 9, 1998, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In his concise statement of review issues, defendant challenged the sufficiency of evidence to support his conviction of several sex offenses involving a young teen-ager and the determination that he was a sexual predator. He also argued that the court had provided inadequate reasons for deviating from Pennsylvania's sentencing guidelines.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by a jury of rape, statutory assault, indecent assault, and corruption of minors. He was adjudicated a sexual predator under Megan's Law, Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9794(a), and sentenced to prison terms in the aggravated range. In preparation for his direct appeal, defendant submitted two concise statements to the trial court. The first challenged the constitutionality of Megan's Law. The second discussed sufficiency of the evidence and of the court's articulated reasons for imposing sentences in the aggravated range. The court reviewed its own record, and found plentiful evidence, primarily from the victim, supporting defendant's conviction of sex offenses. It also found that it had provided sufficient reasons for sentencing defendant in the aggravated range. Because the court to which defendant would be appealing had recently found the burden of proof provisions of Megan's Law unconstitutional, however, it suggested that the higher court remand for resentencing.


OUTCOME: The court explained why it believed the evidence was sufficient and that sentencing procedures were proper, but requested that the eventual reviewing court remand for resentencing in light of that higher court's recent constitutionality ruling.

Commonwealth v. Bungarz


no. 3302-97


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF BERKS COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1998 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 87; 41 Pa. D. & C.4th 191



December 7, 1998, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed an appeal following his criminal conviction for rape.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty by a jury of rape, statutory sexual assault, involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, indecent assault, indecent exposure, corruption of minors, and terroristic threats. The victim was a 15-year-old girl. He was ordered to submit to a test to determine whether he was a sexually violent predator under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9794. On appeal, he alleged evidence sought to be admitted by defense was improperly excluded under the Rape Shield Law, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3104(a). The law excluded both opinion and reputation evidence of a rape victim's past sexual conduct. The evidence proffered by defendant concerning the victim's physical attire, the act of the victim in seeking help from the defendant in the park where she was raped, and evidence of her conduct toward other men, was properly excluded under the Rape Shield Law. Defendant was provided with a fair trial and the jury properly found him guilty.


OUTCOME: The appeal was denied.

Muhlenberg College v. Zoning Hearing Bd. of Allentown

COMMON PLEAS COURT OF LEHIGH COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA, CIVIL DIVISION


1998 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 212

794 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Snyder v. Penndot


no. 97-C-2601


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF LEHIGH COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1998 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 195; 38 Pa. D. & C.4th 504



May 20, 1998, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant licensee sought review of the decision of appellee, Pennsylvania Department of Transportation (PennDOT), which suspended his operating privilege under the Driver's License Compact (Compact), 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1581, for a New Jersey conviction for driving under the influence.


OVERVIEW: The licensee was arrested for driving while under the influence in New Jersey, pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 39:4-50(a). Thereafter, the Compact became effective. After the licensee was convicted as charged, PennDOT suspended the licensee's operating privilege. On appeal, the court held that the Compact applied to the licensee, regardless of the date of the New Jersey violation, because his New Jersey conviction occurred after the Compact's effective date. The Compact did not operate retrospectively because some predicate facts existed prior to its effective date. Further, § 39:4-50(a) was substantially similar to Pennsylvania's driving under the influence statute, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731(a). Moreover, although New Jersey's procedures and penalties for driving under the influenced differed from Pennsylvania's, the issue of substantial similarity related to the substantive elements of the New Jersey and Pennsylvania law, and not to the procedures or penalties for such violations. Finally, any arguments based on N.J. Stat. Ann. § 39:4-50(a)(2)(3),(4) were waived. Thus, the licensee's New Jersey conviction was a proper basis for the suspension of his operating privilege by PennDOT.


OUTCOME: The court denied the licensee's appeal from PennDOT's decision. PennDOT was directed to reinstate the suspension of the licensee's operating privilege.

 

795 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Wolff


no. 4297/1997


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF BUCKS COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1998 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 139; 39 Pa. D. & C.4th 480



May 6, 1998, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Subsequent to the entry of defendant's pleas of guilty to two counts of statutory sexual assault, 18 Pa. Const. Stat. § 3122.1, and two counts of involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, 18 Pa. Const. Stat. § 3123(a)(7), and prior to the imposition of sentence, defendant challenged the constitutionality of the designation provision of Megan's Law (Act), 42 Pa. Const. Stat. § 9794, which classified him as a sexually violent predator.


OVERVIEW: The Commonwealth invoked the Act, 42 Pa. Const. Stat. § 9791, for the prescribed mandatory sentence. The court declined to apply the Act. On defendant's challenge, the court held that the procedures provided by 42 Pa. Const. Stat. §§ 9497, 9799.4 violated defendant's rights to due process under the Fourteenth Amendment and Pa. Const. art. I, § 9. 42 Pa. Const. Stat. § 9497 provided the sexually violent predator designation for persons found guilty of certain sexually oriented crimes. 42 Pa. Const. Stat. § 9799.4(a) provided for the minimum sentences for sexually violent predators and § 9799.4(c) stated that a subsequent violent sexual offense committed by a sexually violent predator required the imposition of a mandatory life sentence. The court held that 42 Pa. Const. Stat. § 9497 improperly imposed the burden of clear and convincing evidence on defendant when he challenged the designation. A review of the legislative policy declaration supported the finding that the purpose of the Act was punitive and aimed at retribution.


OUTCOME: The court held that sections of the Act, which imposed the burden of challenging the designation as a sexually violent predator on defendant and imposed a mandatory life sentence on defendant for his repeat sex offenses following the designation, were unconstitutional.

Nolan v. Bd. of Pensions & Ret. of Phila.


No. 9704-0970.


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF PHILADELPHIA COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA, CIVIL DIVISION


35 Phila. 513; 1998 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 66



April 22, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed to the Commonwealth Court at No. 0358 C.D. 1998.
Affirmed without opinion by Nolan v. Board of Pensions & Retirement of Philadelphia, 724 A.2d 1010, 1998 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 931 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 1998)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant pensioner challenged an order of the court affirming the decision by the Board of Pensions and Retirement, which (1) decided to recoup monies received by the pensioner under his Plan "D" pension while he had been working as a city police officer ; and (2) to reduce the service-connected disability pension that the pensioner received under Plan "J" by the amount of the lump sum workers' compensation benefits received.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was employment with the police department until his retirement, at which point he began receiving service pension payments. Thereafter, he received an appointment as an accident investigator with the city. After an accident, the petitioner applied for a service-connected disability. The Board awarded him a service-connected disability pension in Plan "J." Sometime later, the Board discovered that the pensioner had received a commuted award of workers' compensation benefits, which ran afoul of the requirements of Philadelphia, Pa., Municipal Retirement System Ordinance § 206.3. Additionally, he had been receiving a service pension under Plan "D" from his former employment with the city. When the pensioner retired from his position with the district attorney's office, his retirement benefits were required to be suspended if he began to work for the city or any authority established by the city. Because the city qualified as an authority established by the city, the pensioner, it was properly required suspension of his Plan "D" pension during his employment with the city.


OUTCOME: The court denied the appeal from its order affirming the decision by the Board in the pensioner's claim challenging the adverse determination by the Board regarding his retirement benefits.

 

797 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Hurd


no. CC-528-97


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF ADAMS COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1998 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 140; 39 Pa. D. & C.4th 493



April 17, 1998, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, having entered open pleas to statutory aggravated indecent assault, corruption of minors, and furnishing intoxicants to a minor, challenged the constitutionality of Megan's Law, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9791 et seq., and requested modification of the sentence imposed on him.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who was 22 years old, took a 13-year-old female to a motel room and provided her with alcohol. Temporary impotence caused by intoxication thwarted sexual intercourse. Instead, he penetrated her vagina with his finger. Defendant pleaded guilty to aggravated indecent assault, in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3525(8), corruption of a minor, and furnishing intoxicants to a minor. He was notified that the assault charge required an assessment by the state sexual offenders assessment board to determine if he was a sexually violent predator. After defendant was so classified, he filed a motion for extraordinary relief, which challenged the constitutionality of Megan's Law, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9791 et seq., which required the assessment. He also requested modification of his sentence. The court held that the conviction and sentence for aggravated indecent assault under § 9791 as applied to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3525(8) was unconstitutional because it made an impermissible distinction on the basis of digital/penile penetration that defied even rational basis analysis. The court clarified the remaining portions of defendant's sentence.


OUTCOME: The court held that Megan's law violated defendant's constitutional right to equal protection under the law, as applied to defendant's sexual assault conviction. Defendant was sentenced on the assault charge to a term of one to four years, with the state sentence suspended to allow time in a county facility. He was fined on the corruption and alcohol convictions and assessed probation, with the sentences to be served concurrently.

Commonwealth v. Rickabaugh


no. 97 CR 272, 273


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF BLAIR COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1998 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 80; 41 Pa. D. & C.4th 16



January 26, 1998, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After his conviction for numerous sexual offenses against his minor daughters, defendant filed a motion for extraordinary relief that challenged the constitutionality of the "sexually violent predator" provisions of Pennsylvania's Megan's Law, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9791-9799. At issue were the provisions for adjudicating offenders as "sexually violent predators" and the consequences of that classification.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to numerous sexual offenses against his minor daughters, with a reservation of his right to challenge Pennsylvania's Megan's Law, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9791-9799. Defendant claimed that the procedure for the adjudication of a "sexually violent predator," 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9794, were an unconstitutional denial of due process. The court held that: (1) the statute did not make the commission of a specified crime the basis for sentencing, but made one conviction the basis for commencing another proceeding under another Act that involved a new finding of fact that was not an ingredient of the offense charged; (2) the "sexually violent predator" was subject to enhanced punishment; (3) the statute shifted the burden of proof to defendant; and (4) defendant was entitled to a full judicial hearing and all due process protections.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion for extraordinary relief in his motion to challenge the constitutionality of the "sexually violent predator" provisions of Megan's Law.

Commonwealth v. McCafferty


No. 9710-0987, No. 9706-3324, No. 9707-1564, No. 9709-0599, No. 9709-2744, No. 9707-0806, No. 9706-3713, No. 9708-0175, No. 9707-0600, No. 9707-0736, No. 9707-1531, No. 9707-3368, No. 9709-4372, No. 9709-2565, No. 9709-4283, No. 9710-0985, No. 9706-1890, No. 9706-3055, No. 9710-0786, No. 9706-4172, No. 9710-1631, No. 9710-1049


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF PHILADELPHIA COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


34 Phila. 462; 1997 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 92



December 31, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reversed by, Remanded by DOT v. McCafferty, 563 Pa. 146, 758 A.2d 1155, 2000 Pa. LEXIS 2383 (2000)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner drivers sought review of orders from respondent Commonwealth of Pennsylvania's Department of Transportation (DOT), which suspended their Pennsylvania motor vehicle operator's licenses. The drivers contended that the DOT's suspension of operating privileges for one year, following a six-month New Jersey suspension, violated their federal and state constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: As an initial matter, the court held that the holder of a motor vehicle operator's license was not to be deprived of that privilege by arbitrary action of the government. Upon its determination that such an action had occurred, the court granted a reversal of the operating licenses' suspensions. The court found that insofar as the suspensions imposed a punitive sanction substantially greater than that which would have been the case had the suspensions been imposed as concurrent, rather than consecutive to the suspensions of operating privileges in New Jersey or elsewhere, the drivers' constitutional rights had been undermined under the Fourteenth Amendment. Moreover, the provisions of Articles III and IV of the Driver's License Compact of 1961, 75 Pa. Const. Stat. Ann. § 1581 et seq., had been applied improperly by the DOT, to the prejudice of the drivers. The court therefore directed the DOT to modify the term of the each driver's license suspension to total no more than one-year's suspension pursuant to the statute, as taken together with the out-of-state suspension. Such modifications were to be accomplished immediately.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petitions for relief and reversed the suspensions at issue.

 

800 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Ihnat v. Pover


no. GD94-17465


COMMON PLEAS COURT OF ALLEGHENY COUNTY, PENNSYLVANIA


1997 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 111; 35 Pa. D. & C.4th 120



December 16, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Dismissed by, in part, Objection overruled by Ihnat v. Pover, 1999 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 225 (1999)
Decision reached on appeal by, Sub nomine at In re Life Ins. Practices, 913 A.2d 951, 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4751 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff customers and defendants, insurers and agents, sought a determination of the applicability of 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8371, in the customers lawsuits involving alleged improper sales practices of insurance companies.


OVERVIEW: The litigation involved three general categories of claims: vanishing premiums, replacement transactions, and retirement or savings plans. Defendants alleged that not all of the customers were current customers and that some of them lacked standing to sue, but the court held that the ill effects of the alleged improper sales practices had lasting effects and that the customers were entitled to bring suit. A cause of action did not exist an insurance agent under § 8731. The section applied only where a court could find that an insurer acted in bad faith toward the insured. Defendants were entitled to dismissal of any bad faith claims based on transactions that occurred prior to July 1, 1990, the effective date of § 8371. Section 8371 could not be applied to bad faith conduct occurring prior to its effective date. A § 8371 claim could be raised against an insurance company based on an agent's fraudulent misrepresentations that induced existing policyholders to fund new policies with the policy reserves of their existing policies. Section 8371 was not restricted to lawsuits based on an insurer's failure to pay a claim under a policy.


OUTCOME: The court held that claims based on § 8371 could not be raised against the agents, claims based on § 8371 could not be based on any conduct occurring prior to the effective date of the legislation, § 8371 was not limited to actions for an insurer's failure to pay a claim under a policy, and claims against insurers under § 8371 could be based on alleged misrepresentations occurring during the sale and solicitation of an insurance policy.
